The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and verses of sailor-men's
and wharf-men’s life which E. J.'Brady has contributed
to Tiib Bum.KTiN and the Sunday Times, Sydney. It is
the. first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—-the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered * Jones.’” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing ; and the price is 55.,
post-free.

“Dreams in Flower.”
The Bulletin Newspaper Company, Ltd., will
publish, about the middle of this month, a booklet
of verses by Louise Mack, under the abov'e title.
The style will be that of previous Bulletin
booklets, but the size larger, extending to 48
pages, iii artistic cover, enveloped for postage.
This collection includes all the best vei-ses which
the author has written during the last ten years,
with several unpublished pieces.
As only 500 copies are printed for sale, intend-
ing purchasers are recommended to forward
names and addresses to the Bulletin office at
once; and the booklet will then be forwarded
immediately on publication.
The price is 2s. bd., which may be enclosed by
postal note or stamps of any Australian State, or
of Maoriland.
THE RED PAGE.
Glimpses.
IT is nearly four years since I left that home-
stead on the Barrier, and yet the harsh lines
and angles of my impressions have not be-
come so softened as to fit them for artistic
treatment. They are too near, too recent, and it
is in remoteness that the hand of enchantment
weaves its wonder-work. Yet, here and there, an
experience has drawn apart from its fellows,
taking to itself the peculiar memory tinge which
is to mental impressions what the scent of old,
dead roses is to our favourite verse-book.
The country round about the homestead seems
no kindlier now than when I resided there ; time
can never make that country lovely, and yet, in
looking backward, I find in the brown ground and
broken quartz a certain charm. The saltbush and
muiga did not pretend to be what they were not ;
they did not put forth any flowers in the Spring.
Spring and, Summer and Autumn and Winter
were one to them ; and, whether or not the sap
rose in their veins when the winter was over, they
did not hold out any flower signal, blue or
yellow or red. They were honest in their ugli-
ness. Ido not remember to have seen any
rich sunsets: the great artist lacked a canvas
such as the humid coast atmosphere and its cloud-
gatherings provide. But, nevertheless,-the West is
memorable to me .because of something that came
out of it. It was red as flame and curved like
flame, and, though it was but dust scalped from
the plains and lifted into the air, it sent before it
such a thrill of awe as no knowledge of its cause
or composition could resist. There was such
directness in its approach, such deadly steadiness
of motion, that deep called unto deep, and the
soul was troubled by elemental fear. In the
presence of death one might be brave, it is only
the individual life which is imperilled. But
when Nature becomes the aggressor, and the whole
race appears as if on the edge of extinction, the
mind shrinks within the grip of a panic, dark and
mysterious. There is no moment so filled with
terrible sensation as that in which we lose con-
fidence in Nature.
* * * *
The homestead garden was always good to see.
11 was like one of those green kindlinesses done to
the deserts of the old world, only that in place of
wandering Arabs it consoled the eyes of rouse-
abouts and sundowners. After the white quartz
and the'hateful glare of the tracks had strained
ami p lined the visual sense, the green garden
seemed a ccSisoUfion, a blessed thing-. 1 could
imagine a man living on the Barrier, one who had
never wept before, whose heart was a dead thing
with a live grief in it, coming at length to this
green place, aqd emptying his whole sou L. at sight
of it in a while of quiet weeping.
As one came Over the crest of the hill and saw
it in the hollow under, the heart went out of him
and ran before, As he went away again, to face
the mirage and the dust on the lone, hot track to
the Never Never, it was the last picture with
which he filled his mind.
1 have an idea that a landsc ape without green
is like a life without -love, and I have often won-
dered in what way an artist, born in such barren
environment as that 1 speak of, would make his
gift manifest. If he never saw any green in his
life, other than that of the saltbush and the muiga,
what would the loss mean to him ? Could he feel
any of the thousand and one associations that
are linked with the colour—as, for instance,
hope and graves made beautiful—and love ?
Could he ever learn the that follow its
decease, the sisterhood of sad things that come in
the train of Autumn ? All he would have to draw
from would be the fall of the leaf in his blood, the
memories of old-world seasons experienced by his
forbears. ''
Of one thing, however, I am certain, and that is
that the artist of the Barrier would never be re
markable for the quantity of his output. The
workshop is too large, and to get at its best the
mind needs compression, narrow walls that force
it back on itself. In spacious surroundings, wide
plains and seas, thoughts become as birds released :
they fly away into an infinity from which no call
of will can bring them back.
* * *_ * ' '
Nowhere else is night so full of magic. The
stars seem very near and their appeal to the eye
is intimate. In the city a thousand mundane
lights salute one, but on the plains a man ? s first
look is upward, and, if the stars are there he feels
that the world means well with him. They are a
symbol of release, a message that the red day is
dead. They soothe the system with the exquisite
sense of a perilous ordeal passed, and appeal to the
mind in the manner of a kindly convalescence, as
when a fever is over, and body and brain made
free of delirium.
• •, • *-
The violin is said to be the sweetest music-
maker that men know. But when the system
craves for the sound of water, when all the pores
in one’s body yearn for rain, as' happens almost
every month of the year and every year of the-de-
cade on the Barrier, a sweeter music than any
violin’s is made by water running through green
places 'in the darkness. It is like the voice of a
friend—a promise of help. Yet, beyond that, it is
something more—something which only the
genius of a German poet could half-interpret—
something not sad, but weird and beautiful.
It needs the night and the place to lend the
enchantment—trees to divide the current and
darkness to hide it. The water that I heard was
neither good to see nor to taste. It came from
the depth of a hundred feet, and the poison of
many sodas made it acrid. It nauseated even the
flowers; they drooped away from its bitterness,
and perished. Still, it was their only
drink, the garden-tank being long since
dry and the skies holding no promise of rain.
Bain ! A hit a lovely word it is, what a complete
sound-sig.iifiliation of all it means. And it came
cloudlet 1* t j' if : u wise : The skies were altogether

I,®, IL t ° f th ? corpse, and, when he opened his

t oor beggar !” said the policeman; “but
there are lots like him.”
This man had worked, how many years I do not
remember, m one of the Broken Hill silver-mines ;
and he had sold the purity of his blood, drinking
m poison at every pore, so that others might be
noh—-find for some shilling’s a day. “ There are
lots hke him,” and I wonder, if they were all mar-
shalled in the presence of the rich man, would he
be altogether happy ?
A week after, this man was found hiding like a
j n . a deserted hut. He was taken down to
Adelaide and put away in a gaol, or an asylum, or
a hospital, or (God being kind to him) in a
cemetery.
* • * •
The other man was an old track-wanderer to
whom the Great Silence of our country had given
the weirdest of missions. “Promise that you
will teli no one,” he said ; and I promised.
Wherefore I may not write what he told me.

RODERICA QUINN.
Bedtime.
‘‘Bedtime, children!” (Howls, &c.)
“Well, just live minutes more.”
(Five minutes later.) “ Come, now ! time’s up.”
(More howls.)
“We wants supper.”
“ I’m so hungry.”
(Bread and milk hastily prepared and set before
them.)
bay, ma, how do you spell Presbyterian?”
Lorry, aged 7.
Ma pretends not to hear,'and bends over the
three-year-old. “Be quick, Teddy; hurry up,
mother’s waiting.”
By-and-by they are safely landed in the bed-
room.
“My teacher was so cross to-day,” begins
Lorry ; ‘ ‘ can’t I stay away to-morrow, ma ? ”
“ Where sail I put my horsie?” demands Teddy.
“ O, here’s a bootiful place.”
“I say, ma, how do you spell Presbyterian?”
“Teddy,” says ma, sharply, “put that horse
on this chair at once.”
“A boy had such a dirty face-to-day, ma ; the
teacher washed it.”
“Who was it?” said ma, thoughtlessly; “one
of those dirty Smiths ? ”
“O, I’ll tell them you said they were dirty, to-
morrow,” said Lorry, quickly’
“If you do, you little imp, I’ll whip you,
mind ! ”
“No, I won’t do it.” (Grinning). “But, ma,
how do you spell Presbyterian ? ”
“Here, hurry up and get undressed, Teddy
dear; see now, I’ll unbutton your slices ; be
quick, Lorry’s nearly undressed.” (Prolonged
howls).
“ Stop crying—what’s the matter now?”
“ Oo nasty boot sing ; I wanted to take off my
socks.” (More howls.)
“Well, there, there; I’ll put it on again ; now
you take it off yourself. Lorry, what are you
touching up there? Come down this minute ;
you’ll break your neck.”
“ You do look so little, ma, down there,” says
Lorry from the top of the wardrobe.
“ If you don’t come down quickly, I’ll give you
such a caning.”
“ Stop ! Come back, you, bad boy !” as Teddy
hits out of the room, his little shirt-tails fluttering
in the breeze. “ And the cold you’ve got on you,
too ! Lorry, put your nightgown on at once, do
you hear! ”
“All right, ma; but is it P-r-e-s ”
“Drat the boy; if you worry me any more
with your Presbyterian I’ll give you a spanking,
I know.”
“Come here, you young monkey ! ” —to Teddy.
“You shall have this on.”
(Teddy captured and forced into the nil welcome
garment. Howls.)
“ Well, you shouldn't have been a naughty
boy, and then mother wouldn't have had to whip
you.”
“Now," says Teddy briskly when he has been
comforted. “ Now, as I dot everysing ? Where’s
my horsie ? I wants my horsie, dear little horsie,
did er’s daddy leave it in the cold ! ”
“You good-for-nothing little monkey, you !
what's that you’ve got in the cart?” (Sniggers
from Lorry.)
“Dat's my dirt.” (Howls.) “Don’t frow it
away ! my hoisie brought it.”
“ You scamp, in the clean sheets too !” (Howls.)
“ O, if .you cry for a week you shan’t have it, so
there!” (More howls, and groans from Lorry.)
“I say, do stop his noise ! I know I’ll have a
headache to-morrow.”
“I’m sure 7 will,”- says ma, crossly, as she
cleans up the mess.
‘ ‘ l wants my-y-y ’igs, ” sobs Teddy.
“Your what?”
“Mgs.”
“ What does the child mean, Lorry? ”
“ What's it you want, Ted?”
“My i;s ! ” fairly shrieks Teddy, “oo nasty
boot sings!”
“0, pigs ! his pigs, that’s what lie wants, from
his Noah's Ark.” P
After a long and weary search the pigs are
found under a flowc.r-pot in the summer house,
and Teddy nestles down among the pillows and
piep ires to sleep. There is for a time a silence
which is broken by Lorry.
v.
‘‘Ma, how many letters are there in Presby-
terian—seven ? ”
“ ’Sh, I’ll tell you in the morning ; go to sleep
now.”
“ I don’t believe vou know, ma.”
“If you don’t be quiet now, i’ll give it to you ;
Teddy's nearly asleep.”
“ No I isn’t.”
“Well, what are you so quiet about, you dear
little mouse—well, you are a little imp to-night ! ”
(pulling back the bed clothes in a hurry).
“ You've been sucking- Noah, and you’ll be
poisoned! you naughty, naughty child.”
Lorry sets up howling excitedly. .“O ma, will
he die ! what if he dies ? ” Teddy howls as his
mouth is roughly wiped out.
“ Ma, will he die ? ” frantically yells Lorry.
“ Wills I die ?” wails Teddy.
“ No, but he deserves to. You shan’t have
Noah any more.” (Noah is thrown out of the
window.)
“ Oo nasty boot sings oo ! ” splutters Teddy
when he can get breath. “Where’s my ’igs? ”
“Can't have the animals any more. See, here's
a pretty' scent bottle to cuddle up ; now be a good
dear little boy and go to sleep.”
“I won’t go to sleep.” (.Scent bottle is sent
flying and shatters a looking-glass in the distance.)
“Oo’s a nasty boot! ”
‘4Good patience! seven years bad luck.”
(Smacks, howls, sobs.)
“ I say, do keep him quiet! I can’t go to sleep a
bit,” groans Lorry.
“I wants biscuit, lolly, and d winks,” sobs Teddy
exhausted.
“ I’ll have one, too ; I’m so hungry,” says Lorry
springing up joyfully.
So refreshments are produced.
“Now-, for goodness sake don’t make any crumbs
in the bed!”
“No, Teddy, you can’t hold the cup by- your-
self.” (More howls from Teddy.)
“ Me wants to.”
“You can’t, I tell you. Well, just for a
minute. There, I thought so.” (Smack, smack.)
“All over the bed. Well, of all the tiresome
little —— ” (Howls from both.) “O, I’m cross
with him. It’s just soakj>”
“Well, will you ever do it again? There,
there; he didn’t mean it, did he ? He’s very
sleepy and tired, poor little man. Did mother
whip him ; but then he was naughty, wasn't he?”
“ I wifi bedood.” The sobs gradually subside,
and tired nature sinks nearly to rest.
“Mother,” break* in Lorry’s shrill, flute-like
voioe, “if Teddy had died just now, where would
he have gone?”
“ Hush ! To heaven, of course.”
“lint he’d been naughty, and sucked the paint.
You said bad hoys couldn’t go to heaven.”
“Be quiet, be quiet ! you’ll wake him up ; and
I want a few minutes quiet.” (Two minutes
silence.)
“ I wants to kiss Lorry.”
“ But you kissed him before. There, there ;
don’t cry ! kiss'him and be quick.”
“ Oo’s c’oss.”
“No ! mother’s not cross, pet! dear little follow,
he’s going to be a good boy, isn’t he?” (Kiss, kiss.)
“ Toll me about the bears.”
“Mother,” says Lorry, as she tucks him up.
“ Do tell me how to spell Pres ”
“If you wake Teddy up again. I’ll give vou a
whipping that you’ll remember : so there ! Go to
sleep.” - •

They please me not—these solemn songs
That hint of sermons covered up.
’T is true the world should heed its wrongs,
But in a poem let me sup
Not simples brewed to cure or ease
Humanity’s confessed disease.
But the spirit-wine Of a singing line,
Or a dewdrop in the honey cup !

The murmur of the mourning ghost
That keeps the shadowy kine,
“ Oh, Keith of Ravelston,
The sorrows of thy line !”
Ravelston, Ravelston,
The merry path t-h it leads
Down the golden morning hill,
And thro’ the silver meads ;
Ravelston, Ravelston,
The stile beneath the tree,
The maid that kept her mother’s kine,
The song- that sang she 1
She Sang her song, she kept her kine,
She sat beneath the thorn
When Andrew Keith of Ravelston
Rode thro’ the Monday morn.
His henchmen sing, his hawk-bells ring,
His belted jewels shine !
Oh, Keith of Ravelston,
The sorrows of thy line !
Year after year, where Andrew came,
Comes evening down the glade,
And still there sits a moonshine ghost
Where sat the sunshine maid.
Her misty hair is faint and fair,
She keeps the shadowy kine ;
Oh, Keith of Ravelston,
The sorrows of thy line !
I lay my hand upon the stile,
The stile is lone and cold,
The burnie that goes babbling by
Says nought that can be told.
Yet, stranger ! here, from year to year,
She keeps her shadowy kine ;
Oh, Keith of Ravelston,
The sorrows of thy line !
Step out three steps, where Andrew sfc©*#
Why blanch thy cheeks for fear? . « •
The ancient stile is not alone,
'Tis not the burn I hear !
She makes her immemorial moan,
She keeps her shadowy kine
Oh, Keith of Ravelston,
The sorrows of thy line !

Nothing to do but work,
Nothing to eat but food,
Nothing to wear but clothes
To keep us from going nude.
Nothing to breathe but air,
Quick as a flash’t is gone ;
Nowhere to fall but off,
Nowhere to stand but on.
Nothing to comb but hair,
Nowhere to sleep but in bed,
Nothing to weep but tears,
Nothing to bury but dead.
Nothing to sing hut songs,
Ah, well, alas ! alack !
Nowhere to go but out,
Nowhere to come but back.
Nothing to see but sights,
Nothing to quench but thirst,
Nothing to have but what we’ve got;
Thus thyo’life we are cursed.
Nothing to strike but a gait;
Everything moves that goes.
Nothing at all but common sense
Can understand these woes.
Alfred Dermttt.
We meet heath the sounding rafter,
And the walls around are bare ;
As they shout to our peals of laughter
It seems that the dead are there.
But stand to your glasses, steady !
We drink to our comrades’ eyes :
Quaff a cup to the dead already, - •.
And hurrah for the next that dies 1 /
Not here are the goblets glowing,
Not here is the vintage sweet ;
’Tis cold as our hearts are growing,
And dark as the (loom we meet.
But stand to your glasses, steady 1
And soon shall our pulses rise :
A cup to the dead already ;
Hurrah for the next that dies 1
Not a sigh for the lot that darkles,
Not a fear for the friends that sink ;
We'll fall ’mid the wine cup’s sparkles
As mute as the wine we drink.
So, stan 1 to your glasses, steady !
’T is this that the respite buys :
A cup to the dead already ;
Hurrah for the next that dies !

But soon, though our hearts are breaking';
They il burn with the wine we’ve drunk, -
So, stand to your glasses, steady !
’T is here the revival lies :
A cup to the dead already ;
Hurrah for the ncjxt that dies !
Who dreads to the dust returning?
Who shrinks from the sable:siiore
Where the high and haughty yearning
Of the soul shall sting no more ?
So, stand to your glasses, steady !
The world is a world of lies :
A cup to the dead already.;
Hurrah for the next that dies !
Cut off from the land that bore us,
Betrayed by the. land vve find,
Where the brightest have gone before isw,
And the dullest remain behind.
-Stand, stand to your glasses, steady !
’T is all we have left to prize :
A cup to the dead already ,
Hurrah for the next that dies !
Capt. Doivlimf. ,
Sigh, priests:! cry aloud, hang your pulpits with black
Let sorrow bow down every head :
The good friend who bore all your sins on his back,
Your best friend, the Devil, is dead.
Your church is a corpse you. are guarding its tomb :
The soul .of your system has fled.
That- death-knell is tolling your terrible doom ;
It tells us the Devil is.dead.
You ’re bid to the funeral, ministers all ;
We’ve dug the old gentleman’s bed : .
Your black coats will make a most excellent pall
To cover your friend who is dead.
Ay, lower him mournfully into the grave ; .
Let showers of tear-drops be shed.
Your business is gone ; there, are lio souls to save ;
Their tempter, the Devil, is dead.
Woe comes upon woe : you can,ne’er get your dues.
Hell’s open, the damned souls have fled :
They took to their heels when they heard the good news—
Their gaoler, the Devil; is dead. V\
William Denton.

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
As a result of prolonged drought in Queens-
land, the Registrar-General estimates the decrease
in stock for last year thus : —Pigs, 17,235 ; horses,
23,275; cattle, 995,632; sheep, 4,874,761 —total.
5,910,903. Taking an all-round valuation of 10s.
a head, this means-just on three millions dead los
to the Statp.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue *
Song of Black Nights.
Some sing Hymns to the Dawn ;
Let them sing, I will not bring
My harp to keep accompaniment.
Some make Music of Moons ;
Ah, pale Nocturne, my pulses spurn
Your liquid silver, your dim, wet gold.
I worship you, Moon, but you shall not hold
My soul in your hands, and the Sun’s red poem
Shall pass me by like a hidden cithern.
Moon, is it fault of mine that I do not set
Your tender crystal high in my heart?
Moon, is it shame to me that I will not let
Your fragile shining light me to Heaven ?
Fault or shame, I will keep my name
To set at the end of the only song
I ever will sing, my whole life long.
Sun, is it written down in your red, red book
How I was faithless, who loved you so well?
Then is it written, too, that my false eyes look
Up to your face, Sun, and all’s forgiven ?
Faith or fall, I must keep my all
To swell the sound of the only song
I ever will sing, my whole life long.
Dawn, shall I weep that the youth of the world
from me
Has passed and left me lonely and old.
Blind to the perfect rose that I would not-see,
Your beckoning blossom, tenderly calling.
Blind with tears, I have turned the years
To swell the tides of the only song
I ever will sing, my whole life long.
Night, will you hear as I lie at your shadowy gate,
And silent, silent, wait for your perfect breast.
Night, will you know, though my Wandering
Heart is late,
It is yours at last, and is yours for ever.
Little Dawn and the Middle Morn,
And Moon and Sun, I have left them all
For the tireless peace of your passionless thrall.
Listen, listen, my Heart,
Let us lay the white Moon here asleep,
Eiss her, and say a low good-bye.
Cover her face with the vines that creep
Through sunny places. Ah, do not weep.
Let us lay her here, asleep.
Listen, listen, my Heart !
Let us hush the baby Dawn to rest.
Kiss her, and sob a sweet farewell,
Cover her little angel breast
With tiny blossoms, half-blown, unpressed.
Let us leave her here, at rest.
Listen, listen, my Heart !
Let us clasp the red Sun once and then
Leave Him and utter no good-bye.
Cover his limbs with the eglantine
Too heavy and honeyed for mortal men,
Let us clasp him once, and then
Then to the Night,
And good-bye to light,
For ever, and ever, and ever.
Oh tender, noble, imperious black,
Best and bravest, shield that I lack,
And lacking, fail in the fight out there,
Wrap me round in your long black hair,
Cover me close with your tender arms,
Blot out the memory of stars and morn.
Wrap me close in your long black hair.
Warm and fragrant, and when I stare
Up through its masses to where the trees
Mutter above me their Symphonies,
I shall see no trees, and the Symphonies
Will persuade my beliefless, vagrant soul
That she is the only music-maker.
Only law giver, condoner, law-breaker. . .
And wrapped in your shadow, so close, so strong,
Lying silent, perhaps ere long
I shall make, or capture one perfect song.
Wrapped in the Night !
Ah, the wild delight
Of the great fresh world that creeps down and
near.
Wrapped in the Night,
Shut out from the light.
At last I can listen, at last I can hear.
At last I have caught the meaning
That haunted me always, but always fled
-Just as I gained it. Now, living or dead
I shall never be haunted any more,
For the black, black night has revealed the shore
Of the furthest sea in any world,
Has carried me up to the highest steep,
Has borne me under the under-deep,
And lying silent I know, ere long,
I shall catch and capture my perfect song.
My splendid, passionate, scythe-like song,
Blown of the dark as a soul is blown
Out of the black unknown.
N.S.W.
LUISE MACK.

Dear Sirs, —
In reply to your letter of the 16th inst., I have
much pleasure in stating that in January last during
some very hot weather your “ P & B ” Ruheroid Build-
ing at the Agricultural Ground, which was used as an
Orderly Room, was very comfortable and wonderfully
cool. I am of opinion that your Ruheroid Roofing fur-
nishes a very effective protection from the heat of the sun,
I remain, yours faithfully,
J. 11. A. Lee, Major,
N.S.W. Engineers, O.C.S.M.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia, and Itlaoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland! is now
■6l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of 1 \d. postage, the subscription
to Tiib Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease of in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage —ljd. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with pottage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in IHacJc and White.
Anyone supplying to Tiif, Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient,
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfit.te l for direct reproduction
—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under ivhich
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MAY 4, 1901.
Official Jingo Blackmail —Again!
The following circular, of which many copies
have been sent to The Bulletin by indig-
nant teachers, speaks for itself :
Department of Public Instruction,
Sydney, 25th March, 1901.
TO THE TEACHERS AND OFFICERS OF THE DE-
PARTMENT OF PUBLIC INSTRUCTION OF
NEW SOUTH WALES.
At a meeting of the teachers and officers of the Depart-
ment of Public Instruction (the Chief Inspector presiding),
held at the Girls’ High School, Sydney, on Saturday, 9th
March instant, it was decided to recognise the con-
spicuous bravery and ability of our comrades who fought
in South Africa and so highly honored our profession." It
was considered that contributions from One Shilling to
Five Shillings per head by every teacher and officer in the
service, would furnish a sufficient fund with which to pro-
vide suitable souvenirs expressive of our admiration for
our returning confreres, and mementos for the families of
any who in the fortune of war have given their lives for
their country.
The Executive Committee, consisting of Lieutenant
Colonel Paul, Messrs. J. W. Turner, W. H. Teale, W.
Gee, R. H. Grieve, G. A. Rourre, and F. Garden,
believing that the whole profession is heartily in accord
with this movement, confidently appeal to you for your
warmest and prompt support.
Contribul 1-mis (in postal-notes preferably, or stamps)
will be received by the Executive Committee, or by the
honorary treasurer, Mr. John Dart, Public School, Croy-
don Park, Croydon.
Head teachers are requested to kindly invite the co-
operation of every member of their staff.
F. BRIDGES, D. T. WILEY \Hon.
Chairman of Committee. J. H. STRONG / Secs.
• * * *
The school-teacher is one of the most
luckless of Australian civil servants. He is
exceedingly ill-paid as a whole, especially so
far as concerns the lower branches of the
profession. His lines are often—very often
—cast in lonesome bush places such as no
other public official has to continuously
inhabit. A very large proportion of the
N.S.YV. Civil Service lives permanently
among the flesh-pots of Sydney. Of those
who are not so fortunate, the Lands official
and the magistrate and the C.P.S. and the
border customs functionary mostly dwell in
towns of some size, where the cool’shadow of
the public-house makes an oasis in the land
and the long beer falls as the gentle rain
from heaven. The farthest-out railway man
is at least in touch with civilisation, and.
adjacent to the two streaks of rusted iron
which lead Sydney-wards and Melbourne-
wards ; also he is not wholly removed from
the stream of passing humanity. But the out-
back teacher is about the last and remotest
pioneer of the public service. He has to
carry the blessing and illumination of geo-
graphy and syntax to the last hamlet on the
track to Godforgotten, and almost the last
hove on the outer rim of the county of God-
torsaken. He is expected to know more
than 95 per cent, of other public servants
have ever heard of. His slow promotion is
gained by brain-racking study and severe
examinations. He is judged by his success
in sowing facts in the wooden and unrespon-
sive mental, soil of the bush - cursed child
out-back, and if he strikes a sutficientlv
dull patch of childhood, his prospects, how-
ever clever and industrious he may be, are
small indeed. He is a man of education, and
often of refinement and culture, who is
always liable to be sent to herd with hope-
lessly uncongenial minds in a sandy-blighted
land at about the wages of a corporation
laborer. And, in addition to his other woes,
he strikes things like the above.
* * * *
Ever since the Transvaal butchery started,
the N.S.W. teachers have been harried for
subscriptions as no other class has been
harried. They had the misfortune to have
as their political chief the boss political fool
and the boss public groveller of the N.S.W.
Cabinet. He apparently conceived the idea
-or some of his underlings conceived it for
him and he accepted it—that there would be
some special glory for somebody if the ill-
paid teachers contributed about six times as
much to the various S’African butchery
funds as any other equally-numerous section
of the community. So they received circular
after circular demanding to know how much
they would give in a lump sum, and how
large a contribution the Department might
deduct regularly from their scant monthly
cheques for a long period. And as the
result, there was a yell every now and then
that the teaching staff had nobly and volun-
tarily put up another thousand, or some
other large sum, in the good cause of blood-
shed and loot. When a bigger sum pro
rata had been officially squeezed out of the
teachers than was raised from any other
section of the Civil Service, the Public
Instruction authorities actually had the
brazen insolence to boast of the Patriotism
and Liberality of the squeezed teachers.
* • f *
The teachers are a numerous class, but
they are too much scattered to exercise any
very appreciable voting-power anywhere.
They had the misfortune, as already men-
tioned, to have the boss public groveller, or
else the weakest person of no account in the
Lyne Ministry, as their head. They read
these truculent demands for Jingo black-
mail, and noted that the men who demanded
it were those on whom they were absolutely
dependent for promotion.’ And either out
of a grievous mistake, or out of a good and
extensive knowledge of. the general work-
ings of human nature, they decided that
their Jingo bosses, or some of them, were
just the kind of persons who would block the
progress, and blight the career, and possibly,
in the end, harry out of the service the
teacher who didn’t “part,” and especially
the teacher who dared to “kick” openly.
So out of the pittance on which country
teachers are expected to keep up a semblance
of respectability they somehow made up the
subscriptions practically demanded of them.
* * * *
Of course the official reply in such cases
is that the Department has received no com-
plaints, and therefore the Department has
no reason to suppose that anybody is dis-
satisfied. If there have been no complaints,
that is about the hardest thing that can be
said of the Department. It simply means
that the ill-paid and plundered victims of
the Department’s yearning to be lavish
at somebody else’s expense know, or
think they know, the unspeakable
nature of the Department, and are con-
vinced that the man or woman who com-
plained, or who didn’t pay up, would suffer
heavily for it in some way. It is quite
probable, too, that the Jingo daily
papers received no complaints— at least
no signed complaints. They all ap-
proved, in a general way, of the steal,
and probably there was a pretty general fear
that the name and address of any objector who
wrote to them might be handed over to the
departmental bosses, to the objector’s serious
detriment. But The Bulletin can say con-
fidently that it has received somewhere
between sixty and a hundred separate pro-
tests, many anonymous and others not, and
no doubt other democratic papers have a
very similar record. And in every case
where name and address were 'given,
there was the same demand for absolute
secresy about the writer’s identify ; also in
almost every case the same reason was
ascribed—that the man who didn’t submit
cheerfully to be blackmailed would be surely,
on some pretext, or another, made to suffer
for it, and that under the present
regime freedom of thought, speech or action
meant ruin. If The Bulletin was rich
enough to compensate all these teachers for
the consequences it would put the whole-
batch of letters on public exhibition, and
make it known to the Education authorities
that they are, among a very large section of
their own subordinates, about the most
utterly despised men in New South Wales.
And now the employes of the N.SAV.
Education Department,'who have already sub-
scribed enormously more per head to war-
funds than the men of any other profession in
the country, are to be made the victims of
still another grab, and are called upon to
screw out of their meagre pay, the material for
still another splendid example of spontaneous
Jingo enthusiasm. “F. Bridges” “con-
siders ” that a certain sum will be enough,
and F. Bridges, as Chief Inspector of
Schools anxi a member of the Public Service
Board has such extensive power to make or
mar the career of any teacher that the de-
mand is a matter in which the poor teacher,
if born with a taint of suspicion in him,
may consider he has very little option.
F. Bridges requests every head-teacher to
pass the squeeze along, and to bail up every
member of his staff, presumably right
down to the worst-paid little girl-instructor
who is anxiously saving up her scarce
shillings to buy a new hat or a pair
of water-tight boots for the winter. And
F. Bridges is a man of sufficient official
weight to make most head-teachers bail up
all their underlings with energy. The state-
ment that the “ whole profession is in
accord ” with this scheme for once more
bleeding them of the money they can so
ill spare is an insolent lie, and if the pro-
moters don’t know that it is a lie why do
they urge schoolmasters to round up their
assistants and practically drag contributions
out of them ? The piratical demand for
cash admiration of colleagues, who have
been away* on a long holiday at better
pay than many of the working teachers
enjoy, is indeed a fine example of generosity
by deputy. The Bulletin is waiting to
hear about the liberal contributions of F.
Bridges and Minister Perry, who, if they
give £5OO each towards this burst of spon-
taneous admiration, will still have a far
larger margin left in their year’s salary than
the great bulk of those whom they are
harrying for contributions. And it is wait-
ing to see some Minister or high public
servant stick up the railway employes, the
police, and other public (and better-organ-
ised) functionaries for blackmail after the
same fashion and to the same extent as the
teaching staff. The new swindle is a small
one, but then it is only one of a long series
of similar squeezes. Many a weary, nerve-
racked girl teacher, to whom the semi-com-
pulsory toll levied upon her by fat, comfort-
able official city Pharisees has meant the
sacrifice for months of the few poor little
luxuries and enjoyments that made the dire
monotony of her existence a shade less
intolerable, will remember the present pro-
cession of insolent levies for many a long
day.
Talking About the Corpse.
New South Wales will shortly have a general
election for its State Parliament, and a certain
dismal fag-end of the Freetection party —mostly the
Sydney Telegraph and David Storey and some
other persons and institutions of their kind—pro-
poses that it shall be fought on the old fiscal issue.
The idea is apparently catching on to some
small extent, and it really seems as if the Protec-
tion v. Foreign-trade issue may do not a little to
decide who will be the next State boss in Corn-
stalk Land. The device is hardly likely to work
more than once, but if the people are taken in by
it even once it will be a public calamity. No sen-
sible individual wants to hear any more about the
fiscal issue in State politics. The State Parlia-
ment has no more to do with it now than it has
with Polar exploration, and just as much
to do with it as has the Municipal
Council or the Water and Sewerage Board.
If every one of the 125 members of the State As-
sembly was aForeign-trader of thebitterest descrip-
tion, or a Protectionist who wanted a thousand
per cent, tariff, neither circumstance would make
the difference of a single penny in the fiscal policy
of Australia. The fiscal issue in State politics is
Dead, and N.S.W. doesn’t want to waste time
talking about the corpse. There are live subjects
to talk about, and the State Parliament should be
elected solely with reference to the matters it has
to attend to —not on account of its views on the
tariff 4 , or the prevalence of, whales upon the
west coast of Greenland, or the designs of Russia
on Constantinople, or the alarming prevalence of
bluebottles in the south of England, or any other
abstract question. Even the exciting problem as
to whether there ever was a large Australian in-
land sea should not be allowed to distract atten-
tion at this crisis from matters of real import-
ance.
* * * *
The Foreign-trade Party (i.e., the party which has
always preached Freetrade and practised modified
Protection - cum - Revenue Tariffism) would, no
doubt, gain something if the fiscal issue could be
made the battle-cry at the coming State elections,
lints sadly indefinite way it has certainly a majority
in N.S.W.—not a large majority by any means,
but a majority none the less. If the election turns
on the tariff question, and the country wastes its
time talking about the corpse instead of dealing
with surviving subjects, that party gets whatever
advantage there is to be gained by representing
the majority on this very much defunct matter.
If the same party could contrive to hang the elec-
tion issue on the equally-defunct question as to
whether the export of British convicts to
N.S.W. was desirable or otherwise, it might
gain exactly the same advantage with the same
entire absence of any benefit to the public. But
it is only the dull and Tory members of either
fiscal crowd who are likelv to profit much by this
resurrection of the buried Lazarus of State fiscal-
ly™. As an example, that out-of-date fossil, Frank
Grimley, specially urges that the Foreign-trade
Party shall be kept together in the local Legisla-
ture as a bulwark against minimum wage
laws and other progressive ideas, which is
another way of saying that the otherwise impossi-
ble Grimley kind of man shall get into
Parliament on thefalse pretenceof Foreign-tradein
order to block social progress. There are men of
both tariff creeds who have lived all their political
lives on the tariff alone. Strongly Freetrade
constituencies and strongly Protectionist constitu-
encies have alike, in certain cases, returned men
on this issue alone —and have winked at their
Toryism and general incapacity for the sake of
their reliable machine vote on customs and excise
questions. These men lived on the tariff shindy,
and they don’t want to part with their bread and
cheese. If the only subject they know anything
about, and the only one which made the public
tolerate them, is torn from them, where are they ?
Obviously, they are nowhere in particular.
* * * *
To take a single instance, it is impossible to
imagine that any constituency elected David
Storey by reason of his liberal views on the re-
duction of the Assembly membership, on Upper
House reform, on the establishment of a State
Bank, State Land Bank, and State Life Assur-
ance Department, on a comprehensive Local
Government system, or on account of his brilliant
services in the cause of factory legislation, the
enactment of a legal minimum wage, the abolition
of borrowing, the enforcement of a graduated
land-tax and absentee-tax, the resumption of large
estates for closer settlement, the extension of Old-
Age Pensions, the shortening of hours of labor,
and such-like subjects. David Storey and his
kind lived on one subject—the subject that is
Dead so far as State politics are concerned. And
if the State won’t re-elect them on the ground
that they can be relied upon to talk eloquently
about the corpse, then their chance of being re-
elected at all is seriously diminished. As a
natural result, they want to keep the corpse well
to the front.
* * * *
The reason advanced why N.S.W. State politics
should be mainly devoted to animated conversa-
tion about the corpse is that members will fre-
quently gravitate from the State to the Federal
Legislature, and that if the Foreign-trade party
keeps hold of the State Legislature it
will make it so much the more sure that
N.S.W.’s delegation to the Federal Parlia-
ment will be mostly Foreign-trade. If the
old party-hacks who advance this theory
really believed it they would fight all municipal
elections also on the fiscal issue, on the ground
that men constantly gravitate from municipal
councils to the Assembly. It is just as essential, or
just as foolish, to try to maintain a Foreign-trade
majority in the State Parliament as in the munici-
pal council ; one body has exactly the same con-
trol of, and interest in, the fiscal question as
the other. The State Parliament is a body
whose sole business it is to look after the
internal government of N.S.W. ; not an institu-
tion for faking Federal elections. It has
enough work of its own to do—more work than
it is at all likely to do well—and it has no time to
act as Federal Parliamentary committee, can-
vasser and bill-poster for Joey Carruthers and
Co. Apart from this, the scheme of these gentle-
men for capturing the State Parliament m the
Foreign-trade interest is a purposeless affair, even
from their own standpoint. The people might send
125 Foreign-traders to the State Assembly (where
they can’t in the least influence the fiscal issue, and
where their tariff ideas are of no more public
interest than their ideas about a material hell)
and yet not send one to the Federal Parliament.
The Foreign-trade State Government and the 125
Foreign-trademembersin the State Assembly would
have no control whatever over Federal elections.
The constituencies would be different; the ques-
tions on which the election would hinge would be
different; even the franchise would probably be
different. If the N.S.W. Foreign-traders give the
Storey and Carruthers push a majority in 'the
State Parliament, in order that they may in-
fluence Federal elections, they will presently
awake to the dismal fact that'the State Parlia-
ment has just the same influence over Federal
elections as it has over the Gulf Stream.
* * * •
The Labor-party in N.S.W. State politics has
now the opportunity of its life. Its motto is, and
always has been, to sink the fiscal issue. Now the
fiscal issue has sunk itself, and the party which
doesn’t encumber itself with trying to keep the
head of the drowned object above water should
make the best time, other things being equal, in
the race. The question which has been the
biggest half of N.S.W. State politics for many a
year is only represented by two cold tombstones
in the background of State history. The public
wants to know now what its would-be rulers pro-
pose to do about the questions which are still
above ground ; it can’t take any prolonged in-
terest in squabbles as to whether tlie tombstone
that See used to sit on is better than the one that
Lee sat on. The live issue at future State elec-
tions is that of Progress versus Toryism. Every
man who even alludes to the old fiscal issue,
except to state that it is dead, should be dis-
qualified. Every old Tory Foreign-trader and every
old Tory Protectionist who applies for support on
the ground that he used to sit on the right tomb-
stone should be remorselessly rejected. Evcru
Foreign-trader and every Protectionist who applies
for support on the fiscal tombstone dodge should
be rejected on suspicion of being a Torv
and of using his old fiscal principles to
disguise his grievous want of new democratic
principles. All the old distinctions should be
thrown into the melting-pot, and democratic Pro-
tectionists and Foreign-traders should join together
in opposition to unprogressive Protectionists and
I oreign-traders. J. See and C. Lee must appeal to
the people equally on the strength of what they
are going to do in the future, and what they think
about the past is as unimportant as what they
think about the drainage system of the Ancient
Britons.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
225 Square Miles.
The Constitution of the Commonwealth provides
that the Federal territory, on which the capital is
to stand, must consist of at least 100 square miles
—it may consist of any amount more, but 100 is
the minimum. Last week, the announcement
appeared that Premier See was willing to go a
little beyond the irreducible allowance of 10 miles
by 10, and to surrender 225 square miles. The
offer can’t be described as wildly liberal, and The
Bulletin still holds that 5000 square miles, with
the Commonwealth as permanent ground landlord
of every inch of it, is the lowest figure which
should be accepted. The Federal territory wants
to be of such a size that all the land which gains
an increased value by the creation of the Federal
city shall be included in it, and that increased
value shall be the property of the Commonwealth.
With only a 225-mile area it would come to pass
in time that an immense increase m land values
—an increase created solely by the expenditure of
the money of the people of all Australia—would
take place outside the Federal boundaries and be
mopped up by the adjacent private landlord. To
anyone who considers the circumstances, 225 square
miles is an absurdity ; as for the proposed 100
square miles, that is mere burlesque, Melbourne
with its suburbs occupies 254 square miles, and
Melbourne is not a very large city, nor at ail an
old one; moreover, some parts of it are crowded
in a way that, it is to be hoped, the ideal Federal
city never will be. Taking Melbourne as an ex-
ample, the chances are that in 100 years the
-c ederal city would more than occupy even the


miserable “ enlarged ” area which,Premier See
proposes, and a part of the city would stand on
private land over which the Commonwealth
Government had no control, while every penny of
the increased land values created in the surround-
ing country would be annexed by the private land-
grabber. Australia has now a unique opportunity
for a great experiment in land nationalisation. If
it fools it away after this fashion it doesn’t deserve
to have another.
Federal Labour Legislation.
When there are no longer any border tariffs in
the Commonwealth territory, a new Labour pro-
blem must needs present itself. Victoria has a
minimum wage law —the only minimum wage law
on earth. It only covers a certain number of
trades, but it is being constantly extended, and
despite the hostility of the awful J.P. class, which
strives its hardest to nullify the Act in the law
courts, it has levered up wages till the Victorian
worker is by far the best-paid worker in the
Commonwealth. Victoria, by reason of the
scarcity of coal in its territory, has always been a
manufacturing State under difficulties, and if, in
addition to this, it has to compete, when border
tariffs are removed, with the lower-wage States
around it —the States where sweating is practi-
cally unchecked—its industries under present
conditions must needs come to grief. They
will be wrecked on the brutal commercial
law of the Survival of the Cheapest. Either
the other States must also adopt the minimum
wage system and pay their employes better, or
else Victoria must abolish its minimum-wage law,
and the best and most advanced idea in the
world’s social legislation must be abandoned.
Tory politicians in Victoria advocate the abolition
of the minimum-wage law. Democratic Vic-
torians, on the other hand, want the Federal
Parliament to take over the question, and make
equal minimum-wage laws for all Australia, and
lever all wages up to the same standard instead of
down to the same standard. And Sydney Tele-
graph, whose rabid hatred of everything Victorian
is miles ahead of the bitterness of the absinthe-
sodden gutter-journalist of Paris against every-
thing British, denounces the idea of abolishing
the sweater all round as another awful Victorian
conspiracy against New South Wales. It evi-
dently hopes that by denouncing better wages all
round as one more deep device on the part of Mel-
bourne to undermine Sydney it will rally up
the good old parochial gang to vote solidly in the
sweater’s interest. Some samples of the Tele-
graph’s crude and cumbersome curse follow :
The movement is regarded by politicians here with
some amusement and suspicion. ... It is taken to be
the aim of Victorian Protectionists to force on the Com-
monwealth the factory laws of Victoria, so as to reduce
them all to one level and secure that the Victorian laws
shall not place Victorian factory-owners at any disadvan-
tage in competing with the rest of the manufacturing in-
terests of Australia.
How terrible ! By this showing, the idea is sim-
ply to put all Australian workers on the same
level so that the Victorian factory-owner won’t be
crippled by having to pay higher wages than are
paid in the adjacent States, whereby the workers
of the other States will get better wages, and
there will be a fair field all round, and no favor
for anybody. More D.T.:
Mr. Higgins’ intention is interpreted to be to place the
factories in N.S.W. more especially under the same laws
as those which now exist in Victoria, and N.S.W. mem-
bers are not disposed to accept his view that it would be
a good thing to do it. . . . Mr. Barton forgets that the
whole of the States are not likely to take this bait just to
please Victoria, who is intensely afraid of FAIR COMPE-
TITION.
“ Fair competition ” in this case evidently means
sweating competition. The Freetrade party is,
and has always been, the sweating party, and its
organ calls on the people of N.S.W. to vote for
sweating on pretence of preserving themselves
from the encroachments of their insidious neigh-
bor. The Telegraph is one of the papers which
demands that the fiscal trouble shall still be kept
alive in State politics, and which wants to elect a
strong Foreign-trade party to the new State Par-
liament, and apparently what it wants that party
for, both in Federal and local politics, is to resist
minimum wage laws and other such interferences
with the blessed freedom of the sweater. Frank
Grimlet, one of the little wooden gods of the
N.S.W. Foreign-trade crowd, puts the same idea
even more forcibly :
lb would, therefore, be strange indeed if Freetraders
did not desire to be represented in the State Parliament
by men professing the same principles as themselves, and
it is absurd for anyone, whether Protectionist or weak-
kneed Freetrader, to cavil at that course being pursued,
or at the continuance of an organisation designed to
bring it about.
Besides, while it is true that after the adoption of a
uniform tariff the State Parliament cannot interfere with
that aspect of fiscalism which has to do with Customs
and excise, there are many other ways in which the State
Parliament can interfere with freedom of trade ; for in-
stance, by excessive harbor dues and wharfage rates, by
business licenses, or other internal exactions, by undue
preference in connection with Government contracts, and
by importing a few more legislative novelties from Maori-
land, such as polltax on foreign commercial travellers or
the Minimum Wage Act. Surely there will be work for
the Freetraders, as such, in the State Parliament.
F. Grimley, Foreign-trader, demands that the
Foreign-trade party shall capture the N S.W.
State Parliament in order to block laws that tend
to increase the wages of the workers. And the
Foreign-trade Telegraph backs Grimley in this,
while it also denounces any attempt at similar
legislation in the Federal Parliament as a loath-
some Victorian conspiracy, which every good Corn-
stalk should resist to the last. The Bulletin
moves that the Federal Parliament, by hook or
crook, takes over the question of Labor legislation
and enacts a Minimum Wage Law for all Aus-
tralia and Tasmania —not the Victorian one by
any means, but something even better and more
comprehensive. And it moves that all N.S.W.
should make a note that the avowed reason for
sending a Foreign-trade party into the N.S.W.
State Parliament is to block Labor legislation,
and should cast the fiscal question bodily out of
State politics for that very good and sufficient
reason.
Lucre and Loyalty.
The hard cash basis of Melbourne’s Great Grovel
is as palpable as are the Freetrade fallacies of G.
Reid. First of all the Government and the Mel-
bourne City Council wrangled as to the amount
each should pay towards the cost of decorating the
streets with colored sticks and bogus stone arches.
Then Mayor Gillott called a meeting of citizens
and it was rushed by all the boodle-seekers. These
disinterested patriots voted each other on to
committees to the number of 130 persons. The
apparent reason for this large number was to get
invitations from the Government for deadbeats
who had no hope unless they were on committees
of some kind. As the work that these representa-
tive persons had to do was very simple and did
not fully occupy their time, some of them
turned their superfluous energy to private
ventures. For instance, one little committee
conceived a grand scheme of decorations for a
block in Swanston-street, near Collins-street. And
part of that scheme is a grand-stand along the top
of the verandahs, the seats whereof will sell for a
couple of guineas a piece. A prominent member
of another committee was standing by when a
suggestion was made that the Government should
erect stands over the intersections of the main
streets, and charge a shilling a head for seats to
cover the cost of the stands. The prominent com-
mittee-man fired up instantly, “What!” he
gasped. “Charge a bob a head ? Where do we come
in with our stands ?” Where do we come in ! ! !
That is the ruling sentiment of the “patriots”
who are managing the Jookoyork demonstrations.
From the Government which spends public money
in providing entertainment for invited guests, and
the Cathedral chapter which sells the Tabernacle
frontage to a Commercial Travellers’ Club Syndi-
cate, down to the smallest shopkeeper on the pro-
cession route, the desire to “get a bit”- —even if
it be only a bit of cold turkey—out of the Joolc’s
visit is the motive power that works up the ex-
citement.

Federal IKkembers.
“Wurrym” sizes up the Westralian Federal
members :
Now that the Federal elections are over, including most
of the “ shouting,” people will wonder to some extent
what the gods have sent them in the way of senators and
representatives. Of the Westralian crowd, not one is
possessed of conspicuous brilliancy. Staniforth Smith,
who topped the poll for the Senate, owes his position to a
charming manner, a good stage-presence, and that mag-
netism that makes a new acquaintance say at once,
“ Here’s a white man.” Little versed in debate and a
diffident speaker, he will have to rely mostly on his per-
sonal charm to carry him successfully through the career
on which he has entered.
Matiieson has been apely described as “an English
Tory, masquerading as an Australian Democrat.” The
voice is the voice of Democracy, but the paternal “ coat-
of-arms ” with which Alec has adorned his shop in Perth
doesn’t belong to this country. He is said to be ambitious
of obtaining a portfolio in the Reid Govt., which, he
hopes, is to come shortly. An anxious waiter for dead
men’s shoes is Matiieson, according to common opinion.
Pearce and De Largie, the two Labor-members, are
workers, the one a carpenter, the other a miner. Pearce
was defeated when he tried to get elected to the State
Leg. Council. On that occasion the gilded Brookman
topped the poll, his election expenses being equal to 50s.
for every vote obtained. Pearce was fourth at about ljd.
per vote. Pearce is a burning, eager being, very much
in earnest, a great believer in the power of truth, thrifty
—he adds a little bit to his cottage each day after the
completion of his day’s work—with a kindly and attrac-
tive manner. De Largie has always been a fighter for the
workers —notably in the great Newcastle coal-strike of
years ago. He is a poor speaker, and, unlike Pearce,
does not seem to improve with practice. Personally he is
not so popular nor so well-read as Pearce, but the
miners have remembered his past services.
Harney largely relied on the Catholic vote to pub him
either first or second, and curses latterly at being placed
below men with what he is pleased to consider intellects
inferior to his own. Harney lias been successful as a
pleader, and has got Big-Head. Trusting to his powers
of elocution, which are undoubtedly great, he uttered
throughout his campaign large mouthfuls of froth, varied
at times with chunks of foolishness, such as his state-
ment that the northern part of Australia would have to
be allowed to employ colored labor. This was bad for
Harney, and it was doubly bid when, in trying to
explain away his foolishness, he blamed the reporters for
misreporting him.
Just- as Harney trusted to the Catholics to boost him
in, so EwiNG(brother of the N.S.W. senator) relied on the
Orangemen, he being Past Grand District VVilliAM-goat
of the order. Ewing is certainly one of the brainiest of
the crowd from W.A.—not that that is saying much—but
he is shifty, and, like Matiieson, though he talks “de-
mocracy,” he thinks the other thing. To him, social
position, birth, wealth, count for much—at least that is
the sort of Democrat Senator Ewing of W.A. is suspected
to be bj- some people. As a speaker he relies rather on
declamation than logic, Bis style being of the Buzfuz
order.
Of the M.H.R.’s little can be said. With the exception
of Fowler they are a colorless lot. Fowler is a type of
Scot that makes the name of Bis country respected where-
ever he goes. He has not quite learned to be an Aus-
tralian yet, but there are hopes for him. He is a stub-
born Freetrader, who accepts a revenue tariff grudgingly
enough, as a matter of expediency. Solomon was the
chosen of the revenue-tariff crowd and is locally popular
in Fremantle. Maiion.is the journalistic John Norton of
W.A. ; and Forrest—well, he’s just Forrest.
One lesson I think has been taught by the electors to
candidates —“ Leave religion out of politics.” Harney
and Ewing, representatives of rival sects,'were put at the
bottom of the successful lists. Cavanagii, the beloved
of the Roman Catholic Hierarchy, was easily put down by
Fowler, who mad 3 no sectarian references and for whom
no sect fought —this despite the fact that the R.O.
b shop instructed the nuns at the convent to tell the
children to tell their fathers to “ vote for Mr. Cavanagii
and to pray for him.” It is not to be supposed that the
day of politico-sectarian bitterness is over, hut I think it
has had a bad shake in W.A.

Editor Bulletin. —As to the following circular :
Dr. O’ Haran’s Vindication. Gundagai, 13th April, 1901.
Dear M id un,—The recent trying ordeal which the Right
Rev. Dr. O’Haran has undergone, and come forth with
his character unblemished, calls for strong sympathy
from all who are women in the truest sense. It is also
well known that Dr. O’Haran has been put to very heavy
expense, and for the purpose of helping him, who has
nobly and grandly fought a great battle, I have great
pleasure in convening a Meeting of Ladies of Gundagai
and district, for the purpose of felicitating Dr. O’Haran
on his triumph, and to assist him in a financial way. The
meeting will taka place at 4 p.m., on Wednesday after-
noon, 17th April, at Walker’s Hall, Gundagai. Should
you be unable to be present, I will be glad to receive
intimation of your cordial support, as we would be failing
in our duty as true women were we to allow the occasion
to pass without showing our appreciation for the man
who has so well and ably fought the cause of truth and
justice. I remain, yours sincerely, A. F. Dodd, Mayoress.
Now, whilst Dr. O’Haran’s vindication at the
hands of the jury was as complete as the issues
determined by them could legally extend, and
satisfactory to all right-thinking persons—does it
not strike you that his R.C. female adherents are
exceeding the bounds of sympathy and discretion
when they send out broadcast invitations for all
“true women ” to subscribe to an object which, if
its obverse be to felicitate the priest, has as its
reverse the desire to denounce and fuss about the
actions of the unfortunate member of THEIR
SEX, who evidently must have been either the
victim of a rake or the tool of a blackguardly con-
spirator ?—“ Vihtus Millia Scuta,” Gundagai,
April 12.

In the matter of the Federal flag and seal, regard-
ing which the Commonwealth Cabinet offers two
prizes of £75 each for the best designs, we read
that “a board of experts” is to select the two
best designs, which are then to be sent ’Ome for
approval of Downing-street; but whether the Im-
perial authorities approve of them or not, the
designs selected by the “board” are to take
the money. Which means that, either the first
prize-money may be thrown away and a new
competition called for, or the designs will be
adopted whether the British Govt, likes them or
not? And why not?—why, in the name of com-
mon sense are they to be sent te England at all ?
Does a man take the patterns of his new suit to his
mother-in-law for her approval ? As to the kind
of flag that will be adopted, it is pretty well a
foregone conclusion—six bars, five stars, a Union
Jack plastered on somewhere as an emblem of the
“loyalty” of the beknighted land. Our artists
may be as patriotic as any, but they want the £75
(with another £5O added by a tobacco-firm) more
than many, so they will design a variation of the
old commonplace, knowing they will have no show
for the money with any other. In this matter,
also, Premier Barton should see that the compe-
tition is timely and widely advertised, and not
left to the uncertainty of a newspaper par, as
was the case with the N.S.W. “Federal Ode”
competition.

Champion’s cutting phrase applied to the workers,
“Lions led by asses,” needs revision. “Lions
led by foxes,” fits in better in many places. Here’s
a pretty example of cunning : The Broken Hill
Municipal Council, controlled by a Labor party,
adopted the very good principle of spreading out
the available road work, &c., among as many of
the unemployed as possible ; so the rule was laid
down and generally applauded—“ First man put
on, first man to go off.” Next—“ What’s right
for a working man is right for a clerk; ”
wherefore, when retrenching, the Labor Council
sacked the oldest officer (the accountant) avowedly
because he was the oldest. Then —“What’s fair
for a working man is fair for a working mans
representative ; ” and so, against the reflection of
Labor-members Cann and Ferguson, the foxes
have been working all they know (over a dozen of
them are candidates for the Labor nomination
to the seats), raising this insane cry—“ The old
members have had a fair innings; give new
men a show now.”

Reading the cables by the light of events in
China, there can be little doubt that it is impera-
tively necessary to bring the Boer war to a speedy
end at almost any cost. Practically the whole
British army is locked up in S’Africa, and, no
matter how dire the need elsewhere, not a regi-
ment can be moved thence without imminent
risk of an explosion. The Indian army system
has been strained to breaking-point in China, and
it is notoriously uncertain whether the native regi-
ments would stand against Europeans. They
have never been trusted in the field against
Asiatics, even, without a white admixture of
about one regiment in three. Indian native
troops are notoriously shaky if their flanks and
rear are not quite safe, or if they are not well-
supported with cannon. If there is to be a row in
China, white troops, and plenty of them, must be
sent there, and, failing conscription, they can
only be got from S’Africa; hence, peace must be
made there at any price. Any trouble in China
might easily involve India —with results impos-
sible to guess.

Premier See in his manifesto to N.S.W. electors :
COMMONWEALTH CELEBRATIONS.
In view of the many extravagant statements which
have been made as to the cost of the Commonwealth
celebrations, the following figures will be of interest :
Accounts paid and due .. .. .. • • £115,418
From this amount may be deducted
the following sums —
Materials and electric and other
plant in stock .. .. £17,500
Increased receipts from Customs 9,400
~ ~ ~ Railways 35,000
61,900
Estimated net cost of celebrations £53,51S
There are just two objections to this off-hand way
of disposing of the Great Loose Drunk. The
£17,500 of material represents dead stock ; it may
have cost £17,500, but it has little or no saleable
value. And the £35,000 extra railway revenue is
a delusion and a snare. The Railway Commis-
sioners at the time informed the press that, owing
to the low fares, the cost of extra trains, and the
payment of overtime to employes, most of the in-
crease in railway and tramway receipts would be
swallowed up by the increase in expenditure.

Arthur Griffith, Labor member for Waratah
(N.S.W.) strongly opposes the proposal to reduce
the number of members in the State Assembly.
His objection is based on the result of the Senate
election, which was disastrous to the labor can-
didates, and he argues that the State electorates
are unwieldy enough already—instancing Wil-
cannia, which is 400 x 200 miles in extent. And
when members are fewer the new constituencies,
being naturally larger, would become unmanage-
able. And so they would be if the local member
still acted as general commission-agent for each
and every of his supporters. But with a Local
Government Act all this would be changed. Mr.
Griffith’s fear is that with big electorates his
party will be snuffed out. But he forgets facts.
Wilc-annia (his own dreadful example) Alma, and
The Barwon in the State Parliament, all huge
electorates, are labor seats. And if the N.S.W.
Labor Party blames the size of N.S.W. (polled as
one electorate) for their Senate defeat let them
study the Queensland figures. In Queensland the
Labor Party made its first startling success at
that same Federal election, and carried a con-
stituency twice the size of N.S.W. in a remarkable
fashion.
4
The census returns show that Sydney and Mel-
bourne are both much larger than was estimated
—Melbourne, 493,956 people; Sydney, 488,968.
Also, Sydney is much closer up to Melbourne than
was commonly supposed. When Melbourne was
believed to be a long way ahead, the daily howl
of the Foreign-trade press was that Protection led
to the congesting of people into huge, crowded,
horrible cities; now that Sydney proves to be
bloating much the faster of the two, the passing
shriek is that great and prosperous centres of
populationgrow most rapidly under Foreign-trade.
As a matter of fact, Sydney is far the more con-
gested of the two cities, and has been for a
long while ; it has nearly as many people on 142
sq. miles as Melbourne has on 254 sq. miles. And it
is quite true, as pointed out by the Sydney papers,
that if Sydney was extended to include 254 square
miles, as Melbourne does, it would be vastly the
more populous of the two capitals. In fact,
if the two cities were put on exactly the same
basis, Sydney would probably contain very nearly
half the population of New South Wales, and the
country population would probably be actually
less than the country population of Victoria.
If another 112 square miles were included in
Sydney, to make it the same area as Melbourne,
it is probable that N.S.W. would have a larger
proportion of its population crowded in one city,
and a smaller proportion in the country and in
secondary towns, than any other big State on
earth.

Regarding age, there is not so much difference between
him and young Parker, who had to take command of
the first contingent after the death of Major Moore ; and
who in that capacity earned a name amongst the best of
Australian officers. Perhaps, however, the name of
Forrest was regarded as sufficient evidence for damna-
tion. Just a word on that subject. I came to this State
with a prejudice against that name; but I have had
it knocked out of me, for in my life of half a century I
have never met better or more sterling men. John For-
rest is not the fool he is sometimes called ; and at any
rate he is a Man,

not in W.A. sth contingent many just as brainy
and manly as young Forrest? Yes. 2. Is it
desirable to-make lads officers when there are men

miers’ sons should be jobbed into commissions
just because they arc sons. No. 5.
Then where is Vic.-Westralian ? Gone wool-

Ed. B.J
Thus, an Adelaide “Anglican,” writing to the
press about Cremation :
Surely in our civilised land we are not going to stand
by as heathens, and allow so brutal and heathenish a cus-
tom. How dare anyone fly in the face of Almighty God
by handing over a body for cremation. When the soul
leaves the body the body as well as the soul belongs to
God, and to God alone ; therefore, how dare any human
creature attem >1 to put hands near it for the purpose of
destroying it? . . . God never intended that man
sh mid destroy a body in death, any more than he should
destroy it in life ; and, in my opinion, he will stand
equally guilty before his Maker for one deed as for the
other. He intended, and as good as gave the law, that
the body should be lovingly laid to rest in the earth in its
natural state, and so decay at its Creator’s own disposal.
No truly loving and sensitive person could possibly hand
over the remains of beloved ones for so ungodly and
heartless a ceremony! . . . The face, the form we
so adore, to be blown into ashes by the cruel ruthless
hand of man. “Oh, my God!” I cry, “in Thy mercy
deliver our land from this awful stain of guilt and dark-
ness.”
And this remarkable person—who implies inci-
dentally that he alone is in God’s confidence about
His intentions as regards the human body —leaves
it also to be plainly inferred that, as a “ loving
and sensitive person,” he would rather think of
his dear departed as a hideous mass of corruption
than as a handful of fleecy and inoffensive ashes.
There is a certain kind of alleged human being
which nothing can possibly drag out of the intel-
lectual pig-sty of the Middle Ages.

Hicks-Beacjh’s recently-promulgated Budget gives
cost of Boer war to date as nearly £150,000,000 ;
that is to say that each Boer of the (at outside)
15,000 killed, crippled, or captured has cost
£lO,OOO. This in 18 months and the war
is still costing a million-and-a-half weekly.
Then there is the expenditure of men—re-
cently cabled as nearly 18,000 dead to the end of
March, and 2069 invalids discharged as unfit.
But, as The Bulletin pointed out, that is not the
whole truth. There were thousands of others
who had been invalided, but had recovered suf-
ficiently to totter through their duty with perma-
nently impaired health. Now we learn that up
to end of February 40,594 had been sent home in-
valided, and it is safe to say that not one of those
would have been sent home if there had been any
chance of his making a recovery in S’Africa.
Hence the 37,000 or so who are supposed to have
recovered and returned to duty in England are
only the residue after the 2000 or so who are hope-
lessly crippled have been discharged. S’Africa is
enjoined to keep as many of its invalids as possible
to itself, and the Medical Board which examines
invalids at Netley is cautioned to pass for dis-
charge none but the hopelessly-incapacitated.
The others can be got rid of quietly by degrees.

Sir Alfred Milner, in a recent despatch, says :
“It is useless to deny that the preceding half-
year has been one of retrogression.” We knew all
that before; but what about all the wonderful
“victories” of that half-year? “Seven months
ago,” Milner wails, “the Cape was perfectly
quiet, and the southern half of the Orange River
Colony was rapidly settling down, while a con-
siderable part of the Transvaal appeared definitely
reconciled to the British. But to-day the scene
is completely altered. Fighting is still carried on
by the Boers, mostly for supplies, and the country
is denuded.” No wonder, also, that rumour in
London has it that Milner’s three months’ leave
is only preparatory to his recall. Surely that
“six months of retrogression” will be too much
for Chamberlain’s stomach to digest.

Editor Bulletin, —A few words in connection
with the recent Federal elections in Westralia.
The chief cause of the opposition shown to the
Barton Ministry was the fact that John Forrest
was included in the Administration ; the electors
voted not so much against Barton as against
Forrest. A short time ago Forrest made a
statement setting forth a list of reforms which he
had carried out, and I noticed that The Bulletin
referred to the matter, stating that even Forrest’s
reform record was better than that of Reid. But
what is tiie truth ? Forrest has been practically
an autocrat here for the last 10 years by reason of
the shocking electoral laws. The W.A. people
are the .most democratic in the Commonwealth,
but they have been effectually muzzled for as long
as possible. Nearly all the reforms which For-
rest boasts of having carried out were formerly
strongly opposed by him, but were all along con-
sistently supported by the Opposition. As the
feeling against the autocrat grew stronger, he re-
sorted to every expedient to save himself,
and to this end performed somersaults galore,
dishing the Whigs for the time being most
effectutilly. In fact, so much could be wrung out
of him in this way that many democrats were eager
to keep him in power. Present writer was one,
but with the passing of the Electoral Act of 1899
came the conviction that the Forrest Ministry
must get no quarter. Previous to the passing of
that Act, Perth, Fremantle, and the eastern gold-
fields, with a population equalling two-thirds of
that of the whole country, had but 12 members out
of 44. The redistribution scheme was one of the
greatest infamies ever perpetrated in any British
country, for all it did was to give a few extra
seats to the big centres of population, leaving the
sparse agricultural and grazing districts with
practically their old representation. In the Nor’-
west seven members are returned by about a
thousand electors, while North Perth has only
one member for 6000 on the roll. Then consider
how Forrest flouted the people on the question
of Federation, performing his usual somersault at
the close to save his neck. The people got
even with Forrest on the Federal referen-
dum, and again at the recent elections. Some
of the Bartonian candidates stood a chance
till supported by Forrest then they were
doomed And the coming State elections will, in
spite of the inequitable scheme of distribution,
sweep away the last remnant of the Forrest
Govt.— J.H. .
Writes “ Crank ” ;
The old bogey of the extreme complexities of the Hare
Spence system leading to blunders and a waste of votes
is surely decently buried now. To compare the elections
for the Senate in Vic., and Tas., each voting as one elec-
torate, the number of informal votes was in the first 8769
out of 149,012, while in little Tassie only 419 ballot papers
had to be rejected out of a total of 18,403, or 2 2 per cent.
The elector is not so stupid after all as his friends make
him out to be. What led to the enormous waste of votes
in N.S.W. was the attempt to prevent plumping, by which
not only any paper with more than the magic six but any
with fewer was rejected. In Victoria, with no such
paternal restriction, there was less disfranchisement; and
in little Tassie, where it was voting by preference, but
otherwise go as you please, only 419 voters were self-
disfranchised.

The announcement that the soloists at
Sydney’s big State concert are to be all visiting
pro’s, has aroused vigorous protest. In defence,
S.JM. Herald says that Australia is represented
by the choral societies and orchestra, in both of
which are many of the native-born ! Though no
doubt, the two ladies engaged (Mdlle. Dolores’and
Madame Belle Cole) materially strengthen the
programme, the Citizens’ Committee blundered
badly in aiming at the production of a “musical
treat” for the occasion. The “draw” for the
evening’s amusement at £1 Is. per head is the
presence for 50 minutes of the Royalties and suite.
Tor such an event Australian artists should have
been chosen, to show the visitors what the country
can do, good or bad.
