After sitting out “My Jack,” which drew
a very large house at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday,
this scribe has come to a resolution. If ever he
writes a melodrama (a thing that may happen to
any man if he is out late) he will submit the
manuscript before publication to a barrister in
large criminal practice, a solicitor, a sea-captain,
a full-blown general, and a professor of chemistry,
and get it edited by all these authorities regard-
less of expense. Then there is a fair chance that
his house will blow up in a natural sort of way ;
and his ship won’t fall overboard ; and his regi-
ment won’t make the grievous error of taking the
regimental plate with it on to the battle-field;
and when his villain dies of heart-disease it will
be heart-disease, and not a mixture of strychnine-
Eoisoning, ingrowing toe-nail and dandruff. Also
is falsely-convicted hero won’t be pardoned sud-
denly in the middle of the street by a policeman,
and the processes connected with the transfer of
real property will be less shamefully mixed
than they are in the usual drama. In the
usual drama large estates are transferred simply
by the villagers giving three cheers for the newly-
found heir, whereupon the evil-minded usurper
turns pale and flies by the back door, and Hi«
Majesty is done out of his lawful stamp-duty.
« * * *
In “My Jack” the long, greyish baronet
is harassed all his days by a terror which any
attorney would dissipate for 6s. Bd. He came
into the property because the family of the
previous owner was drowned on a stormy coast in
one fell splash, along with a whole ship load of
other people. But a certain Greek, who didn’t
know the previous owner from Pompey, found on
the shore, on a date unspecified, the birth-certifi-
cate of the previous owner’s infant son. Also,
Peter, the fisherman, who didn’t know the pre-
vious owner or the Greek or anybody else, found
a live male undressed baby on the shore, on the
same or some other date and either at the same
place or somewhere else ; and brought him up by
the name of Jack. And the fact that the Greek
is prepared to swear he found the birth-
certificate on the shore proves conclusively that
the infant that Peter is prepared to swear Ac found
on the shore is the rightful heir—at least, so it is
darkly understood. And the wrongful heir, in-
stead of hiring a Turk or some other foreigner to
swear that he found a nosebag on the shore, and
thereby proving that Jack is not an heir but a
horse, winch would be a simple and logical way
out of the difficulty, lives in a cold perspiration,
and his hair stands up in the night like the leg of
the fretful dining-table when it is turned upside-
down. Also, he wastes great sums.on the Greek
to buy his silence, and constantly puts dynamite
and false charges of murder under Jack, whose
escapes are marvellous. In the end, Jack and the
birth-certificate come together, and all is over.
Nobody dreams of asking for any evidence that
Jack is the person mentioned in the document.
In the Lyceum variety of melo drama, if a white
man has the birth-certificate of a black female in
his hand, that proves that he is her, and, if he has
two of them, then he is both of her.
* • * *
Nobody connected with “My Jack”
arouses any special enthusiasm, though Robert
Inman, Stirling Whyte, Frank Reis, and Miss
Ada Guildford inspire no active execration at all
events. H. O. Willard, the terribly-depraved
baronet, has one chance to make a hit, and he
throws it away. He is left alone for a few seconds
with a fuse, and there is an enormous quantity of
gunpower, or tooth-powder, or something, in the
next room, and he wants to blow Jack up. If he
snatched the fuse in dead silence, and lighted it,
and took two huge, noiseless strides into the next
room (being a long-legged man he could get there
in one stride), and came back with a glare in his
eye, the audience, not being an absolute fool,
would understand. But when a man with only
half a minute to do the dreadful but silent deed
wanders up and down asking himself at the top of
his voice if he shall do this thing, and calling to
himself to make no sound, and stands still for a
long time stating that there isn’t a second to lose,
the scene grows ridiculous. It is equally ridicu-
lous when the heroine’s brother (an escaped con-
vict) learns that the police will be on him imme-
diately, and sits down on a chair for a long time
remarking that he must fly without the loss of a
moment. And when old Peter goes out in a
howling storm which no one else dare face, and
sculling his boat quite easily with one oar saves
the shipwrecked crew, this writer’s resolve to
have his drama revised by a competent sea-captain
grows stronger than ever.

The Bulletin has nothing remarkable to
add to its previous observations about “Floro-
dors‘., A , 1S writer desires to record it as his
candid opinion that the beautifully-artistic “Rose
of Persia was worth 25 of “Florodora,” and
when he considers the cold neglect which fell on
le former show and the wild public enthusiasm
about the latter, his previous ideas re the deficient
intellect of his fellow-creatures are only con-
nrmed. -Probably his fellow-creatures, when they
read the above statement, will say that their pre-
vious ideas about his deficient intellect are also
confirmed—which only shows still more clearly
that a really able and cultured opinion is as much
wasted on them as an oil-painting is on an
aboriginal. Any letters contravening the above
V \ e ' V i. °* n S? 3 will be carefully suppressed,
Wh 1 4.u enable the writer to state next
week that his theories have evidently proved
unanswerable. As “Florodora” is now in
the middle of the biggest boom which Sydney
has seen for many a day it can evidently
struggle through without the aid of present
scribe s good word. As a matter of fact, he has
noticed that the shows he least appreciates often
struggle through much better than the other kind
—which is owing to his taste being above the level
of the common herd. It is no earthly use for the
common herd to call round at The Bulletin office
and argue this point. This paper is going to be
out on urgent business that day.
** * *
John Fuller’s company at Sydney Empire
goes through its programme with a swing and a
dash from beginning to end. The humorist of the
show is Will Watkins, who never escapes with
less than five encores. The average man tires one
out with one encore, but William keeps ’em
an d gets more of them. He is a parodist,
and as a rule parodies are very tiresome,
but all William’s are gems of the first slush of
t’l iriV : One can hardly call a new version of
„ j- .il 1 Oolden Slippers,” dealing with decayed
fish, like William’s “ Golden Kippers,” a gem of
the first water ; but of its kind it is amusing.
His burlesque of the Mafeking Night, “Just as
the Sun went Down,” is excellent. Two coons,
the Jones Brothers, are rather entertaining, and
the same may be said of Fred. Bluett. The Em-
pire’s prices are the cheapest in Sydney, and it
certainly does not give the least in return for
them.
* * # *
To-night (Thursday) “Smith” will leave
liis home at Sydney Palace Theatre for the last
time, and that deserving bird, the “Grey Parrot”
will cease at the same address on the same date.
Friday is to be devoted to a one-night revival of
What Happened to Jones.” On Saturday night
“ Jones ” will happen at Parramatta Town Hall,
and if the quiet and moral old town can shake off
its aged lethargy for once it will find that
“Jones” is good for it, and that it would be a
great deal younger in spirit if it saw things like
“Jones” oftener. “Why Smith Left Home”
will probably not appeal to Parramatta. It is a
very clever and artistic comedy, but then Parra-
matta (like Geelong) never leaves home, and a
play whose name suggests rapid movement might
make it feel tired. The thing that most impresses
a. stranger at Parramatta is the number of shop
signs stating that something or other will be done
for you “while you wait. Waiting is Parra-
matta’s strong point. And Arnold and his
manager, Lohr, are two busy men who wait just
about as much as a whirlwind does. When they
are done with Parramatta they do “Jones” at
Goulburn on Monday, and on Wednesday com-
mence an eight nights’ season of “Smith” at
Adelaide.
** * *
“ Cinderella ” closes its career at Sydney
Royal this week. It has done very well on the
whole, and if the book had been anything like
equal to the spectacular part of the show, and the
jokes had been only half as light and fresh as
Madame Darto’s dancing toe, it would have done
still better.
** 0 m
It was roughtly estimated that from
1500 to 2000 people swarmed Sydney Town Hall
last Saturday on the occasion of the “Bohemian
Night” tendered to F. R. Woodhouse. Com-
plaints were made that the bloated Philistine
made up altogether too large a proportion of the
1500, but the battered and sidelong soft hat of the
real Bohemian was not wanting. It was also
alleged that someone was knocked down
for saying Damn at the singing of “His
Majesty, The King,” but otherwise no excessive
brotherliness was shown. The Empire’s “Sad-
Eyed Shriek” sang “Three Nice Girls,” and
successes were scored by Burns Walker, Miss
King, and others. The usual people who pro-
mised to help along the benefit in the provision
department were accused of frequently forgetting
to do so ; and it was complained of one lady in a
high position that she did not even bring a bun
with her. The audience was very friendly to-
wards Mr. Woodhouse, some of it taking away his
overcoat and umbrella as mementoes.
** * *
Snazelle, with Chas. M‘Mahon as manager,
opens at Sydney Palace on Saturday with his huge
and glittering entertainment “Our Navy.”
Snazelle is a showman who needs no recommenda-
tion, and his present show has already travelled
over a good part of Australasia, and earned
good notices wherever it has gone.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Marie Lloyd has changed
her programme, and now sings of mixed bathing
and other things. Marie’s songs are mostly
jewelled in the tip of the tail, and the gem is of
sapphire hue. Her husband, on the contrary,
eschews the fleshly joke, and confines himself to
the psychological side of costers and their donahs.
Irene Franklin is back again and concludes her
turn with the jingle about keeping them Golden
Gates wide open \ and Riano, Johnson and
Bentley are giving a farewell demonstration that
man did indeed ascend from the low-browed
gentleman who travels across the tree-tops by his
tail.
The Melb. dailies are unrelenting in their
nonsense notices of the Musgrove Opera produc-
tions. The fiction that G.M.’s artists are too
brilliant for the tuneful old ballad operas was re-
peated when “ Maritana ” came along. Said the
audacious Age man : “It is in the last degree un-
likely that Madame Slapoffski ever took part in
this opera in her life before; even artists of her
rank have to come to Australia to learn some
things; and in her case the music of ‘Maritana’
has almost certainly been one. . . . Music
which your tenth-rate chorus girl—nay, even your
aspiring amateur —thinks well within her power
was here translated into sound by a singer who
is a thorough mistress of her art.” Now, what is
the truth about Madame Slapoffski’s “rank”
outside of Australia? In England, the clever
little woman gained most, if not all, of her opera-
tic experience as a member (not a star member) of
the Carl Rosa Co. And now she is taking her
opportunity in a country which has not quite for-
gotten Fanny Simonsen as Maritana, and Lucy
Chambers as Lazarillo, not to mention other and
more recent artists who sang Wallace’s music with
a charm which Musgrove’s co. cannot rival.
** * *
Musgrove’s co. is making things as vari-
ous as possible at Melbourne Princess’s before
the season closes on May 11. Last week “ Mari-
tana ” came to pass, and was found to be a more
satisfactory lapse into light opera than the
“ Bohemian Girl ” performance. All the same,
it was not nearly up to the brightest “ Maritana”
traditions. The music appeared to suffer from a
sort of Wagnerian depression, and some of the
principals were not the people whom a hard,
practical jury of patrons would have selected for
their gaudy parts. Culchaw mocks at “ Mari-
tana” nowadays, and even the Philistine believer in
“ toon ” pure and simple begins to demand an irre-
proachable character with the toon he purchases.
But it is only fair to mention that some of these
objects of Culchaw’s scorn owe their ancient fame
to finer voices, prettier women and more imposing
men than undertake the job at the Princess’s.
For instance, The Bulletin flatly declines to
accept Mooney as a picturesque Don with mash-
ing designs upon the Queen of Spain, nor does
Madame Slap, realise the giddy nightingaley
gipsy maiden of this journal’s dream. But no
matter. “Lohengrin,” with Ella Russell as
prima-donna, Berthold as the glittering custodian
of a weird water-fowl, and Lempriere Pringle for
Emperor of Germany, is more vigorous and human
than previous treatments of Wagner’s melodrama.
Also, “ The Flying Dutchman,” which was given
to Australia (D.V.) for the first time on Monday
night, is probably a serious success, although the
present paragraph can’t vouch for De Wet having
fulfilled the promise of Musgrove’s advt. If
another first production is to happen ere the
season comes to a respectable end, “LaTraviata”
will be the novelty.
** # *
At the Royal three or four evily-disposed
men of “The World” are still plotting to keep
the lawful baronet out of his title and estates, and
every night they take him to a lunatic-asylum
instead of persuading him to try a nice, long drink
with poison in it. Two of these conspirators
don’t object to blowing ships to pieces for the
sake of a little insurance money. The blood of
many drowned and exploded passengers and
crews lies clotted on their black souls, and yet
they permit one friendless individual (Albert
Norman) to go on worrying them. Other villains
in newer narratives have misapplied their wicked-
ness many a time, but Ikey Mossy Holt
and his brother spielers hold the record for
criminal stupidity. “ The World,” by the way,
is the only drama that lands Holt within
easy distance of the gallows. He makes
his final appearance at a fancy-dress ball where
the ballet girls’ dresses are trimmed with electric
lights, and thence he is led away to be hanged
without the option of a fine. After which Miss
Harrie Ireland drops dead casually, not because
her husband has recently broken Ins neck, but in
order that Albert Norman may request the
illuminated ballet to cease their revels, and
bring the curtain down with a few words
concerning the lady’s decease. Harrie of the
auburn hair is a specially attractive Mabel on this
occasion. Her voice is stronger than it was a
while ago, and her figure looks more plump, and
the thought of such a fine girl being buried next
day, or the day after, casts a gloom over the
audience as it clears out.
** * *
Nance O’Neil and Co, at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) have not raised the standard of W.
Shakespeare, but their wrestle with Ibsen was
brought to a close on Friday last. “ Lady Inger ”
settled down into a fairly comprehensible drama
after the first night, and Nance, in the name-part,
was able to deliver her final shriek of horror at
II p.m., or thereabouts. Nevertheless “ Lady
Inger ” showed no reason why it should ever be
played again, outside of Norway. The legendary
interest appears to turn upon a feeble mediaeval
insurrection, headed by a Count Somebody who
lives and dies behind tne scenes, and every time
Lady Inger’s twenty retainers want to throw off
the Danish yoke, she remarks that the spirits are
rising. Also, she has a confusing tendency to get
herself and other people mistaken for ghosts, and
her daughter, too, throws out a ghostly suggestion
in the first act, and the ghost of another daughter,
lying coffined under the premises, is sometimes
vaguely understood to be frightening the mes-
meric Danish Councillor, who leads both young
ladies astray. A drama in which a high-spirited
damsel falls victim to the first' foreigner who taps
at her bedroom door may be called “strong” by
the Ibsen cult, but such virility looks cheap
enough, anyhow.

** * *
The last weeks of Rickards’ show at Mel-
bourne Bijou, prior to its moving into the new
Opera House, are being gladdened by a remark-
able tromboner and a genial mechanical donkey.
Frank Latona, the tramp, is a humorist who
brings along a new joke in the first place
and sundry musical instruments afterwards.
His toy a§s commands shrieks of ap-
plause in between the serious parts of his turn ;
which turn stamps him as an expert performer
with bow and mouth. Latona, in fact, is nearly
all that the advt. calls him, and would be quite all
if the adjectives were a little less numerous,
Austin Rudd, Lizzie Kirk, the De Wynnes,
Leonards, George D’Albert, and- other people are
likewise ministering to the wants of Bijou visitors
in this time of colored poles and social unrest.

Some unjointed chatter from George Mus-
grove :
I haven’t a photo, of myself. I haven’t had one taken
for eight or nine years.
When the “ Belle of New York ” was running in London
I used to go to the theatre about once a month. No need
to check anything ; £302 a night every night and every
matinee, arid we had eight performances a week.
£60,0l)0? the man who said that I netted that Out of it
—well, I suppose he saw the books. I have another
“ Belle ” running now at the Shaftesbury Theatre. Its
name is “ The Fortune-Telleh” It ran three Seasons irk
America ; and every seat is booked in London for the
next six months.
Augustus Harris had six principal tenors irk his com-
pany. The night came when the tenor set down on the
programme became suddenly ill. Gus. ran round to
the other five. One wouldn’t, one couldn’t, the third
wouldn’t think of going on without being properly
announced the day before ; the fourth had an important
business engagement, and the fifth had a very interesting
novel and was too comfortable to talk business. No ;it
didn’t upset Harris. He was too used to it.
lam quite distinct from Williamson. All business is at
an end between us ; and we are just running in friendly
opposition. When we ran in partnership I stayed in
London and sent all material, pieces, and companies.
Charles Kenningham was principal tenor at the Savoy
when I engaged him, and Tapley occupied the same high
position at the Lyric and the Avenue. Kingston ? I en-
gaged him on his first appearance in London. It was a
matinee, and I arranged with him directly after the per-
formance. He’s got the stuff in him, and is good enough to
play all-round leading man in drama, in London or any-
where else. With the experience that Kingston has gone
through in Australia he should, with his ambition and
enthusiasm, become a finished actor by the time he gets
back to London.
I offered Charles Hawtrey, brother to W. F. Hawtrey,
£lOO a week for a five years’ engagement. He will possibly
earn £5OO a week in America—going as star and sharer in
profits. Little Tich, music-hall artist, wouldn’t go to
America for £4OO a week. Why ? Because he didn’t feel
inclined ; so he broke his contract.
American interviewers ? “ And,” said the American
interviewer, “ of course you think New York city is the
finest city in the world ?’’ “ No,” I said. “ Ah ! you prefer
Washington?” “No!" “Chicago?” “No!” “London?”
“No!” “ St. Petersburg?” “No!” “Vienna?” “No!”
“ Paris ? ” “No ! ” “ What, then, sir, do you think is the
finest city in the world?” I answered —“ Woolloomooloo.”
“ Dog gone me, if ever I heard of it,” he observed. And
next morning he had half-a-column about me and Wooll-
oomooloo.
Six American reporters got around me in an hotel-room
and wouldn’t let me out. “ Gentlemen,” I said, “ what
do you say to to-morrow afternoon ? I’m very tired now.
To-morrow afternoon I’ll give you a couple of hours if you
like.” They didn’t like it, nut, after a conference among
themselves, they consented to leave me alone till the
morrow afternoon. Next morning, however, a telegram
necessitated me leaving before noon, and so I didn’t see
the reporters. But it didn’t matter to them. Each had
a 2col. interview with me in his paper next morning.
At Poverty Point.
Manager Williamson, before leaving America, secured
the option of Australian rights in all plays, present and
future, produced by the almighty Frohman. Among
Frohman’s recent successes are “ L’Aiglon,” wherein
Maud Adams has “ knocked ” New York, “ Zaza,”
“ Sherlock Holmes,” “ Under Two Flags,” and “ Madame
Butterfly,” the latter, a short play with some remarkable
effects, being a big “go ” in London. All these are
likely to be staged by J.C.W. anon, as the first results of
his important ’Murkan contract.
News comes that Williamson was on the spot when his
Italian Opera artists were finally selected, and he reckons
that the enthusiastic Hazon is not overrating them. The
programme for the first three or four weeks is fixed.
They open (June Ist) in “ Aida,” with Larizza in the
tenor part; “Lucia” and “ Ernani ” come next, and
then “La Boheme ” will be introduced to Australia,
“ Otello,” “Romeo and Juliet,” and the more ancient
“La Favorita ” are also listed for a first production
this side of the line. Mr. Williamson, having heard
Melba in the opera, was much impressed by“La Boheme.”
It should get a fair show at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) next
June, for the orchestra is to number 41, and the chorus
is the most Federal body of singers that any Australian
management has ever collected. Voices have been
brought from Sydney, Adelaide, Hobart, and Brisbane,
but—says George Tallis—the choristers were mostly en-
gaged in Melbourne, “ which seems to be the best of all
recruiting-grounds. ”
A certain tenor has never been desperately popular
with local petticoat-wearers since a malicious rumor ran
that he had said, after his Faust debut: “ Vimmens lofe
me now, but ven dhey schall schee my Lohengrin dhey
sehall be mad mit lofe ! ”
Tom Kennedy, who looks after the Distressed Actors,
has had a distress of his own lately. A while ago long
Thomas suffered the serious accident of falling casually
down a cellar hole at a restaurant where he had never
before had reason to suspect an opening in the floor. The
question of a benefit for the damaged hon. sec. of the
D.A. Fund is being held over until the return of president
J. 0. Williamson to Melb.
Melbourne’s awakening to Nance O’Neil’s true dramatic
value seems to have been hastened by her La Tosca per-
formance. It *vas bound to come, anyhow. Rather
strange to say, many of the women—other than pen-
women—who were Nance’s hottest admirers at the outset
of her first season in Melbourne, are now vehemently ad-
mitting their loss of faith in the unconvincing stormstress.
As for the local press “ critics ” of both sexes, they have
so far committed themselves to the worship o< Nance,
that they couldn’t now see their way to go back on it if
they wanted to.
Tenor Louis Arens, late of the Musgrove Grand Opera
Co., has gone to Prague. He would have stayed had his
salary been increased, but Muzzy would not pay him
more than the £3O a week he was then receiving. A
commendable decision : the excessive payments made to
some grand-opera artists are not to the advantage of Art.
When a manager pays these thumping big salaries he has
to make things balance by cutting down orchestra or
chorus, or the minor performers.
Snazelle’s biograph, “ Our Navy,” which opens at the
Palace Theatre, Sydney, next Saturday, is about the onlv
show that seems at all likely to benefit by the “ York'”
flutter. In Bendigo last week the public were found to
be loyally affected by the fact that the little Dook is
something of a naval officer, and the biz. for Snazzy’s
instructive Bio. Song and Story entertainment was boom-
some.

* * * *
The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have now
completed and handed over their contract for the supply
and erection of a bucket dredging plant for the Ovens
Valley Gold Dredging Co., Pore Punkah, Victoria. The
work was done in record time, and the plant started off
and has continued to work without a hitch. Messrs.
Richardson and Blair, the engineers for the Ovens Valley
Co., express themselves delighted with the class of
machinery supplied and the expeditious and satisfactory
manner in which the contract has been carried out.*

* * * *
Most readers of The Bulletin will have heard
that Luhrig Vanners have proved a phenomenal success
on Broken Hill sulphides as also on low grade gold and
copper ores. All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on
up-to-date lines include a number of them. The South
and Junction companies each have about a dozen, no
slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances. *
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “ big prospecting shows ” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “ Option,”
Melbourne.*

Birthday, Yic. : Lewis, the former boss, has
taken the place of the man who got himself the sack
by writing a letter, the discovery of which he admitted
was an “ unfortunate circumstance.” For two years the
show has paid regular monthly divs. of 2s. ; reef small,
and sometimes erratic. Men who examined the mine
after one div. often wondered where the next was coming
from, but it always arrived, nevertheless. Mine looking
remarkably well in the bottom. Shares, 70s. ; have been
much higher, and not likely at present to go any lower.
* » * •
Birthday South (Vic.): Shaft 600 ft. For years
have been developing the northern ground trying to pick
up the Birthday run of stone, which has been worked to
within 200 ft. of its boundary, and good gold in the face.
Recently a prospecting drive was put out south, and
after going about 100 ft. a reef was struck, and has since
been driven on over 50ft., from 2ft. to Bft. thick, payable
all the way, and from 2oz. to 3oz. stone in the face. Looks
like a new mine, and there is no reason why the reef
should not be carried right up to the surface, but much
will depend on the length of the shoot. Shares, till
recently a few shillings, now 175., and worth at least Is.
more for every foot the level is extended on payable
stone.

Colonial, Royal, and Ballarat Banking Co. And as this
position of affairs cannot now be ascribed to the payment
of an exorbitant 4} per cent, interest rate on recon-
structed deposits (these having been wholly cleared off)
the rate seems low enough to suggest that a little further
writing down of the assets might not be out of place.
During the last six months the note circulation
of the National Bank has increased about £30,000 ; bills
in circulation have increased about £300,000 ; and de-
posits have increased by about £556,000. Also cash
and liquid securities stand at the very massive figure
of £3,464,264 —representing no less than 43 per cent, of
all liabilities to the public. The National Bank’s holding
of State and municipal securities alone amounts to
£768,610. From a depositors’ point of view the National
is one of the really strong banks of this country, but from
a shareholders’ standpoint it might have been better in the
end if another £250,000 or so of capital had been written off
last year. Further, it would certainly be much better if
the dividend on ordinary shares was kept down to 2} per
cent. The 5 per cent, dividend on preference capital and
the 3 per cent, paid to ordinary shareholders absorb
almost the whole earnings, and the process of rebuilding
the shattered reserves is now exceedingly slow. A sum of
£69,654 (of which only £19,220 is accumulated profit, the
other £50,434 being forfeited capital) is a miserably in-
adequate reserve for an institution of the National’s size,
and a much more serious effort should be made to
strengthen it.

doubt that it is doing well; in fact, so far as all available
records show, the year just ended has been the most
profitable in all its long existence of 66 years.

The income, at all events, shows a handsome improve-
ment, and the total funds are now £1,437,612. But while
the income has increased by £10,551 as compared with
1899, the expenses have increased by £6065, which con-
siderably raises the expense-rate for the year. The
Mutual Life Association’s expense-rate, in proportion to
income has been as follows :

The increase in the rate is a considerable one, and it is all
the more unaccountable because the new premiums (it is
new business which is always supposed to involve the
heaviest expenses) are no larger, in proportion to total
premiums than they were in 1898, and are smaller in pro-
portion to total premiums than in 1899.
Another regrettable feature, but an unavoidable
one, is the further fall in interest earnings. Every Aus-
tralasian life office is troubled this way. The interest
earnings for 1900 were at the rate of £4 9s. per cent, on
the average funds for the year, which compares with
some previous earnings, as follows
1891 £5 8 2 per cent. 1896 £4 18 2 per cent.
1892 510 2 1897 418 7
1893 5 7 7 „ 1898 414 11 „
1894 5 310 „ 1899 410 0 „
1895 5 0 2 „ 1900 4 9 0 „
The Mutual Life Association has abundantly
provided for this fall by reducing its estimate of future
interest earnings to 34 per cent., so there is a large
margin of safety. And as the fall in interest can’t go on
(or ever, It may be that an upward movement will set in
before it is necessary to get down to a 3 per cent, basis.
The Association still adheres to its laudable practice of
writing off losses and depreciation every year. Most
offices allow these matters to accumulate until the tri-
ennial or quinquennial investigation comes round ; others
follow the still worse practice of not writing off losses but
keeping the old values of depreciated properties standing
on one side of the account, with a reserve against them
on the other ; and one or two have been suspected
of sometimes keeping the inflated values standing without
any adequate reserve, which is worst of all. The Mutual
Life Association adopts the only absolutely sound principle.
It would do no harm if a little more information was sup-
plied re such items as “ Debentures, £78,515 ” ; and the
fact that the Association holds £91,569 worth of foreclosed
property—nearly £19,000 more than it did a year ago—is
a regrettable, though probably inevitable, circumstance.
Until the property-market improves, foreclosed property
seems likely to be a large item in every life office’s balance-
sheet. But, all the same, the Mutual Life Association
stands higher above the breath of suspicion than most
institutions, and it has always been remarkable for the
absence of doubtful or paper assets from its accounts.
Even such an- item as “ outstanding premiums,” which
certainly can’t be called very papery in its character, is
eliminated.
• • • •
The Carlton Brewery, Limited (Melbourne),
after tottering on the verge of great grief for a long time,
has at last made an arrangement with its debenture-
holders under which it should pull through. The Carlton
Brewery, at the date of last balance-sheet, had a deficit,
even if all its capital was written off, of £343,440. It had
some £681,000 of debentures, most, if not all, falling due
in 1902, but as it had lost all its capital, and more than
half the debenture money, payment was naturally im-
possible. On the other hand, the co. had a very good
paying business (its losses were not on its beer, but
mostly on its property transactions in the boom days),
and was making over £15,000 a year net. Therefore,
it appeared that if the debenture-holders demanded
their money at the due date they stood a
fair chance of losing it all; whereas if they were content
to wait they could get their interest in the meantime and
have a reasonable prospect of getting the principal later
on, when the deficit was made good by slow degrees out
of earnings. It took them fully three years to see the
point, but they have finally decided to renew for 10 years
at 3 percent, interest; half the profits of the brewery to
go in reduction of the principal of the debentures. On
these terms, and assuming a fair and reasonable public
thirst, Carlton Brewery should get over its difficulties.

• * • •
Emu Bay Railway Co. (Tasmania), on whose
line the passenger gets there gradually in an antiquated
carriage at a first-class fare of fourpence a mile, presents
a third disastrous-looking balance-sheet. The visible
loss is by no means the whole trouble, bad as that is. A
further grief is the large amount of paper assets in the
balance-sheet. “Rights acquired under V.D.L. Co.’s
Waratah-Zeehan Railway Act and other concessions,
£100,000,” is apparently a purely good-will item, which
should be wholly disregarded,,for the rights and conces-
sions of a line that is making a dead loss are worth less
than nothing—for the present, at all events. “ Prelimi-
nary expenses (including £37,433 on issue of debentures),
£42,124,” is also an item that is no real asset at all. If
these paper items are disregarded, and only saleable
assets dealt with, the situation appears to be somewhat
as follows : _ ,
Further Loss by Total
Ordinary Increase of Visible
Loss. Purely Paper Deficit.
Assets.
1893 .. £1395 £104,404
1899 .. 5078 £39,055 148,537
1900 .. 9181 , 60 157,778
There are a good many things wrong with the
Emu Bay Railway Co. in addition to the inevitable
troubles that attend any new railway in a thinly-peopled
country. The original shareholders put up £160,000 cash
(of which £57,778 appears to be more or less gone) to build
a line which (including surveying, railway and rolling-
stock) seems to have cost £394,535. The other £lOO,OOO
which makes up the £260,000 ordinary paid-up capital
went in the shape of shares to the vendor—whoever he
might be, and whatever he might have to vend—and this
£lOO,OOO, having no assets to represent it, makes
up the deficit of £157,778. Said £160,000 was, of
course, hopelessly inadequate for the construction of a
£394,000 railroad. Then instead of putting up the rest of
the money themselves the original shareholders started
raising it elsewhere at insane rates of interest. They bor-
rowed £200,000 on debentures at 6 per cent., and as the
expenses of floating these debentures were about £38,000,
the money really cost £6 3s. per cent. Further, a suni of
over £lO,OOO is deposited with trustees as security for the
debenture-holders’ interest, and if the co. loses the inter-
est on this, the cost of the borrowed money is really about
£6 11s. per cent. Further, £77,172 was raised by the
issue of preference shares which must receive 8 per cent,
dividend before the ordinary shareholders can get any-
thing, and these same preference shares have a claim on
4 per cent, whether it is earned or not.
Further, the Emu Bay Railway Co. only owns
half the line from Emu Bay to Zeehan. The section to
Waratah is merely leased. It was built by the V.D.L.
Co. and sold by that awful land monopoly to the Emu
Bav and Mount Bischoff Railway Co. of London (£60,000
paid-up capital and £130,900 in 4J per cent, debentures,
so the value of this section of 48 miles seems to be about
£190,000). The Emu Bay and Mount Bischoff Co. made
no dividends in 1895-6-7. Then the Emu Bay Railway
Co. leased this utterly unpayable section for 99 years at
£lO,OOO a year—to earn which it required to make nearly
6 per cent, clear profit out of a railway which was then
earning practically no profit at all. It has also a
right of purchase for £198,000. With half its railway
borrowed at nearly 6 per cent, interest, and the other half
largely built with money borrowed at somewhere between
£6 3s. and £6 11s. per cent., the Emu Bay Railway Co.
naturally makes heavy losses (it is difficult to see how it
can ever hope to make anything else). Meanwhile the old
Emu Bay and Mount Bischoff Co., which gets £lO,OOO a
year for the loan of its scrap of line, pays its shareholders
5 per cent, dividends. If the State had built the whole
concern with money borrowed at 3£ per cent, and no pre-
liminary expenses or debenture floating expenses, and no
£lOO,OOO for “rights and concessions” it would pay its
way at far less than existing rates—at least the evidence
seems to point that way. But there are hardly three
railways in Australasia which would have a ghost of a
show under the insane conditions with which the Emu
Bay Co. has to struggle. Despite the awful leasing
arrangement the co. would have shown a small profit last
year if its own line had been all built with its own
money. The interest bill caused the whole loss. Almost
every private railway in these States comes to grief
because the shareholders won’t put their own money into
their own speculation, but must needs go borrowing at
high interest. A co. which won’t back its own spec,
freely with its own money can’t get other people’s money
at a cheap rate.

During the quarter ending March 31 there were

* * * *
Instead of recovering, the lead market remains
unsteady, with the price bobbing up and down a trifle
over £i2. The Junction, which put its new re-treat-
ment plant to work in a final sprint to keep its head
above water, has thrown up the sponge, and (writer 8
supply of metaphors is exhausted), sacked everybody but
the watchman. The directors say the re-treatment plant
(that is, the plant which was used to pick a bit more of
the metals out of the tailings) did good work, and could
have paid with lead at £l3. Suspension of labor con-
ditions for six months has b'een applied for ; it s many a
day since a main-lode B.H. mine directorate had to be so
humble. All the same, it is better for everybody that the
mine should be closed for a while than that the recent
policy of picking out the eyes and jeopardising the
stability of the workings should be continued.
• * * *
Applications for suspension of labor conditions
have prettv often been part of a wage-reducing scheme ;
and on the board of the Junction are men whom the
workers don’t trust a bit. Why, however, can’t suspen-
sion of labor conditions be accompanied by terms
offering the men some protection for the future?
“Right,” the Minister might say, “ I’ll absolve you from
doing what you have covenanted to do if for 12 months
(or two years, or any other term) you agree to submit
every dispute as to wages to arbitration.”
• * * * *
Block 14 is discharging men, and keeping the
rest at work on the richer stuff. The B.H. Prop., how-
ever, is working on different lines ; instead of discharging,
it is absorbing the best men from the other mines. At
the end of Dec. last, it had 2426 men at work
on the mine; at the end of March, 2776. The
Central (Sulphide Corporations) and Block 10 are
doing the same ; so that, in spite of the shutting-down of
two mines and the discharge of men from three others,
6480 men were employed at the end of the third week in
April as against 6862 at the beginning of the year—a re-
duction of only 382. Metallurgist Savage, of the Sul-
phide Corporation, has come back from Europe with
authority to extend the reducing plant near Newcastle in
several directions with the object of bringing costs down
further. And as Mr. Savage returns, Mr. H. H. Schlapp,
his old chief and tutor at the B.H. Prop., leaves for his
first long spell since his arrival in Australia under en-
gagement to the Broken Hill Co. 14 years ago.
British B.H. made a profit of £21,393 for the
half-year to the end of December, and will pay a shilling
div. The result is disappointing, remembering that the
big fall in lead-value did not occur until the half-year was
over. It seemed likely that the co. would decide to shut
down till the market recovered a bit ; but, instead, opera-
tions will be curtailed ; the mill will run broken time on
only the better-grade ore. Of this, by the way, there
isn’t too much ; for prospecting has, on the whole, been
disappointing, and much development work is required.

* • • *
To an enquirer, “Pilica” An English crowd
(Mines Selection Co.) sent out a well-equipped party for
12 months’ prospecting in the M’Donnell Ranges, and
found nothing. Know nothing of any 40-head mill up
there. No co. has ever paid a div. out of anything in the
district, and the seasons have been so bad that the coun-
try is now practically abandoned. No word for "gold"
exists in the blackfellows’ language in the centre of S.A.
They have a word for “ quartz,” and plenty of
buck reefs abound, but gold is mostly more than unpay-
able. Consider it worst place in the world to go for
wages, and nearly as bad for minerals.

Gympie actually paid four divs. lately, and Ch.

Golden Gate Mill, on Towers, crushed 315 tons for 1828ozs.
» * * •
Lake View W. A. : The rumoured new issue of
50,000 shares officially denied, and another manager-
well, departed. What a pleasant billet the managership
of the Lake View mine must be 1 The man who can do it
without an everlasting nightmare must be a nerveful in-
dividual. When M'Kinnon reported in Aug. last that
there were only 70,000 tons of ore-reserves left in the
mine, the future of which depended entirely on new de-
velopments, he was called to London, and the publication
of his report was delayed till his arrival. Rumoured that
it was first intended to quarrel with him in London, but,
like Callahan the year before, he carried too many guns for
Whittaker Wright, and went back at an increased salary
—to find, k la Callahan, another man in his place. Of
course, in his absence, marvellous developments took
place which M'Kinnon probably has not been allowed to
se e—he might have looked on them with jaundiced eye.
One report says the mine has made another rich strike,
worth 15 to 18oz., at the 200 ft. level in a cross-cut west of
the main lode, and that shipments are to be renewed.
Even shipments will never send the shares to the price
they once reached, and rumors of them and rich develop-
ments have next to no effect on the market. Fourteen
times bitten, &c.
• « * *
Golden Horseshoe, W.A. : Last month’s output
was 15,0000 z. as usual. £60,000 worth of gold for an ex-
penditure of probably less than £20,000. Whence comes
the yarn published in all the eastern dailies two or three
weeks ago that the reef had not been cut in the cross-cut
at the 700 ft. level at the point expected, and that fear
was entertained that the lode was going back into the
Great Boulder, whence it came, although it was 30ft.
inside the boundary in the level above? Shares were
then £9 10s., they are now £l3 10s. —and probably thou-
sands have been over-sold meanwhile. Probably the
publication of this par. has made some people sit up—
£1,200,000 appreciation in two weeks, gross market valua-
tion £4,050,000 !! Just imagine it—for a 24-acre block
with the lode from which it gets four-fifths of its gold
running along the Boulder boundary, the main reef of
which mine changes its underlay with depth and has a
decided easterly underlay at 900 ft. Imagine, too, a 24-
acre block being worth £4,000,000, buildings included, even
in the very centre of Sydney or Melbourne. Also the
difference in the character of the investment: one
every month £60,000 is taken away from it for all time,
the other is practically indestructible and appreciate#
with time and people.
* ♦ * *
Perseverance, W.A. : Adelaide Critic, for
which paper the writer used to have some respect, says
The Bulletin still flaunts the lie that this mine is. heavily
in debt, and proceeds to show to its own satisfaction that
it is quite out of debt and making a big profit. The
statement is based on a circular issued in Feb., and giving
details only up to Nov. A cursory glance at it might
convey the impression that the co. was free from debt,
but a careful reading proves that £116,000 had then been
spent on the new plant, and this had not been reim-
bursed. The Bulletin cannot help the Critic s dullness.

A new aspect of the Plague question.
Brisbane Courier :
We should never forgive ourselves if an epidemic of
plague were to begin just as we were expecting the Duke
and Duchess of Cornwall and York.
The Courier could easily forgive themselves if
the Plague came and carried off scores of common
citizens, but if it scared off the Dook, that organ
would give one great howl of agony and expire.
Snobbery—thy name is Courier !

One of the sweetest of Yorklunacy surges-
ions was contributed to a Melbourne paper by an
anonymous ‘Flora.” This ingenious nymph wrote:
H.o Pl f P ° Se fhat there be ‘floral gates’ placed at
t ™ riVi an ° e •*? Pnnoe ? Fridge, to be opened by
two little maidens at the approach of their Royal
Highnesses If this could be accomplished I
think it would have a novel and pleasing effect.”
Ilora truly reveals the influence of Royalty on
the average mind, and especially the average
female mind. She thinks of the York and
X uchess not as ordinary worldlings dedicated bv
Dhance to the service of a certain practical system
Dt Government which represents their only claim
to importance, but as a fairy prince and princess
in a panto, spectacle. The real official personage
and his matter-of-fact wife are to be glorified
mummers in a well-staged show. That’s how
I lora, and her like, understand the case, and there-
tore she suggests a pair of floral gates on the Royal
path and a pair of “ little maidens ” to open them.
And why not ? Also, why not grace the York-
opening of the I ederal Parliament with a ballet
selection from “ Florodora?”

The poor, over-worked, ill-paid public-
school teacher is expected to be, at his own
expense, a standing advertisement of the Jingoism
of every blatant lat Man who is too mean to nay
for his own Jingo. In N.S.W., since the war-
craze started many of the smaller-salaried
teachers have been bullied out of as much as ten
per cent, of their scanty salaries in pay inf the
patriotic ” subscriptions truculently ” sug-
gested to them by Minister Perry or his satraps.
Now the same Perry has struck another
idea (one which he has borrowed from
Victoria, by the way, where it was originated by
the dreadful F Sargood, M.L.C., now Senator
by the grace of David Syme). It is that, when
the hrst Commonwealth Parliament is opened
there is to be a simultaneous hoisting of the Union
Jack at all the schools in the Commonwealth—
the teachers to find the flags and flag-poles, either
out of their own money, or by passing round the
weary hat after their day’s drudgery is finished.

* * * •
Geelong is bursting with rage over the
.look’s visit. According to the first arrangement
all Geelong was to see of the great apparition was
to consist of a 15 minutes stoppage at the railway
station. The sad little town on Corio Bay com-
plained that this was an insufficient allowance of
.Took, arid Premier Peacock’s Government offered
to let the royal train slow-down as it approached
the town, so that if York happened to be near the
window those townspeople who chanced to stand
on the right side of the line might see him if they
jumped three or four feet in the air. And Geelong
replied with indignation that it wasn’t going out
in its tallest bell-topper and longest coat-tail to
jump ridiculously on a windy plain to see anybody.
The Peacock party may now consider Geelong a
lost constituency.

The Mayor of Sydney says that the ques-
tion of “ doss-houses ” for the homeless poor must
“ sooner or later ”be taken up. With a severe
winter coming along, the Mayor would do well to
take the question up “sooner.”

Dear Bulletin.—Let rne once more call
attention to the gross cruelty to bus-horses in-
volved in the greasy state of the blocks in Oxford-
street, Sydney, after a little rain. Saw three
bus-horses down one day this week. The strain
on the animals with a loaded bus is terrible ; and
when they reach the top of the hill they are
trembling and covered with sweat. To anyone
who cares for horses, the sight is sickening, and
all for want of a little inexpensive top-dressing.—
Boko. [Absolutely true. —Ed. B.]
Leaks out casually that N.S.W. Govt, has
been spending £lOOO per month in rat-killing.
The capitation costs 6d. per head, which would ac-
count for 40,000 rodents per month, ’supposing
that no other items are included in the £lOOO.
Premier See’s announcement that he intends to
reduce this expenditure has aroused vigorous
protest. Ratcatching has now assumed the pro-
portions of a “ vested interest.”,
“ Shellback ” to The Bulletin :
By an order recently issued under the authority of the
Victorian Ports and Harbours Department it is directed
that the entrance to Port Phillip shall he closed to all
common or garden vessels from 8 to 10 a.m. on May 6th,
and also whilst the Ophir and escorting: cruisers are being
navigated through the Rip ; same orderapplies also to the
South Channel, the only deep water approach to Hobson’s
Bay. Query: What proceedings would betaken in the
event of, say, one of the Vic. colliers disregarding the in-
structions, and poking her grimy nose in front of the
Jook’s procession—for pari icular care is taken not to de-
fine any penalties in case of non-compliance. Said dept,
seems to reserve all its activity for engineering Royal
“ oorrobborees” and Lady Loch “programmes,” instead
of devoting itself to increased facilities in the matter of
coast lighting and means of inter-communication between
isolated and almost (from shoreward) inaccessible coastal
points. Recent wrecks on Vic. coast, all (with the
Federal exception) point to the absolute necessity for the
immediate erection of a leading light on C. Liptrap, which
in dirty weather would save skippers many an hour’s
anxiety on the long stretch intervening between O.
Schanck and the Promontory, and vice versa.

The crew of the yacht Ariadne, recently
wrecked under sue 1 ) queer circumstances on the
M.L. coast, were introduced to the owner by the
Duke of Manchester, and appear to have been
composed of “Johnnies” down on their luck, and
desirous of emulating “ Ye good old English Buc-
caneer.’ They were all “gentlemen working
their passages,” and had a delightfully Socialistic
method of toiling, between intervals of “dining”
with the captain and the owner. One of the crew
informed the Court (privately) that he bad just
received a wire calling him ’ome to a £72,000
legacy, and he wanted the bally proceedings hur-
ried up, doncherknow. Meanwhile, some of the
questions asked by counsel at the inquest, but
carefully left unanswered, leave a nasty taste in
the mouth.

An inquiry of a very evil-smelling charac-
ter has just taken place at Sydney Technical
College. It is to he hoped that the papers will
come before Parliament.
“N.’ : Wliafc shocking charnel-
houses, to look into, are the mouths of
too many Australian girls! The teeth
look like a forest, after a blackening,
withering, and blasting bushfire. Or
one might fancy a smashing blow with
a mallet had been struck on all the
teeth at once. Fine young girls, who
can t afford false teeth, appear as crones
with perhaps just two teeth left in front,
and those waggling. It is either this, or
a lot of glaring piauo-lceys for arti-
ficial teeth. Dentists are too prosper-
ous altogether. A radical cure can
no more be expected from them than
Law Reform from the lawyers. Let
me seriously and sympathetically
urge competent inquirers to take this
m hand. It would pay the nation to
see that the growing generation is sup-
plied with good, cheap teeth. Bad teeth
or no teeth cause annually hundreds of
funerals in Australasia.

* * * *
Throughout the abortive trial, at Darling-
hurst, of Jane Smith for the alleged poisoning of
Patrick Conway on board the s.s. Talune, one of
the jurors surprised Bench, Bar and everybody
present by his brisk and breezy examination
of almost every witness. Nearly all his ques-
tions, when answered, told in favor of accused.
Of some witnesses he asked more questions than
Crown Prosecutor Wade and Advocate Reid to-
gether. His Honor occasionally interfered
sharply, but the inquisitive juror held on
tenaciously to the end. He won’t, it is safe to
predict, again be summoned by the Crown.
*.V • *
Acting-Justice Sly followed some old bad
examples in Sydney Criminal Court on April 2(5.
He started the day at the usual hour, and pegged
away at the Patrick Conway alleged murder case
till the lamps were lit. He wound up his charge
to the jury a few minutes after 7 p.m. Then he
tackled another case, and pegged away at it till
10.30 p.m., when he adjourned. Sydney Criminal
Court has a bad record for night-work. A Judge
may be physically able to stand the strain of such
long hours, but more than the J udge should be
considered. “Good law is seldom administered
by candle-light,” said a late English Chief-Justice.
* * * *
In striking contrast to some Australian
experiences was Judge Hensman’s conduct at a
recent Pertli rape trial. Judge Hensman, who is
building up a reputation like Judge Bundey’s in
S. Aus., began his summing up, and then, finding
that it was 5 o’clock, he adjourned till next morn-
ing—he wanted to review the case while he and
the jury were fresh.

“ Don’t you think there is some mistake ?
Don’t you think Jim will come back again ? I
feel he will, somehow. He was too good a man
to drown ! ” Thus the widow of a seaman lost in
the Federal, at Melb. committee-meeting to in-
quire into the needs of the bereaved. Sixteen
widows, 29 children, and a number of relatives
dependent on single men aboard the ship. Old
Ocean’s depths of misery !

. In a M.L. paper appeared recently an
auctioneer’s advt. setting forth that, on behalf of
a returned war-correspondent, whose name is
given, there would be disposed of “a most unique
collection (though sad) of the British army
badges, also Boer official badges and buttons, the
whole being taken from the dead left on the field.”
“ Most unique, though sad ! ” Sad, indeed,
grammatically and otherwise; revolting also,
is the idea of deliberately stripping-off, for
m i ket-purposes, the insignia under which brave
men went to death.
Dear Bulletin, —About time we had in
our Universities a little less lecturing and more
teaching. At every exam.—in science particularly
—the failures usually exceed the passes ; and as
400r50p.c. gives a “pass,” it’s pretty evident
that lecturing on scientific subjects is sim-
ply waste of energy and time. A snub
from the lecturer generally conveys to the
student that questioning is tabooed. Was
present at a lecture one night when the
picture of a dynamo was thrown on the
screen. “ Wha—what is that —that blur
in the corner?” queried a nervous student.
“That, my dear sir,” answered the Pro-
fessor sarcastically, “ is only a blur to the
uninitiated.” Collapse of intrepid
student.—X.
The treatment of even minor-
offenders awaiting trial in Dar-
linghurst gaol does not tally with
the innocent - unti 1 - proved -gnil ty
notion. The solicitor or friend is
allowed to see the prisoner privately,
but is not allowed to bring in any
documents without having them first stamped
by the gaol authorities. Also, on coming
out, everything is inspected and stamped. The
idea may be to catch any information that would
help to secure a conviction, but it is more probably
mere silly red-tapism.
THE MAIDEN’S PRAYER.
[For The Bulletin. [
She raised sweet eyes so pure and true
To the cloudless heavens, all sweet and calm,
“ ’Tis, Oh ! to be safe in those fields of blue,
Asleep in the bosom of Abraham.”
They brought that maiden of thought divine
To the halls of the large and curving noses.
And there she met Lemuel vested fine, '
Abraham, Isaacs, and likewise Moses.
And authorised now by a ring so new,
By rubies galore and by diamonds pensioned,
She smiles, for her pious desire came true,
And she rests in the place already mentioned.
n. s. w. Constance ClVde.
Editor Bulletin. —When will the heads of
N.S.W. Education Dept, cease to demand part of
the teachers’ pay for purposes with which many of
the teachers have no sympathy ? Only a couple of
weeks ago they were required to raise flagstaffs at
their own expense ; now they are asked (really
they are ordered) to supply souvenirs for return-
ing S’African swaddies. If this sort of thing is to
continue, a teacher may safely reckon his salary to
be 10 p.c. below the nominal amount. —A Sweated
Teacher. [The Bulletin has received from
teachers nearly 60 letters written in a similar
strain.]
* * * *
The desecration of romance is crystallised
in the recent N.S.W. official recommendation that
all notices of marriage shall be posted on the
notice-board at the police court of the district.
Alongside notices about dog-licenses probably.
Some people have got hold of the notion
that Jenkins—the irrepressible Jenkins—sacrifices
much boodle for “position” by exchanging—if lie
can manage it—his £l2OO a year billet as Vic.
Clerk of Parliaments for a similar billet in the
Federal Parliament, where he will probably fbe
glad to jump at £lOOO a year, or less. But
Jenkins runs no risk of sacrificing a shilling of
pension. Under the Commonwealth Act all
State Govt, servants passing into the service of
the Federal Govt, take their pension rights with
them. So the disciples of Jenkins (whom Premier
Peacock and the Vic. Ministry in general
habitually address as (‘George”) may be of good
cheer. The present and future of that wily log-
roller are the Nation’s Care. He may agree to
take a* reduced salary to begin with —but then
there will be—well, titles and things.
• *
In illustration of the deftness of the Jap
labourer. A well-known Northern planter tells
this anecdote. One day he noticed a .Tap,
employed about tlie mill, eyeing a rough log
which had been thrown as waste among a
heap of firewood. After a while the little
brown man came and begged the bit of
timber for himself. The log was given
him. With a handsaw he ripped it up into
thin planks, then began to shape them, and
finally turned out a handsome piece of cabinet-
work —put together without a single nail. And
the planter aforesaid solemnly warns all white
workers against so deft and dangerous a com-
petitor.
In the course of a temperance-sermon the
other Sunday, Pastor Tregear told his Wesley
Church (Melb.) congregation aZolaesque anecdote.
It had recently come under his notice, he said,
that a Carlton woman had robbed her sister’s
body of its shroud, pawned it, and drunk the
proceeds—and was found lying speechlessly in-
toxicated over the nakei "orpse.

“Lord Roberts is sending to Australia
a gun captured by her sons from the Boers.” — CABLE.
T. BULL (complacently): “ Yes, my dear widows and orphans, you have
home a nolle part in this great war of the Empire, in the loss of your
husbands and sons and brothers ; but every cloud has a silver lining—
and now you have the proud satisfaction of looking upon a gun taken from the Boers, who
brought upon you all your present misery.”
of invincibly ignorant and murderous savages,
who don’t want the missioner or his religion, and
generally end by slaughtering him. The best of
those who take to the mission ary-business nearly
always lose their lives, or, like Livingstone,
frankly abandon the trade, and take to the
kindred pursuit of exploration. The honest and
capable missionary—seeing how little real effect
he produces—generally leaves-off caring for the
souls of his flock, and takes to looking after their
bodies. The dishonest missioner becomes a com-
mercial or political agent (or both), and some-
times, as seems to have happened in China, even
an open blackmailer.
In a recent M.L. licensing case Judge
Denniston decided that a “traveller” was
only entitled to drinks on Sunday for a
limited time after arriving at his desti-
nation. “He could legally quench a
thirst induced by his travel, but not one
induced by his stoppage at the place
to which he travelled.” The Judge
didn’t go on to explain how the pub-
lican was to tell whether the traveller’s
thirst was the legal one or not; or how
many drinks were allowed for quench-
ing the legal thirst; or what distance,
after the first travelling thirst had been
quenched, must be covered to entitle
the traveller’s month to be legally dry
once more.
* * » *
“X.”: Consider the lob of the
S.A. assistant-teacher. After many
years of service he may possibly reach,
by increments, the noble salary of £l5O
per annum. He is then blocked.
There is a regulation to the effect
that first-assistants may be appointed
with salary up to £2OO a-year; but
hardly one such appointment is made
in five years. In the city schools,
the assistants do the teaching, the
head, who merely supervises, re-
ceiving £4OO to £450 a-year, while
his chief lieutenant, whose work js
the most exacting of the kind in Aus-
alia, gets from £lOO to £l5O. W. A. is
fast drawing off the cream of the trained
assistants of S.A.
• * * »
N.S.W. Government’s resumption of the
Darling harbor area has variously affected different
individuals. Some are losers by the new order
of things, while others will gain largely. Imme-
diately Govt, took over the properties it started
collecting the rents, but it was by no means so
quick in recouping the ex-owners of the properties.
Many instances are related of well-to-do people
suddenly finding their incomes temporarily
stopped. The most astonished are certain absen-
tees in England, absolutely indifferent to the
politics of the land to which they owe everything.
To these the news that a beastly Australian Govt,
had dared to interfere with their plague-ridden
property came as a bombshell.
It turns out that all the talk about
J. 11. Grey, the arrested Geelong solicitor, wanting
to commit suicide on the voyage from Fiji was
pure “ gup.” The Vic. police who went to. arrest
Grey, gave it out that they had to watch him the
whole time, and Melb. Hkrald spread itself on
how detective M ‘Manamny had worn himself to a
shadow in the process. As a fact, Grey took
aboard a cart-load of books and spent his whole
time reading. Grey’s relatives in Victoria have
been quietly circulating the rumor that the little
discrepancies in his accounts and trust documents
were the result of insanity. They say he became
so worried over certain trust matters, that for the
time being he lost his mental balance. When
arrested he informed the police that, until they
pointed the fact out to him, he had not realised
that he had committed a criminal act.
The iniquity of the amateur Judge system
was manifested so strongly in Perth the other day
that the public had to get up and growl. On the
Police-court bench one morning was a person
whom nobody knew. He turned out, on inquiry,
to be a J.P. appointed some time back, but who
had never tried his hand at dispensing law. His
style, however, when he got to work was novel
and interesting, and some days afterwards he,
sitting with another J.P., created a sensation by
sentencing three youths to 14 days “ without the
option ” for playing “ two-up.” The papers made
such a noise, however, that the Attorney-General
awoke with a start and liberated the three youths
without any apologies to the amateur law-givers.
Didn’t, trouble about the Executive Council, but
just rushed off to where the Acting-Gov. was
sitting, and a message was sent away to Fremantle
Gaol.
* * * *
After Easter vacation W.A. Criminal
Court had a hard job to get to business owing to
lawyers and witnesses not having got over the
holidays. The Crown first called a murder-case,
and then consented to take the case next day, to
suit prisoner’s counsel. Then a criminal-assault-
case was started, and after it was under way,
it was found that accused’s counsel wasn’t present.
A messenger raked him in, and the legal man
stated he bad been “led to believe that the case
wouldn’t be reached that day,” and that, conse-
quently, he wasn’t ready with his brief. Then case
3 was put on, but, to make sure, all hands
concerned were first called, when it was found that
the most important witness wasn’t on hand. Then
the Judge, who was getting a bit warm, intimated
that he would take No. 4, but on the charge being
read it was found that accused’s counsel was
missing. He also “hadn’t thought the case
would he reached that day.” When the next case
was blocked on account of a witness’s absence the
Judge looked as if he wanted to burst; and the
court heaved a sigh of relief when the last one
started off without any hitch. While the jury
was in recess one of the back cases was called
again, and two more witnesses were absent.
Nothing happened—no fines —no anything !
* • *
Will King Ned follow the practice of his
mother and hand over £3 to each mother of
triplets? A S.A. groom has put it to the test;
though Gov. Tennyson, fearful perhaps of the
depressing effect of a refusal, has himself handed
out the money. But what is the matter with the
D.S.O. award to the mother in such cases?
The dusk lies on the river,
The moonlight holds the sea;
My heart beats in the rapture
Thy glances shed on me.
The pulses of my being
Flow on to meet the tide
Of passionate embraces
That draw me to thy side.
A silence, soft and mystic
Droops heavy on the air :
The incense of my sighing
Dies tangled in thy hair.
But pallid Dawn is sweeping
Athwart the slumb’rous ski
To slay our joy, by lifting
Night’s glamour from our eyes.
D. K. I.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ . Sydney, April 30, 1901.
Mr Deab Abini,—
A week of rain has given us a ohance of getting
sappy after the long, long summer. Everyone
who hasn t rheumatism, and there are quite 96
persons m Sydney who are free from it, acquires a
juicy look as the winter waxes.
Melbourne is socially the centre of interest. It
is also the centre of an Antarctic disturbance that
is like to wash it away. A correspondent who
looked at the wet, blurred city from the top of a
tower, says it reminded her of Ford Paterson’s
psychological landscapes. Later she grows boast-
ful over its crowded condition. “ Menzies’ has
overflowed into Selborne Chambers, whence the
barrister-people are retreating before a drove of
sheep-owners.”
That proves nothing in particular. Sydney’s
jasper-roofed hotel has been turning visitors away
all the week. Also, it had a German band in its
Winter Garden, the other night, playing “God
bless the Prince of Wales” over and over again,
lhen someone objected, saying “there’s no sich
jierson now,” so the Kaiserites hastily rushed into
the Marseillaise, and some Royalist French wool-
buyers rushed out.
This is the season of the benefit matinee, the
chrysanthemum show and the O’Haran guinea-
gathering. Apropos of Dr. O’Haran : Other day,
1 saw a youth and maid (who looked as if they’d
come from where the pelican builds her nest) stop
at a photographer’s show-case, and —“Emmar,”
said the male pelicanite, 4t look ! ’ere’s the chap
that s been haying his fortagraff took so much for
stamped in with another cnap’s wife.” He was
lindicatmg a portrait of—well, a very well-known
kawyer. Emmar replied that “he looked that
kind.
As for the benefits, George Pack’s showed a
whole star system—Nellie Stewart, Carrie Moore
Brownlow, Lauri, Rivers Allpress, and Arnold’.
Then there was a promising starlet named Maisie
Edwards. The audience forgave Miss Eugenie
Duggan for ringing that cracked old “Curfew”
in their ears because she looked so nice in black
satin and wore a diamond necklace. If these
were not real diamonds they are the best (and
most winking) understudies of the stones I have
seen.
i? n ,^,° f A he six over-grown, over dressed ladies
who (Cook s) tour into the scented island Floro-
dora sat in a stage-box hatted (with strings) and
coated, and boaed in the latest and warmest of
winter fashions. _ Yet you could have raised
mushrooms in six hours in the hot, vapory
atmospheric condition of the circle. A group of
pretty Smith-Leaving-Home girls, escorted by
one of the Florodora, clerks (the kind of clerk we
never saw on land or sea before), sat among the
gods and laughed like mortals.
Some of Sydney’s poor children will have a
brighter time througli Nellie Stewart’s Charity
Matinee—however brief the extra moral glim-
mer may be. _ There was a thronged house.
Several little girls were selling programmes and
bouquets in the circle, and to your Akenehi and a
man who were very early arrivals there,
danced up a little tawny-headed, linnet-like child
who started on the man, “You’ll have a pro-
gramme, won t you ? Yes, you’ve got one but the
lady hasn t and it’s mean to make her borrow
yours. Now have a button-hole. O, you ought,
it s for the great charity, you know ! That’s right
—now have another for the lady ? 0, you ought
I m Nellie Stewart’s little girl, and ” The man
bought all she offered him, biscuits, lollies, nuts
(enough for a family of squirrels) until other men
persons came in and drew off the spoiler.
Miss Stewart and a large amount of baggage,
consisting of her portraits of various sizes and
prices and little swags of sweets, came later into
the circle and did good business. Mr. Innes acted
as baggage mule— acted, mind. He didn’t look the
part.
A. wor( 3 of Tenor Woodhouse’s “Bohemian
Nl ffht. He will get about £lOO, but I think he
would have made treble that sum if Soutar’s
“ artistic playbills ” (scattered broadcast) had been
less—well, less “aitistic.” Scores of church-
social, humorless, non-artistic folk saw that play-
bill, with its leering, female, its
group of Rocks’ ” faces m the background, its
bottle and pipe and got-’em again black cat—and
they spoke, saying, “ A Bohemian Night is an
orgy. Lo, the promoters thereof confess it by
these signs and designs. It is no place for us.”
In reality, that B.N. was a good, too-long eon-
cer: with tobacco-smoke accompaniments. The
only sensational item occurred behind the scenes.
I hate to put anything in this letter that would
make men (or hippopotami) blush, but I must
drop some ink on the straight-fronted corset. It
came out (no, no, I mean it stayed in as usual, but
you knew it was there by the result) at Mrs.
W P. Martin’s reception at the Australia, the
other day. Our friends who have hitherto been
known as possessing developed figures—the dress-
raa _ k er s kind expanded busts, defined waists
and hips, looked strangers with long, flat, thick
forms. A suggestion, if not of slightness, at least
of shghterness, is obtained by this corset of
France that compresses above and below and
laces out the artificial waist-line. Of waists:
there’s one new skirt in Sydney and that’s not
quite finished. The waist of it comes as high as
trie top of a Swiss belt, and no one, perhaps not the
Pans dressmaker that designed it, knows why. A
coat is worn over the innovation. The girl who will
wear it can trim a hat with two fourpenny black
and white handkerchiefs, a buckle, and so much
witchcraft that people say “ how simple, yet
stylish.” J

Have you noticed how fond of big hats are little
women, how fond huge women are of little dogs,
and how pretentious are little sects. Some of the
Primitive Methodists, of Parramatta, have held a
meeting to protest against any alteration in the
Coronation Oath, and a copy of their protest has
been forwarded to London. Yet, the big Anglican
body and the powerful Presbyterian ditto said
nutbn of protests.

Some months ago the cable story-teller said that
a Mr. Hepburn Hastie had got a divorce from his
wife, Major Rankin of the Queensland contingent
being co-respondent. Now if the cable man has a
body to be kicked (we know his soul is not worth
losing) Q. Major Rankin, or his friends, ought
to remonstrate—with boots. The Major Rankin
who was really in the D.C. soup is not the Queens-
lander at all and is in no way connected with him.

smell of mackintosh The announcement that the
Gov.-General and his Countess were to be in
evdence inspired the May-preparers with no
exalted fluttenngs. The sun shone out too late
to provide against noses being damp and eyes

“To the bars of the Anthem, the Excellencies
marched into the Show, accompanied by an aide
(name unknown) who wears a broad boulevard of
a parting m his hair. In their train were
Mayor Gillott and his buxom partner, ex-Mayoress
th U e rn ven fi a rll« ne p 0r -J° hayseeds; also
Presi , dent > w ho seemed very
“2° f th f nove ty of his task when lie
started to adore the visitors. After the
adoration HI. stepped forward, and said,
in clear nasal tones, nice things of the Melb.
people and the reception they gave him the other

he “ ! on e is always expecting the
breath of popular applause to blow poor Hope-

sat bowing her haughty head, so that she pre-

winch was the left. But His Ex., suggesting
j, , warrior in ‘Ma Mie Rosette,’ murmured
other side, please.” A smile covered this little
comic-operatic incident, and, with thanks, labored
and grim, from the Mayor, the Opening Perform-
ance came to an end. 1 ere were a few hollow-
chested cheers, a scrap of clapping, and then the
V.xv. march round and exit. Treasurer Turner’s
spouse and the small young Aide with the boule-
vard-parting got the most fun out of the round
of the exhibits. The jolly matron, whose good
humor is always at high flood, whispered all sorts
of jokes to the imported article till that poor young
man s attitude of suppressed enjoyment seemed
to ask, ‘ Are there many more at home like you ? ’
Also at the Show were some sing-song gut-
tural tones. Minnie Waugh in a real smart
slap-up hat of hooped crown and a flutter-
ing voile skirt was the one sweet musical
exception. When she warbled with some
emotion, ‘ I am young . . . Come and take me !'
a number of earthy men in the audience looked
unsettled. Minnie has a way of leaning forward,
half whispering, and pausing at eloquent points,
which makes admirers rail against the conven-
tions. This inviting warble is a nice cosy form of
art, to coin a new expression.
" " I hate fashionable Melbourneites, they are
such snobs—they are even worse-than English
people,’ wrote the late Francis Adams. Lady
Hopetoun has confided to somebody that she likes
middle-class ’ people in Melbourne very much
better than the ‘ uppali suckles.’ The information
was not intended for publication, of course, but is
really no news, as her Ladyship has never been
at any pains to disguise the fact that Melbourne’s
Naicest ’ likes her not. The pretentiousness of
the first families is distracting to the nerves of
the Governoress-General, but so also is the
avidity of the ‘lower orders,’ who swarm round
the vice-regal carriage like sugar ants round a
treacle tin. Her Ex. finds peace only with the
wealthy lower orders,’ to whom she is gracious-
ness itself.
“SomeToorak women, wroth that the Mrs. Ex-
cellency has forgotten ever meeting them, declare
indignantly that she should never have allowed
her ‘sunny’ spouse to accept the Gov.-General-
ship if she cannot adopt his easy grace, his cordial
manner, and his cheerful philosophy. Why
should the She-Excellency chill them all, and
merely suffer the incense of functionaries with
such a compressed lip under the shade of her
stand-off hat ? Some of these matrons never do
the wrong thing, play the Lady Bountiful with
effect, and are really not at all bounderesque. But
the Excellency will have none of them. All—
mind, all— are to be frozen off. Well, till after
May.
“The Jookoyork business is going to
operate favorably for Melbourne livery-stable
keepers, and the local horsey world in general.
Every hireable vehicle in the city has been be-
spoken for 4 Celebrations 5 week, and every
driver has raised his price. Cooks and
waiters also have agreed to levy a
little Jookoyork tax on the bloated profits of
them who live by purveying food and shelter
to the gilded public. As for domestic servants
they are mostly hesitating just now between a

with missis, which will qualify the strife-seeker to

on Monday. Missis, for her part, daren’t say

“There is much bitterness among Yic. M’s.P.
in re the giving to each one of six Jookoyork in-
vitations for as many constituents. It would have
been all right had no paragraph been published
that these six had been given. But now the
trouble is that at least 600 electors are worrying
every member for each of his six tickets. In
reality, six invitations only enable three con-
stituents to be present, since the member has to
spare a second ticket for the wife of each one
asked. As a member put it to the writer: “I
didn’t want to be bothered with any of the con-
founded invitations. Of course, I should have
been worried by constituents for tickets, and
should have sent the applications on to the Gov-
ernment; but a polite refusal on official paper
would have satisfied me, as I could have sent it
on to the applicant, who would have seen that I
had done my best. But now—all my chief sup-
porters are annoyed because I did not give them
tickets. It is only ass politics to please six people
and cause deadly offence to hundreds.’
