Mn ‘Lady Inger of Ostrat ’ that garnet necklet
and pendant worn with Nance’s black evening*-
gown was a convincing costume-touch. Most
Teutonic women own garnet ornaments, and all
she-visitors to Germany or Sweden bring away
similar trinkets. The pretty red stones are handi-
capped by their insecure settings of pale ‘ German
gold,’ though wearers are generally blissfully
oblivious to missing garnets till some sharp eyed
observer points out unsuspected gaps in the glassy
red mosaic.
“ On first night of said Scandinavian drama, the
old-fashioned expletives pushed the principals into
a tight corner. The author makes the heroine roar
‘Jesus Christ!’ and the hero splutter ‘Damna-
tion!’ whenever things go contrary. Toe audience,
apparently resenting these interjections as gra-
tuitous O’Neilian and Kingstonian blasphemies,
took to catching its breath with a horrified hiss
whenever an archaic oath—most of them were
relinquished after first night—shook up the
dialogue. The supposed culprits soon recognised
the reproof, as one gathered from their worried
looks whenever ‘ language ’ was impending.
“ That assault upon the Blandoltian Miss Ross
—upon whom was thrown a can of beer and
Nubian blacking, mixed —seems so incongruous.
Picture to yourself Frances of the spotless muslins
and innocent hats —Frances, the better angel of
a hundred stage-sinners, douched from a billycan
and felled with a whip ! Now, if the victim had
been our old friend, Elizabeth Watson, with her
mocking laugh, raven locks, and orange or crim-
son raiment, this midnight street-attack by a she-
assailant would have seemed merely an effective
climax to the play—a real-life retribution for
much histrionic heartlessness.
“ One often meets plump, blonde Miss Lorraine
and plump, dark Salvi tramming round town. The
former, not yet quite free of her voice trouble, looks
bored and sad. She and the Italian tenor are old
professional colleagues, and have sung together in
more than one operatic co. T’other day, the lady
related to a local interviewer an adventure of hers
as Mimi, in ‘La Bohemienne.’ Mimi was carried
in dying, and the attendants didn’t fix the legs of
her bed properly. She felt the thing settling
down, and knew that the audience would think it
a splendid joke for a dying heroine to be heavy
enough to weigh down a bedstead, especially as
this particular moribund had rather a well-
nourished air. To make matters worse, stout
Salvi approached to sit on the bed and weep, and
Salvi’s ‘ weeping ’ is none of your passive tearful-
ness. In a desperate whisper, the invalid shooed
the surprised mourner off, while the bed legs
continued to slip. Brave Salvi came to the
rescue ! He knelt by the bier, back to the audi-
ence, propping corpse, couch and all on one
knee. Miss Lorraine,’ says the interviewer,
‘then adds, soberly (italics mine), next day Signor
Salvi told us that when he got home his leg was
bruised and blistered ! ’
“ Here’s a bit of history in connection with Mr.
Peterson’s appointment to the professorship of
music at Melbourne University. Among the ap-
plicants at first was Hamish MacCunn, one of
England’s leading musicians, and a composer of
European repute. Had he continued his candi-
dature, he would undoubtedly have secured the
appointment, as Peterson’s reputation was mostly
an Edinburgh one. But MacCunn withdrew, be-
cause he thought Marshall Hall was sure to be re-
appointed, and Hall is an old friend of his. Had he
had an idea that Hall’s name would be surrep-
titiously withdrawn by the Agent - General, he
would have continued a candidate and then
(oh ! height of comedy) the appointment would
have been a triumph for Marshall Hall.
“ Marshall Hall’s Interpretation Class is one of
the most enlightened and interesting institutions
m this city of impromptu cranks. And it is always
amusing for those not under the scorching fire of
his criticism. The other day he asked a student
who was playing some little thing of Chopin why
she fed angels with roast beef. Another one he
asked in the name of Heaven (or the antithesis)
why she ploughed along in clod-hoppers when she
should be tripping and softly gliding past in satin
slippers. And so on. His metaphors are strong
and vigorous, his candor brutal, his wit pungent
biting—and the students smile.
Nothing matters, he is a god. Besides, the ttirls
like to suffer.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Gov. Lamington goes 'Ome in June. His
book on Queensland should now be nearly ready.
Two well-advertised citizens of Sydney
now enjoy a bodyguard ; and each citizen carries
his own six-shooter.
It has just been noticed with dismay that
not one of the See Government is bald. The new
N.S.W. Premier evidently believes that a Minis-
try can have hair and brains, too.
J. P. Gray, the N.S.W. candidate who
made a remarkably fine score in his contest for
the Senate, is the son of an old Chartist, and has
worked with the British Radical party.
Five of the six N.S.W. Senators are by
residence and associations Sydney men. Millen,
the sixth, though a backblocker originally, turned
city man within the last few years.
William Wise, a native of Sydney, the

Died lately in Adelaide, J. S. Cocker,
whose unpunctuality saved his life once at least.
He was one of the first party sent up to the Nor-
thern Territory to stretch the telegraph line across
the continent, and 'ought to have come back by
the Gothenburg on her last voyage, but missed
the l>oat, and so lived another 26 years.
Mr. A. Matheson, a member for Westralia
in the Commonwealth Parliament, is a grandson
of Spencer Perceval, Prime Minister of England,
who was murdered in the lobby of the House of
Commons, by Bellingham, in 1812. The lunatic-
assassin, who had a serious and well-founded
grievance against the Government, was hanged
within seven days of the commission of the crime.

The London church papers are saying
nasty things re Sam Thornton, ex-Bishop of
Ballarat, who has taken a living worth £l5OO a
year at Blackpool, and the Suffragan Bishopric of
Manchester. When fat Sammy resigned Ballarat
he talked pathetic flapdoodle about wanting to go
home and be buried with his forefathers. But it
is evident that be really went to take a rich thing
in the way of livings. The poorer, hard-working
clergy of the Manchester diocese have a genuine
grievance against Samivel, for snavelling one of
the prizes of the church, which should have gone
to reward hard work and merit.
F. W. Webb’s decision to remain in the
clerkship of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly was not un-
expected. Many wished him to go over to the
Federal Parliament, but fifty years’ service in the
N.S.W. Houses has made binding ties. He was
quilldriving in one or other of N.S.W. Chambers
before the granting of responsible government.
J. G. Burtt, a 93-year-old pioneer of the
Eight-hours movement, died in Melbourne, on
April 21st, the anniversary of the day he used to
celebrate. Burtt formerly sat for North Mel-
bourne in Yic. Assembly, and was a popular
character as far as teetotalism would permit. It
is difficult for an enemy of beer to be the idol of
his constituency.
“ "Dave ” O’Keefe, who goes to the Senate
from Flyspeck Land as a Labor member, began life
as a battery-feeder (The Bulletin knows a dozen
eminent journalists and politicians who started
from the same mark). From country cor-
respondent he moved up to be editor of Zeeiian
Herald, and it was in that capacity that his
straight-out advocacy of the workers’ cause gained
him the friends who ran him into a Senate seat.
Waxellency Madden on the rival sexes :
“ Men are not so simple as women sometimes
think them. Women think they see through men
and walk round them, but men see the women
walking round all the time. Men know perfectly
well what the women are at. They submit because
it is extremely agreeable, and in the next place
it is the line of least resistance under the circum-
stances.” Who would have thought the wordy
man had so much wisdom in him ?
Premier Peacock being told by a batch of
unemployed that they could not take work in
the country because their wives and families
could not be left, sent an officer to wait upon a
dozen of the wives and bring in their opinions.
Eleven of the dozen were quite agreeable to the
idea of the old man going anywhere, and the
cacophonous Premier chortled triumphantly.
Alex, learns quickly. He’s only been married a
few months.
The effort to hoist patriarchal W. H.
Groom, of Queensland, into the Federal Speaker-
ship, is doomed to failure, notwithstanding that,
barring Barton, he alone of the Representatives
has been Speaker in a State House. Sixty-nine
years of age is in itself a handicap. But a worse
handicap is the fact that the inlluential*Queens-
landers who admired him in the Speaker’s chair
are dead or otherwise out of his sphere. MTI-
- Griffith, De Satge, Tozer, Arthur Palmer,
Nelson, and a score of others are really or con-
structively dead, and the Higgses and Glasseys
don’t cotton to him. On the other hand, there
are living and partisan forces behind the other
claimants for the position. Then there are other
reasons why the first Speaker of the Common-
wealth should be one of the very first men in
the Commonwealth.
One of Sydney’s best-known derelicts is a
middle - aged, broken - down English barrister,
whose only means of living
are the results of an almost
daily tour to various offices
and persons to whom his better
days are known. He is a
dreadfully disreputable
object, of appearance
more potent than the
longest temperance-
sermon. He has known
everyone worth know-
ing, and keeps up the
pretence of their ac-
quaintance now. Meet-
ing the refusal of a
long beer in a city office
one day, he announced
his intention of appealing to his
long-time benefactor (a well-known
barrister-politician). “ ;
he yelled, “won’t refuse me a trifle,”
with a withering glance at his audi-
ence. “I know him too well for that.
Do you know” (confidentially)—“it’s
my opinion isn’t a happy man.
There is a look about him I don’t like.
I’m sure he’s not happy. And do you
know why ? He’s not quite a lawyer and he’s
not quite a statesman, and, between you and
me, he’s not quite a gentleman.” And, happy
himself in the thought that there was a man in
the world deserving of his commiseration, and
with a deep sigh for his fellow-unfortunate, the
derelict drifted away.
There is plagiarism somewhere. Several
Df the leading incidents in the recent Coningham
case appear in Maurice Howlett’s short story,
“Madonna of the Peach Tree,” in “Little Novels
of Italy.” A passage: “She insisted upon a
devout churching (whereat, if you will believe
me, no other than Fra Battista himself must do
the office).” But there are others. The book was
published in 1899. The case was “ published ”in
1900.
The recently deceased Francis Curnow,
brother of the editor of S.M. Herald, fought for
the control of the Queensland railways as strenu-
ously and just as unsuccessfully as Goodchap in
N.S.W. Both were brought up on the railways,
and neither could understand the new wave of
thought which proposed to displace them by the
introduction of men from abroad. But Eddy
swept away Goodchap, and Mathieson, Curnow.
Mathieson is the only one of the four now alive,
and he quits Australia for his old home.

Dr. Jefferis, formerly of Pifct-street Con-
gregational Church, Sydney, who, at 76, has just
finally quitted preaching, might loug ago have
been editor of S.A. Advertiser, if not also of
S, M. Herald. As it was he for years combined
the posts and pays of a cleric and leader-writer ;
he claims to have written for the Advertiser a
quarter of a century ago its first pro-Federation
leaders. But nowadays his Federal ideas read
queerly —as, for instance, his declaration, in one
of his farewell addresses in S.A. lately, that the
‘ white Australia ” cry is “ immoral.”
Federal-Treasurer Turner is looking very
weak and ill. His five years’ work in pulling
Victoria out of the hole in which Gillies and co.
left her, has broken his health, and he will never
again be the same man. During his recent attack
of dysentery he fainted three times. It is very
doubtful whether he will be able to carry on the
heavy work that is falling to his share in the
Federal Cabinet. He is the one man on whom
Barton relies in financial matters, and his sound
common-sense views carry great weight i-n other
directions. Barton values no man in his Cabinet
more than Turner.
About the inventor of Voting by Ballot
S.A. Sheriff Boothby still has in his possession his draft
of the Ballot Act, passed in 1802, and since copied all
over the world. Boothby prepared the Bill for Premier
Hanson, who at first strenuously opposed the idea of
putting a cross within the square. Long ere this, Boothby
had conducted elections under the old style, when voters
crossed out with pen and ink the names of those they
didn’t want. Boothby even goes back to the days when
the “ free and enlightened ” went to a clerk and inti-
mated the name of the candidate they preferred. S.A.’s
chief Returning-Officer is a tall, handsome, active
man, 70 years of age, and is the most popular officer in the
State service.
“ Old Jack” : In the brief notices written
about the late “ Bob ” Whitworth, no mention has
been made of his Maoriland experiences. Bob
was the man who, with his bull-dog “Jerry,”
passed from Martin’s Bay, West Coast, across to
the East Coast—a route never before travelled by
man. On the way he lived, for the
most part, on the Maori-hens caught
by Jerry. It was a noted feat for which
Whitworth got scant reward from M.L. Govt.
And Bob did many things which ought to be laid
to his credit. But he was a modest man, and his
wraith no doubt is better pleased than hurt that
his works in life shall be forgotten.
“ Anti-Humbug ” writes from Geelong :
Editor Bulletin. —How on earth did the idea that J. H.
Grey, the defaulting Geelong solicitor, was “ pious,” get
into the press? It is entirely unfounded. J H.G. may
have been a “good liver” in the material sense —and a
lot of fiction has been woven about him in that respect
also —but, in his reckless moments he never posed as a
pietist. There was a maudlin ambition of that kind in his
composition, but he was a good judge of horseflesh, could
exercise a fine, if costly discrimination in the choice of his
liquors, and liked to surround himself with elegance. His
affection also is reputed to have been of an all-embracing
description. But he did a lot of good without bragging,
and his righteous critics, many of whom are better able
to do so than Grey was in his palmiest days, might
imitate his practical and secret kindness to many an un-
fortunate devil who has to thank “Joe” Grey for being
now on his feet. If charity covereth a multitude of sins,
“Joe” Grey has woven a more ample garment than the
paddockful of purse-proud detractors in Geelong who
glory in his downfall. Gleeson, the plain-clothes man by
whom he was arrested at Suva, says that Grey’s condition
there was deplorable, and £6 was all the ready cash he
could muster.
Charity, it is true, does cover a multitude of sins
—charity at your own expense.

for Cowra (brother of would-be
Senator Colonel Waddell) who
is the new N.S.W. State

strongly opposes woman’s-

office may be taken to mean
that a grave constitutional diffi-
culty has been amicably
settled. Waddell’s electorate
contains two big towns —
Blayney and Cowra. At least,
Blayney and Cowra consider
themselves big; and the
jealousy supposed to exist
between Melbourne and
Sydney is as nothing to
that between Waddell’s
two villages. Some few
months ago a P.M. was
appointed to Blayney;
but, not finding a house
to suit him, obtained
permission to shift his
headquarters to Cowra.
The insinuation that
Cowra could provide, what
Blayney could not was too
much for the latter “great
centre,” and the member
was told to see that justice
was done. After some weeks
battling with the Justice Dept. ,
Waddell brought the question before
the House. It happened that Minister
Wood, oblivious of the impending hurricane,
was playing dominoes or something at the
time, and the Assembly waited in solemn
silence until he could be found. The Minister
then plaintively explained that the P.M.
could not live in a tree—even a Blayney
tree _ an d asked Mr. Waddell to put him up a
house as the best solution of the difficulty. Wad-
dell retorted that there were perfectly lovely
houses in Blayney, and that people who wanted
palaces should not be appointed P.M’s. He wound
up by declaring that if the luxurious official was
not transferred in a week he (Waddell) would
cross the floor and sit in Opposition. The House
rose in a day or two ; and noiv it may be taken
for granted that if the P.M. has not already gone
back to Blayney he soon will go.

“ Phil May is not going to lecture,” writes
Thf. Bulletin’s London correspondent. “On his
alleged lecturing tour he is simply going to ‘jaw ’
—at least he says so himself, and he ought to
know. He ‘jaws’ London, the Provinces, the
U.S.—l2 months there—and finally Australia.
He gets £lOO a week for his own pocket-money
alone, and his complete honorarium is munificent.
Hugo Gorlitz—Amy Sherwin’s husband—pilots
him, and young Laurence Bradbury, one of the
Punch owners, goes as Phil’s secretary —a job to
be jumped at. In Australia, Phil won’t ‘ jaw ’ for
pay, but for charity solely, so he will be pleased at
this publicity, which will encourage every dead-beat
charity organisation “down under” to secure his
services for 20 years ahead. He does not ‘get
thar,’ though, for a year-and-a-half.”

S.A. Register, fairly let himself go on the

this month. Stevens was probably in charge of the

was reported next day :
The speaker remarked upon the enviable opportunities
which had fallen to the lot of Mr. Thomas, including his
admission at a comparatively early age to the fine pro-
cession of the Register proprietors—a glorious company
of able men burning with zeal for the public good, and
who, relative to their numbers, had done more than any
other body to establish the prosperity of South Australia.
He was now enjoying the golden dream of manhood —the
artist of his life was completing the landscape by painting
in the silver stream of love, the pearly bridge of marriage,
and the indispensable central figure of the Queen of Life.
(N.B.—He is about to be married). .. . They all sincerely
wished Mr. Thomas a pleasant voyage, and a speedy
return with music in his life—the songs of the angels of
love as they ascended and descended the golden staircase
which connected the humblest hearth with heaven itself.
None of the Sydney dailies managed to
convey to their readers anything like an ac-
curate impression of the individuality of
Mrs. Jane Smith, of Talune fame. The
popular fancy pictured Jane as a forbidding,
prematurely-aged ogre, built on gridiron lines,
with a rat-trap mouth, steely eyes and cat-like
cautiousness. Instead of which, during the,first
days of the trial there sat bolt upright on the
wooden bench of the dock, a desirable-looking
woman with a Maoriland complexion, nursing her
arms and knee alternately. Now and again she
would lean forward to adjusf a dark skirt
over a pretty foot that seemed to have
strong objections to hiding itself beneath
the drapery. Her eyes were small and dim,
shaded with unusually long eyelashes, and looked
out wearily from beneath almost semi-circular
and well-defined eyebrows. The face tapered
down from a broad forehead to a pointed chin,
and the collective eye of tiie crowd was fixed on
the unusually thin neck. The waist was narrow
and the bust abnormally developed. Whenever
she whispered to her solicitor through the bars her
face lit up with an animation that absolutely
transformed her.

To Contributors. — Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items etc., exact date and exact
■place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus

“ W.H.R.” : Recently came on a bullocky
with his waggon down to tlie bed in a bog and
immovable. While talking to him another
bullock team appeared, the driver of which turned
out to be one of the flashest bullockies in the
north. “What,” said he, “has she dipped a bit
on yer; well, I’ll chuck on a dozen of my hides
and give her a jerk,” He went back, brought up
a dozen of his “hides,” as he termed them, and
hooked them on to the lead of the other team.
Then with his “regulator” (as he termed the
whip) swinging in the air, he commenced opera-
tions with, “Gee back!” “gee back!” until be
got a fair slew on them in the form of a half-
circle; then changing bis tune to “Way, woa
back ! way, woa back ! ” Spot an’ Mary, Star an’
Cherry, Wolf an’ Leopard, Lamb an’ Shepherd,
Bally, Bluey, Stumpy, Nobby, tear and stretch it,
burst or fetch it, boot and lift her, rip and shift
her, pull the llaming quill (pole) out of her.
They didn’t pull the ‘ ‘ quill ” out of her, but
bending to many severe cuts of the regulator (and
Erobably assisted by the numerous adjectives not
ere included), they eventually pulled her on to
firmground. “Took a bit of shifting,” I remarked!
“Oh,” said he, “a man wants a bit of a snap
like that occasionally, just to test the jewellery”
(chains).

“Trotter” : One wet night recently I
camped with a corn-cooky on the Gilbert River
(N.Q.), and won’t easily forget it. A bark humpy
with the roof full of holes ; hard beef, damper,and
post-and-rail tea. A large home-made bed with
four posts, from which hung a thick dirty mos-
quito net. A dog, five hens and a rooster, a goat
and five oats camped here also. The mosquitoes
were so ferocious that I had to get into the bunk.
The cats mewed mournfully and climbed the
bedposts all night long ; the rooster gave an occa-
sional crow ; the dog bad sundry loud disagree-
ments with the goat; and my host snored horribly.
The atmosphere was thick and the heat intense,
yet I daren’t put my head outside the curtain. At
the first sign of dawn I rushed madly for the river
and took a header, clothes and all.
* * * *
Warrego Watchman on a Withered
Land :
Talk about Dante’s “ Inferno,” Dante never wrote any-
thing to compare with the actual everyday horrors of
drought time in Queensland bush. Picture a waterhole
in which are dozens of dead, dying, and recently bogged
cattle. One traveller recently counted 7(i freshly
bogged beasts in a hole but a few yards across,
besides 28 old bodies and skeletons. Ride around
them, and watch the hellish part the crows and
hawks are taking in the tragedy. Here is a beast
stronger than the rest turning his eyeless face towards
the sound of your horse’s feet, and dumbly asking-
to be put out of misery. Further along a cow is still
struggling and fast losing her little strength in a vain
endeavor to loosen her imprisoned feet from the cruel
mud, the while two crows dart and peck with their sharp
beak at hir eyes. You watch The game for a minute,
half fascinated, till a little trickle of blood runs down her
cheek and allows that they are making headway.
A third now joins them, flying to her horn and
refusing to be shaken off by the poor, weak efforts.
Then two of the crows start fighting under the
beast’s very nose, and in a trice the third has seized his op-
portunity, and gouged the eye from the socket, heedless
of tlie plaintive bellowing of the wretched victim, and
while you are looking at her the other crows have secured
tlie remaining optic, and a dozen more are attacking the
bleeding sockets and making vicious darts at the tongue
every time the animal opens her mouth to bellow. Mad
with rage and the pity of it, you feel sick, and
wonder why God made the crow. In desperation
you jump off your horse and hurl sticks and
stones at the evil-looking birds, who merely flutter to a
little distance and caw defiance. Then you put the poor
beast out of her agony, and, as you turn to remount, your
gaze, wandering over the waterhole, is met with other
sights ; other beasts raise other sightless eye-sockets or
dumb mouths, whence the tongues have been torn by
eagle-hawks or crows. You gallop away, half mad, and
wholly wretched with the pity of it, your whole nature
resentful against the terrible condition of things.

A Bargowlahmine man, suffering a re-
covery, went wood-cutting out of the town,
hut returned saying that he had been bitten
on the finger by a brown snake, and had
straightway chopped off the injured mem-
ber. A doctor who was procured could
find no symptoms of snake poisoning,
and when the finger was afterwards
produced, affirmed that the snake was
of the “blue” species and lived on bad
grog.

A dairying item :
An outcry is being raised in Maorilaud over the ap-
proaching scarcity of Ivahikatea, or white pine timber.
Till recently, this wood was considered worthless ; it has
no strength, and starts to rot almost as soon as cut. As
dairying spread, however, it was found that white pine
timber, light, easily-worked, and perfectly odorless, is an
ideal wood for butter-boxes. At present, it is more
sought after than any other ; large quantities are ex-
ported to Australia ; and the barrackers for new railways
always end up by stating that “ the proposed line will tap
large forests of white pine.” The tree, which grows in
swampy country, dies easily ; and is rotten a year after
death, so that there really is some danger of the supply
going short. It is proposed that Govt, should regulate
the rate of cutting, and levy a big export duty on all sent
out of the State. With care, however, there should he
enough of it for the next two generations.

Collecting census information from the
natives proved a tedious job in M.L., and enu-
merators had to sing and spin yarns before estab-
lishing a friendly footing at some pahs. The
Brownies thought they were being enlisted for a
Boer-prodding expedition or that the blue-paper
men were after taxation information. The Age
column was a steep contract. One immeasureably
ancient dame waxed wroth at being set down at
70 ; said she was 200 at least—and she looked it.

In a S.A. up-country court, t’other day»
the black-tracker, wood-chopper, groom, and
general rouseabout attached to the local police-
station was called to give evidence. There was
the usual J.P.’s bobbery about the oath. “Do
you know, Jimmy,” asked the chairman with only
the whites of his eyes showing, “where you will
go when you die if you do not tell the truth ? ”
“ Heaven,” cheerfully replied Jimmy. The chair-
man expostulated and consulted the policeman ;
and in Jjhe end they chanced it and swore him.
Later on, tho chairman, complimenting him on
his evidence, asked him why he said “Heaven”
to the first question. Jimmy, by this time grown
confidential, answered promptly—“ Well, boss,
like this-—if I said ‘ hell ’to magistrate ho say,
‘ Lock that blanky Jimmy up, he too much
blanky swear ! ’”

From Walgett (N.S.W.) Spectator re the
divining-rod
During the late dry spell Mr. J. Niven, of
Spring Kidge, found it necessary to muster his
flock once a day for the purpose of watering
them. Every paddock was absolutely dry ex-
cepting one near the hotel. There was an old
bull (a rogue) among the stock. This animal
became so roguish that finally the stockman
found it necessary to cut a huge fork of a sapling
and fasten it on to the bull's neck in order to
prevent hint from breaking into dropped land.
Next day the bull was not among the mustered .
cattle, lmt one of the hands saw him lying down
on the flat. Day after, he was in the same place.
An examination was made, when it was found
that the great fork had got the bull down and killed
him. The wise man was sent for, and he at once
pronounced judgment. He said that the fork being
green had acted as a divining-rod, and that when
the bull had walked over the right spot the rod had
been powerful enough to turn the animal’s head to
the ground and keep it there. A number of hands
were put on to sink a well on the spot where the
bull’s spirit had departed. At four feet a fabulous
flow of water was struck. The .Cucfgebego?
residents aver that the creek has been a bank
ever since.
“ Steff C.,” concerning the (probably) last
of the great native Fijian functions—the marriage,
at Rewa, in 1900, of Tui Sawan (chief of Rewa)
with Adi Vuikaba, youngest daughter of the late
Ratu Timoce, second son of the renowned King
Cakobau:
It took several days to get through the various cere-
monies. The first day, there took place, in the presence
of some 8000 natives, the official landing and reception of
the bride ; and next day was performed the Taura liga,
or celebration of marriage, at which all dance, sing and
feast, though the couple were not finally united, as other
ceremonies remained. On the third day took place the
Cola Cake , or carrying of the bride to the bridegroom,
after which they were left alone for two days. Then fol-
lowed the Kana Vat a (first meal of the couple as man
and wife), and, next morning, the Teke Vau, or exchange
of property between the couple’s friends. The final day
was one of terrific uproar over the Cola gie Sill, or carry-
ing the princess in a canoe from her house to the river, so
that she might fish to provide for the family that ought
to come.
The European portion of the wedding was performed
by a Wesleyan minister, assisted by two other white revs.,
at the house of a relative of both bride and bridegroom.
Amongst the cooked and uncooked food consumed were
50 turtle. On the second day there was a wall of taro,
yams, and fish-puddings, 3ft. thick, 3ft. high, 500 ft. long,
and ornamented on top at equal distances with 200
roasted pigs. This was a record gathering. Never in the
memory of the oldest Fijian present had so many people
been assembled in one place, or had there been such a
display of property and food. During the last days of the
festivities some magnificent melees were danced, the
grand finale being the dance of the “ Waves of the sea
and goddesses fishing.” This dance only happens when
some royal event occurs, and must have taken the natives
a long time to rehearse. There were some 500 per-
formers of both sexes, their polished skins shining in the
sun like burnished copper. First the men and then the
women pretended to rise from the sea, approaching and
retiring, all in exact time to low musical chanting, as had
been done by their ancestors for hundreds of years.

“Canip”: To “Davie” (8., 16/3/1):
Carpet and green snakes will climb telegraph-
poles. Have seen ’em on the job, but never saw
them monkeying with wires, though I once saw a
green tree-snake at Wide Bay, Q., doing
“Blondin ” on a thickish manila clothes-line.
Among the most-sweated men
in N.S.W. are station-managers.
Dwelling in the big cities all
the year round, some of the
squatter-kings employ men
at from £BO to £lOO a year
to handle stations running
scores of thousands of
sheep. These managers,
besides book - keeping
after hours, are
obliged to slave from
morn till night in heat
and rain, and are ex-
pected to contribute to
local charities and stay
at decent hotels. One
big co. allows its man-
ager only regulation
rations —■ i. e. , flour,
sugar, tea, meat, and
currants! While stay-
ing at one of their
stations, their inspec-
tor one morning at
breakfast asked the boss
to “pass the jam.” The
latter rather indiscreetly replied that he “couldn’t
afford to give any away—it cost fid. a tin.” That
manager is now looking for another job.
* * « *
Vic. schoolmasters in the mallee country
have a busy time, being compelled to act as regis-
trars of births, deaths and marriages, and deputy
electoral registrars, and to assist the departments
in connection with the various matters affecting
law. The teachers are kicking vigorously. One
complains that he cannot- be expected to do his
duty by the pupils, when —as happened on a
recent occasion—a small boy rises in his place in
class, and says: “ Please, teacher, father wants
to know if you could spare time to marry him an’
mother to-morrer afternoon.”
* * « *
In ’93 the French Govt, gave the S. Aus.
Govt, a few date-palms received from Sahara via
Algeria—palms of the Deglet Noire variety, found
to be the most suitable to the Saharan oasis.
Though the palms, which were planted mainly
in the overflows of artesian water in the Far
North, bore a little fruit last year, this season's
is the first real crop; and it seems extremely
likely that the variety’s reputation in Africa will
be borne out also in Australia.

A M.L. farmer claims to have invented a
process to abolish the “sweating” set up during the
process of thawing frozen meat, and which so de-
tracts from its appearance. Hitherto it has been
supposed that this moisture exudes from the car-
case. The inventor states, however, that it is
really the air-moisture deposited like dew on the
surface of the meat. He proposes to shield the
carcase by wrapping it in a waterproof cover
during thawing.

Woman’s Pitiable State.
Falls Down From Weakness.
A pitiable tale, in which a Mrs. Catherine
Saveli is the principal figure, was told to
a reporter. Mrs. Saveli, who lives in Argyle-
street, Bowral, in the course of an interview,
said: —
“I am naturally a hard worker, and when
one has a home to attend to, and the anxiety
of looking after a young family, there is not
much time for rest. Years ago, 1 over-taxed
my strength, with the result that my nerves
gradually became irritated and unstrung, and
the sharp, burning pains of neuralgia came in my
head. The gnawing sensation' was so agonising
that it made me light-headed, and I often fell
down from weakness, due to the nervous ten-
sion. Little hills of trouble appeared to be
great mountains, and everything was an
annoyance. Liniments, fomentations and em-
brocations did not even soothe the pain. At
last several well-known people advised me to
use Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale people. I
did so, and one box improved me wonderfully.
I continued taking them, and my nerves be-
came toned up; the neuralgia left me, and I
grew quite strong and bright. Five boxes
completely cured me.”
When the blood is impoverished, the nerves
suffer, then come the cold and the pains of
neuralgia. Those subject to neuralgia, sciatica,
rheumatism and bronchitis, should now take a
few boxes of Dr. Williams’ pink pills; by this
means they will enrich their blood and tone up
their nerves, thus avoiding all winter ailments.
Dr. Williams’ pink pills are sold by chemists
and storekeepers, and by the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and
nine per box, six boxes fifteen and three, post
free. “How to Cure Neuralgia.” Special
directions free on application.*

A Chinaman keeps a livery-stable at a Yic.
town 13 miles inland, but “business allee same
lotten ” lately. There came, however, a storm on
the coast. Whereupon the wily heathen hired a
man to spread a report that a whale was stranded
on the beach. Within two hours he had not a
hoof or a wheel disengaged, and spent the after-
noon offering grateful burnt (paper) sacrifices to
his Joss.
* • * *
A traveller recounts to The Bulletin
some very human-natural experiences
Did a bit of life - insuring at a one - pub
N.S.W. town. First man I met after break-
fast was a carrier who wanted me to have a
drink, and buy his dog—best dog in the world,
only a beggar to suck eggs. Asked him to
insure his life, and spent half - an - hour ex-
plaining the advantages of 1. a. lie didn’t
think it was a bad thing, but didn’t know
about passing the doctor, as he suffered from
“swelled” (varicose) veins, and “had a
cancer coming on his tongue.” Besides, he
didn’t know as it would be quite safe, ’cause
if the old woman got to know his life was
worth two hundred quid, she’d go for the
money straight. The publican’s wife was
very anxious that I should get Bill (hubby)
for’ £5OO or so, but William “ wasn’t takin’
any.” He thought the thing was right
enough, but had the same final objection to it
as his friend, the carrier. Later on, a half-
drunken cockie came along, and I got him for
£2OO. My doctor soon put him through, and
he shouted for all hands and seemed as
pleased as a newly-made J.P. He went up
the street; and, about an hour afterwards,
returned with a woman at his heels. Left
her outside, and came in and asked me to ex-
plain to the old woman, that, in case of death
before 70, he got nothing. You know, said
he, a man has to be mighty careful what
he is at. I agreed. Then he brought the
lady in. We had more drink, and I explained
that if he died before 70 he got nothing.
“ Hear that, Maria ?” he said. The wife, who
had at first seemed highly delighted, suddenly
became dissatisfied, and wanted him to with-
draw, but I pointed out that it couldn t be
done. That was the only life I got in the
place; and before I left I heard that it had
been treated for some heart-complaint, and
was subject to fits.

“Ita": Have several times noticed that
when a cultivation paddock is opened up in timber
country the younger trees on the. border of the
clearing in time develop a decided “lean”
towards the ploughed ground. Can any reader
explain ? [Probably because the roots naturally
make for the cleared ground, where they get more
nourishment; and the tree-tails follow the real
tree-animal, the root, as a dog’s tail follows the
dog.]

NOTICE.
At the Molten Hotel in Sulphuric Glade,
Where the temperature’s nine-ninety-eight in the shade,
There’s a thrice-heated chamber dug out with a scraper
For the fellow who writes on both sides of the paper
Where the billows of brimstone delightfully roar,
And the geysers their seething red cataracts pour,
There’s a blistering bath ill a cauldron of flame
For the chump who neglected to sign his name.
—lnland Printer.

Editor Bulletin. —Dear Sir, —I take the liberty of in-

Maryborough (Queensland)- Nora M ‘Kay—who began her
studies with me here, at the Royal Conservatory, three
vears ago ; and carried off the highest honors. The
first prize with grand distinction was given to her by
the jury of seven of the best violinists of Europe, \saye
being one of them. She is about to begin a tour in Aus-
tralia, and I am convinced that she will make a sensation,
being a wonder, both with regard to tone, technique, and
style. She has a perfect how-arm, and a magnificent
position. Hoping you will find space in your widely-read
paper for the picture enclosed, believe me, very truly
yours, Ovide Musin.
A sign of the times is King O’Malley’s
election by Tasmania to Federal honors. “The
King ” arrived in Adelaide from Canada some
years ago, and served two terms in the local
Assembly. He lias a wild and whirling style of
eloquence, like that of Smiler Hales, with Ameri-
can exuberance added, and he became the delight
of all satirists and paragraphists. To work up a
pantomime with local allusions meant to drag in
King O’Malley’s name as often as possible, and it
was always sure of a laugh. When he was
defeated by Mayor Tucker, after a desperate
fight which split the constituency into _ two
hostile camps, and broke up many local friend-
ships of long standing, K.O’M. moved on
to Tasmania. There he was instrumental in add-
ing the names of some 3000 West Coast miners
and others to the electoral roll, meanwhile talking
advanced democracy and calling ever) 7 toil-stained
worker “ brother.” He petrified the l assies with
his cyclonic eloquence and wildly-humorous abuse
of the ancient fossils of the Flyspeck. He posed
(he’s good at that) as a modern Peter the Hermit,
specially ordained to succour the dispossessed,
and blast and blither the Fatman unto seventy
times seven. Exceedingly shrewd, but not
over-logical in argument, quick at repartee,
not too refined in expression, but withal
clever enough to impress electors with his
cleverness, O’Malley scored. And no wonder. Tas-
mania was rotten-ripe for change, and the man
with the wide-brimmed hat, blue eyes, and brown
beard, who called every man “brother,” and
boasted that he could fight and beat all the news-
papers in the Island, was the best Cromwell who
appeared in the lists. A man of awful skite, cer-
SENATOR KING O'MALLEY (Tas.).
Photo, by T. Channon, Strahan, Tasmania.
tainly, but as bis big heart and active brain easily
outweigh the dross, this paper expects useful
Federal legislation from King O’Malley. A
photo of the Senator, beaming affably through
his spectacles, appears in this issue.
Bunny has a poor time of it now—quoted
in the London market as,flat at he is bundled
out of existence in Victoria with the assistance of
M‘Lean Bros, and Bigg, Ltd., the big iron-
mongers in Melbourne. That firm supplies guns,
ammunition and traps, and, in cases of dire need,
can turn on a full blast of destruction with Dixon
Gould’s Vacuum Destroyer. This machine is the
Jim Dandy of its kind, is beautifully made,
certain in operation as a Waterbury watch, and
counts its victims by thousands. Poor bunny !*

RECENT ATTACKS ON THE
PRESS.
“In America Freetrade had been responsible for the tyranny of
the Tammany Ring. In Australia it had been responsible -instead
for the tyranny of the press, and any scoundrel supported by the
press could get into Parliament.”— The O’Sullivan, at Manly.
“ Speaking of corrupt literature, he (Cardinal Moran) the previ-
ous day had met a gentleman from West Australia who said that
the West Australian papers boasted that they had received their
information and their press wires from the office of the Daily
Telegraph in Sydney.” (“Hear, hear.”) “If such be true,” com
'tinned the Cardinal—“ I don’t guarantee its truth.”

A RATIONAL REFORM IN TEACHING
FRENCH.
Teachers must bear in mind that it is a gross mistake,
as well as a mere waste of time and labor, to land pupils
on the grammatical and literary stages of the study before
the first stage (Phonetic and Mechanical) has thoroughly
been mastered. (Dr. Henry Sweet, Oxford.)
Therefore use PERIER’S FRENCH METHOD (Is. 6d.)
and its PHONETIC COMPANION (Is. 3d.). All book-
sellers. Copies are sent, post free, by sending postage
stamps to A. C. GAERTNER, Sole Agent,
32 Norwich Chambers, Hunter-street, Sydney.

The public want coupons. When in Vic-
toria an attempt to introduce legislation against
the system of the Co-Operative Coupon Co., Ltd.
was made, 50,000 members of the public petitioned
both Houses of Parliament in support of the
company’s system. These petitions aggregated
over one mile long. 1500 traders issuing the
company’s coupons also signed petitions in favor
of the system.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Judging by the number of land-sales
announced in Brisbane it looks as if the financial
institutions smell the advent of a land tax.
Sydney D.T.’s horror at the political pro-
fligacy of Lyne’s appointment of Fegan is badly
off-set by Lyne’s appointment of D.T.-President
Randall Carey. '
During the last 10 years the population of
Sydney has increased by 102,109; Adelaide,
28,842; and Melbourne, 3060. Hobart has de-
creased by 329.
Brisbane Chamber of Commerce is appeal-
ing (and apparently successfully) to the Mel-
bourne and Adelaide Chambers to lend a hand
and save the beloved Kanaka.
Lately a German steamer deposited 114
passengers at Fremantle (W.A.)—BB Italians,
8 Germans and Belgians, 6 Spaniards, 8 English-
men and 4 Irishmen. The Anglo-Saxon race is
getting on well in these parts !
P. O. Fysh takes Neil Lewis’s place as Hon.
Member of the Barton Cabinet. Barton, Deakin,
Kingston, Dickson, and he were the five delegates
to England on behalf of the Constitution, and the
whole five have been included in the first Govt,
under it. ,
Brisbane is the black Conservative blot
on the face of Democratic Queensland. While
North, West and Central went Democratic at the
secent Federal elections, Brisbane stuck to its be-
loved Fat Men like glue. Municipally, that town
is corrupt to the core, and Private Enterprise
grows fat on the city’s negligence. Nationally,
Brisbane was the bitterest foe of Federation m
Australia. Provincially, Brisbane is the coddler
of Kanakas, the harbinger of Asiatics, the sheet-
anchor of the Northern boodlers.
“JAPAN URGES CHINA TO RESTORE LAW AND ORDER
in manchuria."
Brisbane Courier most emphatically de-
mounces the lack of discipline that led to the Q.
Govt, party’s defeat during the late Federal elec-
tions. The League of which the Courier was
the acknowledged organ drew up a so-called
“ticket.” This ticket the Courier promptly re-
viewed and promptly denounced. And now that
organ bellows for “ discipline ! ”
It is peculiar that the German voters of
S.A., and especially where they are segregated in
little Teutonic townships, always follow the
Defence League (Tory) ticket, and almost in-
variably vote solid. Hahndorf, for instance,
put into the box 150 votes for one candidate and
only two or three for the other, though the
latter was the better man, and won in the whole
district.
S. A. Legislature has yielded to the clamour
of the people for reductions in view of the coming
of Jederation. It has abolished the salary of the
1 arliamentary Librarian. Now it will wipe
its perspiring brow and cease from its labors in
the cause of economy.
The salaries of the Governor-General and
the State Governors of Australia amount, taken
together, to over £40,000 a year. Compare
these figures: Australian Governor-General,
£10,000; President of the U.S., £10,000; Presi-
dent of France, £24*000 ; President of Switzer-
land, £740.
The S.A. Cabinet will not be fixed up
until after the York celebration in Melbourne.
Govt, whip Brooker, Ishmael Grainger, ex-. Educ-
ation Minister Butler, and the pragmatical Peake
are spoken of for the two vacancies, and there is
also a suggestion of coalition with the idea of
isolating the Labor party.
The Sydney dailies pooh-pooh the idea
that the faults of Lyne in office should not be
counted against Premier See. The See Ministry
is only the Lyne one continued. Then as the Lee
party is only the Reid party continued, the Neild
grant and other little things should be put down
to the debit of Opposition-leader Lee.
At Wyacca, on S.A. West Coast, during
the Federal elections, 14 persons voted. They all
met together previously, and both for Senate and
Representatives they voted for exactly the same
men—so that six in the first case and seven in the
second had 14 votes while none of the rest received
any. At another polling-place in same con-
stituency (Flinders) the only two votes cast were
informal.
The Bulletin presumes that the insolent
bleeding (by an obsequious gang of departmental
officials) of the N.S. W. Public-school teachers for
money to supply medals to departmental officials
who volunteered for S’Africa, does not take place
without the knowledge and concurrence of
Minister Perry. Also, this paper trusts that
Duke George has a bauble in his bag for Perry,
also one for “F. Bridges.”
Members of the Federal Cabinet have pub-
licly stated that they are confident of a good
working majority. But several of the new and
hitherto-unknown members have not authori-
tatively stated how they intend to sit. Braddon
is supposed to vote as a Reidite —which is singular,
as he is the author of the famous “ Blot ” which
Reid, Sydney D.T. and other Freetrade props
have never ceased denouncing as a death-blow to
the idea of Freetrade.
“J. F. Cullen”
—we seem to have
heard the name before
somewhere —writes to
the Sydney papers
proposing that the
Federal Parliament
Blia.ll amend the Con-
stitution (which it
can’t do, by the way)
to the extent of re-
moving the 100-iniles-
from-Sydney-clause, so
that the Federal city
can be put right against
Cullen’s back-door at
North Sydney. It ap-
parently doesn’t occur
to Cullen that it is
logically impossible to
remove the prohibition
against Sydney being
the capital without
also removing the pro-
hibition against Mel-
bourne, Brisbane, and
other places. And then
the Federal city might
depart out of N.S.W.
altogether. Better
leave well alone.
State Premier See
of N.S.W. proposes to
dissolve Parliament
and go to the country
on a programme
which includes - a re-
duction in the member-
ship of the Assembly,
an enlarged system of
Local Govt., female
suffrage, an improved
Factories Act, arbitra-
tion in industrial dis-
putes, the placing of
the Civil Service and
Police Superannuation
Funds on a solvent
basis, and (though
these are mentioned
rather vaguely) a
North Shore bridge
and a city railway. But Premier See is none
too strong or too definite about a lot of these
things. And there is no sign or promise of the
democratic reform which over-shadows all others
—that the See Government will borrow no more
money for unproductive works and will cease to
heap up ruinous burdens and prospective bank-
ruptcy for the coming generations. That one item
would be worth all the rest 12 times over,
N.S.W. Freetrade politicians Lee and
Ashton both cursed Premier See in last Monday’s
Sydney D.T. because, in his manifesto, he sticks
to the questions which' the State Parliament has
power to deal with, and doesn’t mumble over the
dry bones of the fiscal question, which concerns
the State Parliament just as much as do the rings 1
of Saturn.
THE SILVER LINING.
MRS. SILVERSTEIN: “ Ah, Aby, you’re drunk!”
MR. S.: “’S’hall ri’ m’dear. It’s only on three-
penny long beers.”
The C.M.G. and D.S.O. pest that has
just descended on Australia shows that the
Britisher still believes he can catch the Australian
with forty-third-rate titles in the same way as
he has been catching the “poor heathen ” of the
Pacific Islands with shoddy beads and buttons.
This will continue as long as Australians remain
on their stomachs before the superior British in-
dividual, and accept the shop-stained baubles.
S.A. National Defence League pretends
now that it gave its supporters latitude to vote for
whomsoever they pleased outside the nominees of
the executive, but at the German township of
Moculta (where the League runs the show)
Kingston, Symon and Bonython received but one
vote each, while Holder got but two. The League
candidates—of whom one was a reformed Single
Taxer and another a renegade Labor member-
received 85 votes each.
The Barton Cabinet’s determination to
appoint a Sergeant-at-Arms and an Usher of the
Black Rod does not presage well for the economy
or Democratic simplicity of the new Common-
wealth. Both these officers will be highly paid,
while their duties are either ridiculous or trivial.
It is true there is a Sergeant-at Arms in the U.S.
Parliament, but he is paymaster of the members
and general clerk besides. The Usher of the
Black Rod has no existence there.
“Joey” Carruthers,- in his Rockdale
(N.S.W.) impeachment of the See Govt. :
Thanks to the Freetrade policy of the previous (Reid)
Govt, there was a flowing Treasury, but the flow had
stopped now.
And as N.S.W. is still run on the Freetrade policy
of the Reid Govt., the fair inference seems to be
that it is Freetrade which stopped the flow.
Anyhow, if there was a period of plenty and a
period of scarcity under the Reid tariff, that
tariff can’t get credit for the one without being
debited with the other also.
J. H. Carruthers’ late exclupatory speech
only puts him in a still tighter place. In it he
contends that See is not and cannot be considered
constitutionally Premier of N.S.W. till the coun-
try or Parliament lias ratified his appointment.
Yet in another part of his speech he admits to
having negotiated with Premier See over the
formation of a coalition Government, asserting
that he personally considered such a coalition
advisable. But if See were not constitutionally
Premier why did Carruthers negotiate to join his
unconstitutional Government ?
Q. Minister for Works Leahy announced
mournfully a few days ago that the Q. Treasury
was about half a million to the bad this
year ; that the Govt, had no railway policy to
offer, inasmuch as it would be unable to float a
new loan ; that it would be difficult to raise the
interest to meet existing indebtedness. But Philp
and co. have no ideas on the subject of raising
extra revenue or cutting down expenditure to
meet the shortage, and their policy of leaning up
against a tree and hoping for the best is bringing
the Labor-party nearer to power every day.
Dear Bulletin. —The Fede'ral capital site
(B. 27/4/’01) is correct as regards Bombala v.
Orange, but omits to mention other aspirants—
presumably because Mr. Oliver’s recommendation
of three sites carried a certain amount of finality
with it. A practically unlimited water supply,
with great pressure, can be obtained for “Carcoar-
Garland,” at about the same altitude as Bombala,
and 40 miles nearer Melbourne than is Orange (via
Blayney-llarden loop-line). Annual rainfall 39in.
and conservation would cost less than at Bombala,
as the reservoirs would be quite close to the City
site. —J.B.
Rumor and.ex-Premier Lyne and Varney
Parkes have had a long fight with public confid-
ence, and Rumour comes out (prima-facie) ahead.
When Varney retired from Parliament Rumor
pointed to the Chairmanship of Sydney Rocks
Resumption Board. Varney charged Rumor with
lying. Rumor said Lyne promised the billet to
.get rid of a dangerous Freetrade opponent. Lyne
said Rumor lied. Months passed with repeated
charges and counter-charges of lying. Finally
the Board was formed and Varney was a member.
“I told you so,” cried Rumor. “But another
man is Chairman,” cried Lyne. “Yes,.” said
Rumor, “the other man is put up to be knocked
down.” In two weeks Varney is gazetted Chair-
man.'

has departed from their fur-

Though Lee, of Tenterfield, is the elected
leader of the Opposition in N.S.W. State Parlia-
ment, Carruthers poses as the man who runs the
show, and he offers an Opposition policy and heaves
the official Opposition brick at the Government
just like a real leader. Judging by his indictment
the point on which the See Ministry is most likely
to come down with a loud slam is its annexation,
as current revenue, of the rents of the Sydney
resumed aVoa, while making no allowance for
interest on the purchase money. And on that
point it can hardly come down with a louder slam
than it deserves.
Tasmania is the first State to get out a
rough estimate of its population. The figures are
171,006 against 146,0G7 in 1891—an increase of
24,399, equal to 17 per cent. The total is 11,442
less than the estimate. Hobart has actually
diminished; it has 24,576 people as compared
with 24,905 ten years ago, a decline of 329.
Meanwhile its hated rival, Launceston, has in-
creased from 17,208 to 18,043, and on the strength
of this the animosity between north and south
(the Launceston press generally calls Hobart the
“ capital ” in inverted commas as an expression of
scorn) will be more violent than ever.
Pastor Jefferis, of Adelaide (who, The
Bulletin regrets to notice, has sustained a great
blow by the death of his son, Dr. Eddington
Jefferis) about 20 years ago delivered an address
(both in Adelaide and Sydney) on Federation.
A recent perusal of it in pamphlet form shows
that the Pastor was much more correct in his ideas
as to the ultimate shape to be assumed by Federa-
tion than was politician Parkes. The latter at
that time favored Unification, and was not too
keen on the Crown question. In the light of
present events, the Congregational clergyman’s
lecture must be regarded as a remarkably accurate
forecast.
Westralia recently took off its Protective
meat duties, and sacrificed some small revenue in
the process, with a view to making its daily chop
and steak cheaper, and it is now struck by the
painful fact that its daily chop and steak remain
at the same price. The importation of cattle on a
large enough scale to make the business pay over
the great sea or land distances which have to be
traversed in Westralia’s case, is not a business
that men of small means can go.into. And
the men of large means, who run the business,
simply pocket the extra profit which the abolition
of the duties gives them, and smile a smile large
enough to take in a tombstone.
“Joey” Carruthers, who is bidding for
the position of leader of N.S.W. Opposition,
wailts to “lift the N.S.W. municipalities out of
their present state of bankruptcy ” —not by mak-
ing them levy enough rates to cover their expendi-
ture, but by giving them large, permanent endow-
ments out of the public revenue. And as Joseph
doesn’t propose any extra taxation to cover these
endowments, and asthe revenue only staggersalong
now by charging £BOO,OOO a year of its current
liabilities to loans, it may be presumed that
Joseph intends borrowing a little extra money to
go on with. Joseph will probably get in. A
man with a scheme for paying all the municipal
debts at nobody’s expense appeals to the inmost
soul of this country.
Cutting from London Times (weekly
edition) Parliamentary report, Ist March :
THE PAY OF COLONIAL TROOPS.
Captain Lee (Hants, Fareham) asked the Secretary of
State for the Colonies if he could state whether the rate
of pay—namely, ss. per diem, now drawn by colonial
troops in South Africa, was less than the current rate of
wages for the lowest class of white labor in such colonies.
Mr. Chamberlain.—Five shillings a day is undoubtedly
less than the average rate of daily wages for unskilled
white labor in the colonies from which these troops have
beer. sent.
Thirty-five shillings a week and keep less than the
average wage of white Australian unskilled labor !
What a lovely romance! The Bulletin here
says, in the presence of some millions of people
who know all about the subject, that a pound a
week with keep is a high rate in Australia. What
do the Labor papers say ?


Read what VITADATIO is Doing.
Cancerous Growth.
PRONOUNCED INCURABLE.
Turned out of Prince Alfred Hospital,
Sydney.
Given a Month at Longest to Live by the Doctors.
STILL ALIVE THROUGH
VITADATIO
Read What Mr . Adams writes.
WRITE TO HER YOURSELF.
Helensburg Railway Station, N.S.W.,
19th July, 1900.
Mr. S. A. Palmer.
Dear Sir,—About eighteen months ago I w*as turned
out of the Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney, as incurable,
suffering from a cancerous growth attached to my spine,
and I was told that I could not live longer than a month
at the outside. The agony was so great that I could only
lie on my faoe and hands, and I wished death to come
and release me from the misery. I could do nothing
whatever for myself and could not stand on my feet. I
felt that I must have further advice, and interviewed the
Doctors at the Women’s Hospital, Crown-street, Surry
Hills. I was told there that nothing in this world could
be done for me. I did call there at the end of the month
and after examination I was told that it only was a matter
of time. 1 also interviewed a leading Doctor in Penrith,
and he told me a second growth had formed, and that
nothing could be done, and that I would soon know the
worst. I had been advised to try VITADATIO, which I
am glad to say I did, and have continued with it up to
the present time. After taking it for some time I
improved, and pieces of white skin came away from me.
I began to gain in weight and am now' able to go about
without any trouble, and do all my work. I have every
reason to believe that, by continuing the medicine, I will
be soon as well as ever I was. I will be pleased to answer
any questions about mv case.
HANNAH ADAMS.
For further particulars,
s. A. PALMER ,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

PURSUANT to an Order of the Court of
Chancery of the County Palatine of Lan-
caster Manchester District made in the matter of
the Estate of Frances Raingill deceased and in an
action Beck v. Bankes 100 L B No. 62 the person
claiming to be JOHN HARDY otherwise John
Mayo Hardy (cousin of the said testatrix Frances
Raingill) or to be a child or the legal personal
representative of a child of the said John Hardy
living at - the death of the Testatrix’s brother
Thomas Raingill (who died on the 9th day of
May 1899) are on or before the Bth DAY OF
JULY 1901 to COme in and prove their claims at)
the Chambers of the Registrar of the Manchester
District of the said Court situate at Duchy
Chambers 4 Clarence Street in the City of
Manchester England or in default thereof they
will be peremptorily excluded from the benefit of
the said Order. Thursday the 18th day of July
1901 at 11 o’clock in the forenoon at the said
Chambers is appointed for hearing and adjudi-
cating upon the said claim.
The said John Hardy is believed to have lived
in Sydney during the years 1880 to 1895 and is
said to have died in the Rookwood Asylum New
South Wales in the year 1895. Any person
having information as to the whereabouts of the
said John Hardy (if living) or of any child or
children of the said John Hardy or who is able to
give any information as to any of such persons is
requested to communicate with Messrs. Chapman
Roberts & Beck Solicitors 32 Fountain Street
Manchester England.
Dated the 28th day of March 1901.
HUBERT WINSTANLEY,
Registrar.
TO THE MEM3ER3~OFf
THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY.
Ladies and Gentlemen, —
Permit me to intimate that I am one of your Directors
who, in accordance with the By-laws, retire on this occa-
sion, and, being Eligible for RE-ELECTION, I am again a
candidate, and have the honor of respectfully soliciting
your vote and influence at the forthcoming election.
My interests in the Society are large, and I think I may
claim that my long experience in connection with the
commercial and producing interests of the Colonies has
not been without benefit to the Institution.
I fully recognise the importance and responsibility
attaching to the position of a Director, and should you
therefore again do me the honor of returning me, my
earnest efforts shall, as heretofore, be given to promote
the welfare of our Society.
Yours faithfully,
JOSEPH ABBOTT
(GOLDSBROUGH, MORT & CO., LTD.).
Sydney, 10th April, 1901.

The Secret History
. . or TIIB t .

- CASE.
DH.O’Hflßan’S UHIDICITIOI
AND HOW IT WAS BROUGHT ABOUT.
The Cause Celebre of the
New Century.
The Church Militant and
the Church Triumphant.
IT may be safely said that the people of Austra-
lia—Catholic and Protestant, Jew and Gentile
—have seldom, if ever, before taken so much
interest in any trial as they did in the Coning-
ham Case. And it may be added that a verdict
never gave greater satisfaction to all decent citi-
zens, irrespective of creed, throughout the Com-
monwealth than that which, at the end of the
Second Trial, sent Dr. O’Haran out of the Courts
of Law with a stainless reputation, and a soul
made stronger- by the suspense and suffering of
many weary months. The true story of this
example of modern martyrdom should be read by
not only every Catholic but by every lover of
Justice in the land. It will be found to be full of
surprising incidents, of plots and counterplots,
curious episodes and cunning intrigues.
The Secret History of the Coningham
Case
Will contain all these, and will be illustrated with
PHOTOGRAPHIC FACSIMILES of the Docu-
ments quoted in the Case, and many others that
did not appear in Court, and have never before
been published. The sole right to publish these
rests with the Proprietor of this Work.

A Sydney undertaking firm advertises that
it possesses “the finest plant in the colonies.”
Intentional waggery, or merely unconscious
humor ?
“F.C.”: To-day I ran against the first
chapter of a future divorce-case. Was yarning to
my tobacconist when entered a lady and little
girl. She wanted a cigarette-holder, “ something
nice.” He showed several, specially recommend-
ing an amber-and-meerschaum silver-mounted
affair “ No-o. He—he likes them all amber.
Have you any ?” “I’m afraid not. But meer-
schaum makes the best smoke. Doesn t your hus-
band ever smoke a pipe ? ” Then the enfant ter-
rible : “ That isn’t for Dada, mister ; it s for our
lodger.” The shopman never moved a muscle.
A steam-roller in Sydney streets is a sight
to see. A man ahead with a red flag to warn
traffic—people crowding to see—cab-horses shying
and rearing—spavined bus-horses snorting—even
the trams appearing a trifle startled at the strange
visitant. Present writer recently had an exciting
steam-roller experience. He was coming down
William-street very late at night in a hansom
drawn by an old steeplechase-horse —it was the
ancient’s second day in harness—when the moke,
catching sight of the roller, became quite frantic
and bolted at a gallop right down one of the
highest hills in Sydney, never halting till he
arrived opposite the Coroner’s Court. But he
kept his feet all the time, and what was more, the
driver stuck to his seat.

Treason from Sydney Truth’s London
correspondent, Maude Wheeler :
Red-haired Florence Schmidt’s limitations have already
found her out. She can sing nothing but florid music, as
she has about as much sympathy or soul as a mummy.
. . . The duke of York is a silly-looking coot, and it is
whispered under the breath that he is a little bit balmy.
The Duchess of York is rather stout, and always wears
her mouth wide open, as if she were trying to catch flies.
Perhaps it was worth while settling
whether saccharine in lemonade is an adulterant
or not; but the abortive prosecution of Tooth and
Co. might teach Sydney health inspectors to turn
their attention to the obvious and really damaging
adulterants. Why, there are probably not half-
a-dozen shops in Sydney where you can buy a
decent pound of coffee, free of chicory and burnt
peasemeal. And as for tea !
A few days will find the mammoth York
carnival, in Melb., at its flood. And we must re-
mind sensible citizens who would emerge from the
rumpus in good order and condition, to consider the
quality of their liquid refreshment, D.C.L.—safe.
No stranger in Melbourne need get lost, stolen or
strayed, if he puts his faith in D.C.L, No reason
why his head shouldn't fit his hat after a night
of D.C.L., with an occasional squeeze of lemon in
it. The Wysky of the Wyse.*
Cabled that there has been a great row in
Edinburgh over a speech delivered by Merriman,
of S’Africa, in which he urged that the South
African States should be allowed a Government on
the Australian model. Jingo cable states that
“ Merriman’s remarks to an audience of 2500 were
interrupted by angry shouts of dissent. The pro-
Boer party had no fewer than 500 hired roughs in
attendance, and they were busy from the start.
Many thousands of excited anti-Boers thronged
the streets in front of the building where the
Afrikander Bond delegate was speaking. The
Scotsman remarks that such brutal scenes have
never before been witnessed at an Edinburgh
meeting.” The trouble was probably the old one :
a dispute as to the right of persons who had
forced themselves into a meeting to which admis-
sion was by ticket, to interfere with the proceed-
ings.
The so-called Sydney “Citizens’ Com-
mittee ” of self-appointed busybodies, whereof J.
D. Slush is mouthpiece, complains that N.S.W.
mine-proprietors won’t forward gratis gold to
cover the Jookoyork’s Grease-Album. Why on
earth should they ? What profit is a mine-
proprietary going to get by handing over
yellow-metal to be presented by a Sydney
Slush-Committee? The Slush-Committee will
get the grimaces, and thanks, and loyal kudos
at the miners’ expense. Let the Slushers
put their hands in their own pockets; if
the Jook wants gold specimens, let him go and
collect them on the mines. Apparently the only
gold forwarded so far is a dredging-ounce from C.
L. Garland, valued at £4—and the gentle Charles
took good care to get a £4-advt. out of it, as
he had a right to. If any other mine-
representatives think of following Garland’s
example, The Bulletin recommends them to
consider the fate of all the loyal trophies which
Alfred Edinburgh collected in Australia, and for
the “ sentiment ” of which he cared so little that
they were sold by auction for the dross they
fetched. George York is a chip of the same block,
for he not long ago sold to one of the Rothschilds
his stamp-collection, made by Government influ-
ence, at a figure reported to be colossal.
Politics is an amusing game. The N.S.W.
Ereetrade Party has just found out it blundered
badly when it fussed over the Fegan scandal; be-
cause if the Fegan appointment is not confirmed
it won’t have any stick to beat the Govt, with, and
if the Fegan appointment—againfet all probability
is confirmed, it will have a very nice, useful
stick indeed. So now the Freetrade game is to
stop protesting, and try and get Fegan the job.
Sydney Telegraph points out that the Barton
Cabinet can’t refuse to endorse Minister Lyne’s
independent action—as if the very essence of
Cabinet action was not the surrender of individual
authority. Sydney Evening News goes farther and
says that if 1 egan isn’t confirmed in office, Barton
and Lyne will have to resign. And George
Reid rolls his eyes at a banquet, and
says that Barton and Lyne are not the
clean Freetrade potato, but he’s quite sure
they will never go back on a pal. All which quite-
disinterested advice from the Opposition shows
how badly they want Fegan appointed—to give
them something to shriek about. But that cock
won t fight now ; they should have produced the
bird before. They should have let the Fegan
appointment go unfussed-over, and then, when he
was really appointed, shrieked for Purity in
Politics. Somehow, The Bulletin fancies the
1 reetrade dog is too old to learn new tricks as he
ought to.
The
Original
Agents:
J. Barre Johnston & Co.,
Sydney.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Judged by Royal Artillery’s four-lengths defeat
.of Clean Sweep in S.A.J.C,. Leger (Saturday), it looks as
if the Mulb. Cup winner has gone completely off—and
small wonder, seeing what he has been asked to do. The
nag’s showing at A. J.C. Autumn meet distinctly indi-
cated loss oi form, and should have warned those behind
him, but Scobie evidently thought differently—otherwise
that Adelaide trip would never have been taken. Maybe,
Royal Artillery is quite up to Clean Sweep’s standard ; at
any rate his breeding is not one whit inferior, and he has
been performing well lately. Besides other events he got
away with Onkaparinga Cup (8.5 up), and just before
meeting Clean Sweep and Tinfoil in the Leger had the
colors up for Goodwood Handicap. The Zalinski colt
started red-hot, carrying £IOCO of £1298 invested.
* * » *
“Old ’un ”: Defeat of Clean Sweep recalls a
somewhat analogous case. Pride of the Hills survived
Melbourne and Sydney meetings, but when he returned
.to Adelaide, Filibuster and Moonlight finished ahead of
him in the St. Leger of ’77. Even a horse has a limit.
*•• . •
Bill of Portland—Fishwife colt, Finland (9st.),
was much in the humour on Saturday : he won S.A.J.C.
Goodwood Handicap (6 fur.), cantering from a big field
ln Vi 6 * ’ kut for the re-handicapping provision
would look “ good goods ” for Adelaide Cup this Wed-
nesday ; it might still fall to him. Public picked Q. nag,
Fortune Teller, on his running in Newmarket Handicap,
and backed him consistently, but he could get no nearer
than sixth. Finland has a Maribyrnong Plate and
V. Leger among his fine list of three-year-old wins.
Machine showed £3421 for this event, by far the biggest
amount on any race that day.
* * » *
Peculiar that winners of two big things (Good-
wood Handicap and St. Leger Stakes) on opening day
(Sat.) of S.A.J.C. Autumn Meeting paid £4 55., while
money handed out over the third important race (S.A.
Stakes) was but 3s. more. Amount put through the tote
that day wa3 £11,671 —slightly in excess of investments
for same day last year.
* * * •
La Carabine made hacks of Gunga Din, Tas-
man, Dirk Hammerhand and Flintlock in S.A.J.C. Elder
Stakes (Im. lfur.), a distance she isn’t expected to shine
at. £1332 of £1953 speculated was on the winner, who
paid £1 7s.
* * * *
S.A.J.C. meet, now in progress, calls to mind
that the first Goodwood H’cap was decided 20 years hack,
when 0.0.0 won, and brought £836 16s. to the only man
who “ toted ” him. Suppression of the machine and
stringent provisions of the Betting Act sent the races
away to Melbourne in 'B5, and thence till ’B9, no Good-
wood was run. Ranfurly (.9.4 up) won last year, and was
so little fancied that the tote paid out £l6l 13s.
* * • *
Adelaide Cup has been run on three dif-
ferent courses in the Wheat State, and when Lord
Wilton won in ’B5, Flemington (Vic.) was the con-
vincing place. S.A. racing flourished in the early ’7o’s,
and wealthy men wagered heavily. “Joe” Thompson
had a £15,000 book on the Oup when Nimblefoot started
a red-hot 6 to 4 “on ” favorite in ’72 and got beaten. The
leviathan gathered in every cent of his winnings, much
of it laid in hundreds.
*. * *
Cabled that Australian-bred Australian Star
(7.10) scored in City and Suburban. Handicap at ’Ome on
Wednesday last week, winning by 6 lengths, which means
that the thing was a gift. Alveseot (third) and the
winner were equal favorites at 2to 2. The Star’s victory
was to an extent" indicated by his previous run-away
triumph in London Oup. Australian nags have had a
mortgage Ori the- City and Suburban during late years.
Said that M.L. sport, Mr. Spencer Gollan (owner of Aus-
tralian Star), wants his top-notcher’s full brother, Mr.
Macken s Australian Colors. The price asked is 1200 gs.
* « * *
Not all the wealth of Kalgoorlje goes Bullwards,
after all. Two race meetings.were held there within a
couple of months, with 1000'sovs. prize" money at the first,
and 2750 sdvs. at the secorid : including a thousand sovs.
Goldfields’Cup.
_ Kalgoorlie Cup, to be run in Aug., will be the
richest prize yet offered in W.A. —1000 sovs., and trophy
worth another £lOO.
* * » *
W. H. Jordan, a Sloan-imitating hackbloeks
jock., has the creditable record of 54 wins, 36 seconds and
17 thirds oul of 146 mounts. At recent Wangaratta
meeting he rode in four races, scoring three wins and a
second.
• • * *
Battleaxe —winner of recently-run Auckland
(M.L.) Century Stakes, worth 1000 sovs. —was got by
Hotchkiss from Queen Cole (King Cole-Rosalie), and bred
by Mr. T. Morrin. At Wellington Park yearling sales in
’97 he went for 25 guineas, and started in “ hack ” com-
pany, but wasn’t long getting amongst the cracks.
Battleaxe closed on Taranaki Cup last Feb., and then
followed a long list of disappointments. The day prior
to the Century Stakes saw him run unplaced in a small
event, hence his party handled a comparatively big
“ divy.”
• m m *
Crack N.S.W. country performer, Lucknow,
recently bought for 370g5., and now in Kelso’s (Sydney)
stable, owns an interesting record. He was bred by one
of the “cloth” at Rylstone (N.S.W.), and is by Russley
out of a mare supposed to be by % Kingston colt. He is,
consequently, a full brother to Sam Fielder’s crack
sprinter, Bother. Before Lucknow’s capabilities became
apparent he did service in his owner’s buggy. At last
meeting of Bathurst Picnic Club he humped top weight
(13.12), and won the big handicap (1J miles) easily. His
record at one stage shows 12 successive wins, all scored
in western N.S.W. With a single exception Lucknow
always gave weight away—sometimes heaps. He’ll be
worth watching closely.
• » • *
At a recent N.S.W. country show the “fly”
Sydney push, for a lark, advertised in the local paper—
“ Wanted, a trotter better than 3miu.; apply Molar, Royal
Hotel.” Next morning a yokel brought up a neddy
which, he said, could break 3min. on the road in a heavy
sulky, and a trial was fixed for next morning before
breakfast. To the surprise of the “ talent,” the horse
trotted between two milestones in 2.55, and the trial
ended in “ Molar ” securing the horse for 15sovs. Since
coming to Sydney the horse has never been able to trot
3min. in a pneumatic sulky, and it now transpires that
the guileless country bumpkin had, the night previous
to the trial, shifted one milestone back a couple of hun-
dred yards. And “ Molar ” prides himself upon being
such a judge of pace.
• * » ■
At Bathurst (N.S.W.) annual show there were
some fine exhibitions of trotting. The local handicap (2
miles) fell to St. lago, an upstanding horse by Burlington
8., owned by Dr. Machattie, which covered its 2 miles in
smin. 28sec. Larry, another local horse, won the mile
trot in saddle, heating such a good one as Harold B. He
trotted his first half-mile in lmin. 16sec. and won, pulling-
up, in 2min. 35sec. ’The mile harness handicap was won
by Globe Junior, a fine bay gelded son of Globo, who won
as he liked in 2min. s|sec. Later on Globe Junior started
in the 2 miles handicap ; his future handicappers are not
likely to take his poor showing seriously. In the 2 miles
handicap Silverhue, driven by Dr. Slade, showed herself
a great mare. Moving like machinery she paced past her
field, and, giving nothing else a chance, finished with a
lot in hand in smin. 2sec.
The surprise of Bathurst meeting was the fine
going of Valdemar, a very fine black stallion by Viking
from Lonely Maid. The horse is owned by Mr. J. Joyn-
ton smith, of the Arcadia Hotel, Sydney, who certainly
proved himself a real sport in showing-up a maiden horse
for such small money. Valdemar took first prize for
trotting stallion ; won the maiden trot, and then carried
off the 2mm. 50sec. class trot. In the latter, driven by
Harry Knight, he won with ease in 2min 37|sec. Valde-
mar goes to the stud next season and should prove a
valuable addition to the few desirable N.S.W. trotting
stallions.
* * * •
l< Frank Leleu, the enthusiastic gambler
who died in London, the other week, was originally a
member of tire Billingsgate fish-ring, a lame man with a
quite ungovernable temper, which probably mellowed as
he grew old. Some of your ex-English readers may
remember him by his racing alias—Mr. Beadman (colors,
magenta and green belt). He lost a pot of money on the
turf in the ’6o’s, having the misfortune to own a remark-
ably “ flash ” nag named Orion. In Leleu’s mind, the
Epsom Derby of ’6B was a certainty for Orion, but after
leading the field by two or three lengths for a mile and a
quarter the irrepressible flier came back to them and was
eventually beaten out of a place by Blue Gown and
others. Blue Gown's: time was the record, I fancy, for
the old Derby course, thanks to Orion’s pace-making.
