Discomforting for “Celebration” visitors to
Melbourne to know that the police raided a “ two-up ”
school in that unsafe city the other Sunday morning, and
found about 50 criminals, mostly dangerous, among the
lot of 110 two-uplings who were caught on the premises.
These 50 or so birds of prey paid their fines and went at
large again. Melbourne just now is full of evil-looking
faces. All the “wrong ’uns” from all the States are
rallying to the cry of “ York,” and there will be dark
deeds done this merry month of May within a two-mile
radius of the Federal Parliament. Here is a chance for
combatively-disposed patriots to get sworn-in as special
constables.
•'* * •
He recent raid on that Melb. “two-up school.”
First words that struck the raiders’ ears were “ last
round 1 ” Those running the show sized matters up in-
stanter when they heard the rush, and fell back upon
the boxing-match dodge, but it didn’t go this time, as
the sham belligerents wore coats, collars and cuffs, and
the strings of the gloves hung loose ; besides, half-a-dozen
« COpS ” ha<l been there for hours before in civilian
togs.” The same ruse is always ready in Sydney at the
big “ schools,” where they’ve invariably got the boxers
stripped and wearing Boz. gloves.
* * * •
The S.S. Sonoma brought news that M.L. iron-
man Otto Cribb went under to the ’Frisco “Flyin"-
Dutchman, Thurston, on points, and two more rounds
at most would have seen him knocked out. Happenings
during previous meeting between this pair when Oribb
got the verdict should have warned those responsible to
give Thurston a wide berth, but probably Cribb’s over-
weening confidence prevailed against shrewd manage-
ment. Par. herein, 6/4/T, commented on O.C.’s weakness
m this direction, also his remark that he would “ lay
Thurston out this time,” and ventured to foretell that the
“ laying out ” would probably be done by t’other man
This view was based on the fact that in 'their previous
struggle Thurston made things very sultry all the jour-
ney, and had Oribb in sore straits once'. Besides, he
appeared to be a man of exactly the same variety as
Oribb, but heavier, longer in the reach, and cleverer
»*, * .
Details of the second Cribb-Thurston match are
that the local man stood fearful bangs on the jaw, left
jabs in the wind, and steam-hammer thumps under the
heart, but didn’t remain, idle. Claims of foul against
Cribb were frequent ; even the police took a hand but
the referee remained uninfluenced. Thurston’s strength
and fierceness all through were a revelation. Cribb was
tough and strong until 15th round, when he became
shaky, but the Friscan remained heavily charged with
vim. Thurston fought his man to his knees in the third
round and had him tottering badly just before “ corners.”
It was a cyclonic mad .thing from beginning to end. The
twain tripped, floundered, charged, spraw ed and clashed
together, time after time, while thousands looking on
swayed and howled again in their excitement. The sixth
round was all Cribb’s ; but Thurston went to his corner
apparently none the worse-save superficially—for the
awful gruelling. Thurston fought like a fiend in the. 15th
3min., and so demoralised Cribb that the M.L. pug. did
nothing afterwards but dodge his opponent’s tiger-rushes.
• • • ,
Australian top-notch light-weight Tim Hegarty
leaves for ’Frisco next Wednesday, matched to meet
“terrible” Terry M‘Govern for a big purse and kineto-
scopic privileges. ’Tis hard to judge at this distance how
Hegarty will fare, but as M’Govern is a rushing, bustlino-
fighter, the Australian should be suited down to the
ground ; he’s clever enough, game enough, and can hit
hard. Hegarty. is easily head of the best we have in his
class.
* * • *
Once T top-notch pug Mick Dooley confided to
a pressman t’other day that two weeks would be ample
for him to prepare for a match with Tut Ryan. If the
affair is arranged that way writer will look for a very
short go, with Tut much on top.
* * • *
Erstwhile middle-weight Australian champion
Dan Creedon has taken a fresh lease of pug life. After
being banged about by every man he shaped up to in the
last few years, he has made another bid, and accounted
for two Yankee front-rankers within a short space His
latest victim was Billy Stift, whom he put out in five
minutes, at Arkansas, before 3000 people. Reports tell
that Creedon’s in-fighting was terrific, also that he alwavs
got the better of the clinches. All through, his work
was strikingly like that shown by him at first in America.
* * * *
Maori “Ruenalf” and negro “Starlight”
scrambled through 16 rounds at Golden Gate A C (Syd-
ney) on Saturday night. “Old Star,” almost 50 and in
poor fettle, looked promising for several rounds till
tiring to nothing almost, he could do no more than tap
his adversary. Ruenalf, though of good physique clever
enough, and evidently in fine trim, had little or no sting
Starlight, limp and draggy, caved in at the close of
round 16 through sheer weariness. Ruenalf is a clever
boxer, but at the real thing is quite harmless. Gym-
nasium-made pugs, are not in it with the article born to
the business.

The annual inter-State eight : oar race comes off
on Parramatta River this Saturday. The Vies, haven’t
had as much practice as heretofore, but they will be
cherry ripe as far as condition goes ; having pre-
viously figured in regattas and championships, they were
very fit when selected. All the same the Cornstalks look
like making matters merry, if they don’t actuallv win •
they are a powerful and evenly-weighted lot, and have’
been through a good preparation. Bananaland unfor-
tunately couldn’t send her best team, and S A is in
like case, as some not originally chosen will have seats in
the boat. One of the Vies, is 41 years old, and two are
36 each, which, with the fact that the NSW crew is
thought the strongest raised for years, gives the mother
State much encouragement.
* » * *
Champion sculls this year will be contested by
two States only—Q. and N.S.W. Former sends M. Slack
and J. Lumsden. Slack is present amateur top-notcher
a ll® t , ra ' ia ’ while his pupil (Lumsden) is premier of Q.
I\.S.W. s crack, “ Joe ” Daley, easily accounted for Slack
on the Yarra a couple of years ago, but was unable to face
the music last year. Slack is said to be better this time
than ever, and as Daley is in great fettle, an interesting
struggle should result, albeit home State rowing men con-
sider Daley’s chance excellent. Lumsden, though own-
ing a fine physique, has a crude style, and is not thought
likely to give the other two any trouble. All three will
row in boats built by Chris. Keilson.
* * * *
East Adelaide, the club of which Clem Hill and
his three brothers are the mainstay, has secured the
Adelaide premiership for the third successive season.
Clem’s deeds in local games this season have been second
only to his performances in big matches.
* * * *
Fred. Hack, the lanky batsman who headed the
Australian batting averages in 1899-00, came out top in
local batting and bowling this season. In batting he
averaged 71 and bowling 14. Hack has an undoubted
future despite his partial failure in big cricket this year,
and he is, on present form, the best all-round player in
S.A.
* * * *
Vie. cricket season recently concluded left East
Melb. winners, with the great average of 47 ’23 runs per
wicket lost. Highest av., 103'66, made by J. Howlett in
7 innings with 4 not outs : next come M‘Alister, 69'14 for
8 innings, once not out: H. Stuckey 54, and Laver 44.
Lowest av.—that of bowler Collins—reached 21'25. Laver
got 44 wickets at a cost of 9'03 runs each, Collins coming
next with 19 at 18 runs each. The team went through
the season unbeaten, M.C.O. coming second with one
defeat.

Australian champion pigeon-shot, Mr. A. W.
Eales, now doing the Continent, is beginning to show his
best form. At Florence recently he won two big events,
killing 36 in one and 35 in another. Private letters tell
that a very heavy cold handicapped him much at Monte
Carlo.
* * * *
M.L. crack wheelman Geo. Sutherland is in
Australia for Q. cycling events and others still unde-
cided. At home, this season, he appropriated 15 races in
23 starts, was second twice and third thrice.
John Norton thinks of setting out for
England next month.

Philp’s flight to S’Africa is now stated to
be as advance-agent for a “ real big ” produce and
cattle trust. A lot of Q. moneyed men are sup-
posed to be in it, and Philp, by virtue of his office
as Q. Premier, would have a few cards up his
sleeve. One thing is certain—he isn’t coming
back to the York slobber, and another is that
Rutledge has his legal eye on Philp’s job.
Review-of-Reviews editor Fitchett’s jour-
nalistic cheek is sublime. Fitchett offered a prize
of £5O for a design for an Australian flag; and
the Barton Cabinet unkindly offered £75 for a
similar object, without reference to Fitchett.
Whereupon F. kindly invites the Barton Cabinet
to lump the competitions and the prizes; which
done, he will raise his prize to £7s—so that the
winning design will earn £l5O, presented by Aus-
tralia and Fitchett —or Fitchett and Australia;
and the national flag will do its first flutter, by
Govt, authority, as an advt. for the Review of
Reviews ! This impudent proposal hasn’t been
answered yet; but Fitchett says he’s hopeful. By
the way, the Govt, competition is to be submitted
to the Imperial authorities for adjudication.
Hasn’t Australia brains enough to choose her own
flag? The spectacle of Barton prostrate on his
belly before King Jingo will have to be seriously
considered ere long.
How a reputation is made for Royalty by
its servitors. From an English paper :
To all those who are lucky enough to be born on the
day that their Royal Highnesses first step on to Aus-
tralian ground the Duchess is giving a charming case con-
taining a silver spoon and fork. The Duke, as a “ re-
membrance,” is giving a sovereign—half a sovereign him-
self and half a sovereign from our King Edward. There
is a somewhat pretty little tale in connection with this
present. H.R.H. asked the King what he thought would
be a suitable memento of his visit—could His Majesty
suggest some little keepsake for the Australian children ?
“ You are half a Sovereign yourself—why not give half a
sovereign?” said the King, good-humoredly. “Well,
sir,” replied the Duke, very smartly, “as I am represent-
ing a Sovereign, why not present a sovereign?” The
King was so pleased with the witty reply of the Duke
that he immediately promised to. give the same amount
as the Duke of Cornwall, and thus the little ones who
first see the light on the day that their Royal Highnesses
land on Australian soil will be presented with a sovereign
and a silver spoon and fork apiece.
“ Born with a silver spoon in his mouth.”
From a N.S.W. civil servant, “Still
Hopeful ”: —
Editor Bulletin, —We are now in May, and the “ re-
grading ”is still anxiously awaited. This “ re-grading ”
is a re-classification of salaries and positions that the
Public Service Act requires the Public Service Board to
make every five years. The five years are now up and
the public servant is waiting in a state of suppressed rage
for the lists. For the financial year beginning Ist July,
’OO, Parliament voted £14,000 for promotions, &c., to de-
serving officers from that date. Subsequently the Board
issued a notice that they would grade the Departments as
from Ist Jan.,’ol, thus doing the public servants out of six
months at the higher rate. But the notice was welcomed
as it seemed to promise the early issue of the Gazette,
which would be something. But that was three months
ago, and we seem no nearer to it. Meanwhile every in-
justice felt in an office, any existing anomaly, all appeals
(and there are heaps) are put back, and have been
for nearly a year past, “until the regrading of the pub-
lic service.” The chief duty of the board, almost the
sole reason for appointing it, was the classification of
salaries in the service, and, with only two months of the
present financial year to run, the whole of the appeals
from the last batch of promotions remain unanswered,
and all the errors made on that occasion are still unre-
dressed. The public service vote, which went dead
against the Reid Govt, in 1898, will be solidly cast against
See this time. Whether rightly or not, the service believes
that its grievances can be settled by the existing Ministry,
and they invariably go against the existing administra-
tion, in the hope that the next one may be better for
them. It can’t be much worse.
Charles Mitchell’s (London) “Newspaper
Press Directory ” is, from an Australian point of
view, the most useful that is published. Because
The Bulletin prizes it, here fallows criticism, in-
stead of perfunctory praise, of the 1901 issue.
Minor points Laura Smith’s article on “Some
War Correspondents of the Year” extols well-
advertised duffers like Bennett Burleigh, and
omits to mention A. G. Hales, and indeed all
Australian correspondents. The article on “The
Impending Australian Tariff” is stupid, perpetu-
ating the fallacy that differences in progress of
N.S.W. and Vic. are exclusively due to differences
of Freetrade and Protection. Major point:— The
whole of the excellent Australian section should
be revised in the light of recent changes and the
1901 census : the standing matter is passing out of
date, or lias passed—some of it has nob been
altered for years.

The Talune Tragedy.
The trial of Jane Smith at Darlinghurst Criminal
Court, Sydney, for the murder of Patrick Conway
on board the Union s.s. Talune, on the high seas,
came to an end last Saturday morning about 9.30,
when the jury intimated to Acting-Judge Sly
that there was no possibility of agreeing upon a
verdict. .
As a spectacle, the trial was an interesting
gamble. The case for the Crown rested absolutely
on circumstantial evidence. Patrick Conway died
suddenly and in great agony on board the Talune.
The body was brought on to Sydney, and at first
the Govt, medical officer, on the description of
the symptoms, certified that death was due to a
fit. Later on it was discovered there was some
occult relationship between deceased and Mrs.
Smith, who had come from Maoriland, saloon, in
the same boat on which Conway was a steerage
passenger. Portions of the viscera had been pre-
served, and were handed to Mr. Hamlet, Govt.
Analyst, who swore death was due to strychnine
poisoning swore most emphatically that the
poison had been taken during life, and not in-
jected afterwards. Other Crown witnesses swore
Mrs. Smith had bought considerable quantities of
strychnine in Maoriland, and had given several
contradictory reasons for its purchase. One lot of
30grs. was bought the day before she left Christ-
church to join the Talune at Wellington—reason
given being that she wished to poison the cats
that kept her awake at nights. It was also sworn
that Mrs. Smith bought a bottle of porter on the
night of Conway’s death ; and another witness
testified that he saw her going towards the steer-
age carrying something under a serviette or
handkerchief, saw her sit down on the hatch
beside Conway, and take a glass from beneath
the cloth. Then Conway placed something to his
lips and tossed his head back. About twenty
minutes later Conway was in his death-agony.
The Crown pointed out that peculiar relations had
existed between Conway and Mrs. Smith. He
was admitted to have held a mortgage of £2OO
Over her home, which she alleged had been paid
off a few weeks before they left Maoril'ind. If so,
he must have had a considerable amount of cash
On him, to obtain which, it was suggested, might
have been the object of the crime. On the other
hand, if the mortgage, which was not properly
registered, Was not paid, it might have been de-
sired to remove him in order to escape payment.
Either way, the Crown urged, there was a motive,
but if the jury were satisfied on the other evidence
as to her guilt, they need not hesitate to convict
her because they could not guess her motive. It
was only circumstantial evidence, but to sup-
port it, her actions before, during, and after
the voyage had been, it was contended, most
suspicious. She had bought and handled
strychnine in Maoriland, and yet she went about
Sydney asking people what sort of stuff it was.
She concealed her friendship with Conway, and
described the man she afterwards admitted had
loaned her husband £2OO as a “poor working
fellow whom she and her husband had befriended
with a few weeks’ work in the garden.”
It was a dismal day when George Reid started
his address to the jury. The rain struck the
windows with an angry swish. The atmosphere
of the crowded court was moist and dull grey, and
the only glimmer came from the thin band of gilt
along the cornice. Justice Sly sat writing hard
and quietly. His associate stared fixedly at the
roof, as though his mind were asleep. All around
a sea of faces weltered right up to the dull, kharki
walls, and stared down from the gallery above.
Close to the spikes of the dock a junior counsel
yawned wearily at the ceiling, as though George
Reid were delivering an address on the W estminster
Confession. Behind the green spikes of the cage
sat Mrs. Smith, dressed in a light brown macin-
tosh, with a brown fur boa, a gem straw hat, and
her handkerchief covering her face, sobbing, while
at the barristers’ table stood George Reid battling
for her life.
And he made a great fight for the life of the
terror-stricken, sobbing woman behind the spikes.
He gripped the gloomy-looking case with both
hands and buried it beneath a mountain of doubt.
Where, he asked, was the motive? People didn’t
go about in this work-a-day world slaying others
like the Borgias of old, because they took a sud-
den dislike to them. It was too inhuman and
dangerous. If she had slain him for his money
why did she stay here till the slow-moving officials
had blundered on the supposition that she had
done it? If she had all nis cash but the 14s.
found upon his body would she not have simply
vanished? Instead of which she stayed on in
Sydney and told everyone she spoke to that she
came over in the boat on which Conway had died
so dreadfully, and that, just before his death, she
had given him some biscuits and plums. Why,
the woman was stranded here, and to pay her ex-
Eenses had to raise money from friends and pawn-
rokers. She was actually dependent upon Con-
way, and when he died on board she found herself
penniless in Sydney. Conway had, no doubt,
lost his money in Maoriland, before he left, and
considering that she relied upon him to keep her
it was hardly credible she would slay him. She
had certainly contradicted herself in some of her
stories, but that was in her favour. Any
woman who suddenly sees the SHADOW OF A
HALTER (his shrill voice simply screeched it)
dangling in the distance, not being a har-
dened poisoner with a plausible story ready for
the occasion, would equivocate. Every word she
had said in public had been brought up to H-A-N-G
her. (The shrill, deafening cry with which George
Reid brought out the word “Hang” shook the
building and made the jury leap up in affright.)
When a person’s N-E-C-K I-S I-N D-A-N-G-E-R
she is liable to lose her customary candour and
behave in a contradictory manner. The cool,
hardened poisoner would have had the whole
story thought out to - the last detail, to fit in with
facts that could be easily proved. Why, the first
idea that she had killed Conway occurred to the
police through her own artless prattle. ‘ ‘ Gentle-
men, are—you—going—TO HANG this woman ”
(the house tottered as George Reid said “ Hang ”)
“on her own thoughtless words?” Had she pre-
meditated this crime would she have bought
porter on board to administer the poison in ? Of
course not; she would have taken the means to
administer it, as well as the poison, on board with
her. No doubt Mr. Hamlet was a very able
man, but he didn’t know everything. Another
analyst might have flatly contradicted him. The
Govt. Medical Officer had first certified that
death was due to a fit having something to
do with diseased kidneys. Conway’s kidneys were
very diseased. Truly, that medical officer had
certified something else later on, but who was to
tell if his second certificate was of any more value
than his first ? Both certificates depended upon
what somebody had told him. As for the young
man who swore he had watched Mrs. Smith take
a glass of something to Conway—well, what of it!
Mrs. Smith never denied that she gave Conway a
glass of porter. The only thing was that the
young man swore it was about 20 minutes before
Conway’s seizure. Well, he was a, very young
man. If he really saw someone give Conway a
glass of something just before the seizure it did
not prove that it was Mrs. Smith. This very
certain young man, of course, could not make a
mistake. We are all very confident when we are
young. The youth was not intimate with Mrs.
Smith. He did not even know her name, but he saw
a woman in the gloom—a woman with a light
cloak and a straw hat—stroll forward in the semi-
darkness, and sit down beside Conway, and give
him a drink. Some weeks later he strolled into a
Court and saw a woman in a light cloak and a
straw hat, and was perfectly siire it was the same
he saw in the semi-darkness. He couldn’t make
any mistake —lie was a young man, and thoy never
do. The woman wore a light cloak —so did the
other one. She wore a straw hat—so did the other
one —were they going TO hang (the monosyllable
rolled all the way down Oxford-street and fled in
its wild vociferousness round Paddington and
Bondi) this woman sitting here, because she wore
a light cloak and straw hat, and young men were
painfully confident ?
And the rain swished down ; the woman sobbed
on ; outside the green spikes the junior counsel
smiled softly at his thoughts as though no woman’s
life swung in the balance ; and the shrill voice of
theK.C. rang through the crowded court to the
refrain of hang, hang, hang— death penalty—no
hope of mercy—putting the noose round her neck,
&c., till the horror of the death sentence and the
last grisly scene rose up and smote each juryman
to the heart —and the Twelve shuddered.
Could the jury have retired immediately, judg-
ing by their faces, Jane Smith might have walked
out of the Court a free woman within 30 minutes.
But a tall, black-robed figure rose up in the
damp, cold court, and with a hard, bass voice com-
menced to tear away at the masses of doubt
George Reid had piled up on the Crown’s case
That lawyer, at least, talked as if absolutely con-
vinced of Jane Smith’s guilt, and he pressed the
case against her ruthlessly, and drove home his
points with grim earnestness. The law did not
actually require a witness to prove the mixing of
the poison with the draught, or there would be
few poisoners convicted. If they were satisfied
beyond all reasonable doubt that Jane Smith slew
Patrick Conway, it was their duty to return a ver-
dict, of guilty. And he urged that the circum-
stances surrounding the case were absolutely con-
vincing.
For nearly two hours he pieced up the case
against her, and, strange to say, the woman who
had sobbed so heart-breakingly while Reid fought
for her life was more composed while Wade fought
as resolutely to have her slain. She sat abso-
lutely motionless, with her hands crossed on her
lap, her chin sunk forward on her bosom, the in-
carnation of hopeless misery, quite worn out with
the strain. At 11 p.m. the jury retired for the
night. The trap-door was lifted, and the woman
disappeared down the grave-like aperture with
two policemen.
Next morning, a few minutes before the chime
of 9.30, she was sitting behind the spikes, abso-
lutely white with dread and anxiety. The jury
filed in, and she cast one terrified look at
them, and then her eyes dropped and her face
blanched even whiter. Every seat was packed, and
everyone listened breathlessly to what the fore-
man of the jury might say. He said there was no
chance of their agreeing. The woman’s bosom
gave one convulsive heave, and then she sat
motionless as ever. The jurymen were discharged,
and streamed out; the Crown witnesses were
bound over to appear on May 28th, and the crowd
in the Court melted away. Once more the trap-
door was lifted, and Jane Smith again vanished
down the underground passage with a slight trace
of color in the drawn haggard face that still had
round, plump outlines.

Concerning lady-barbers :
Dear Bulletin.—“Nunc dimittis,” I have met her at
last, in sleepy Blenheim, Maoriland, too —of all places in
the world. Was bored to death, went to get a shave, to
pass the time, and there I found her—the lady barber.
No more talk of races, football, pugs, blokes and coves ;
no more beery breath, badly disguised with cloves, no
more clammy cold hands—these "will be amongst the
troubles of the past when the ladv barber, bless her, gets
more numerous. A dainty little lady this, light of hand,
and supple-wristed. Her touch, as she moves your head
this way and that, is half a caress ; her pretty face, as she
bends to her work, is temptingly close, close enough to
feel her breath on your cheek, a breath of new-mown hay
and wild roses. “No ! I didn’t cut the first man I shaved ;
I did the second, though, but he was nervous. I often
came to dad’s shop, and the boys used to tease me to shave
them, and, just for fun, I practised on my brothers and
then dad got sciatica, and, somehow, I took to it m
earnest, and here I am. A little bay-rum ? Thank you,
sir. Next please.” Ah ! well. I shall settle in Blenheim,
I think, and get shaved twice a day.—O.E.H.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising; is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large s urns of money in studying that- Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally. We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment may
he the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run not in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.

If you live out of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive you
of the benefits of our wonderful treatment, because in the new system
of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro-Medical home treatment is provided for. Scarcely a day
passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a personal
visit could not be made. Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining vour feelings and symptoms as clearly
as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of writing,
and twopence postage to get the opinion and advice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.

J. Gr. Russell, S.M., who began life as a
wharf-hand, and is now S. A, Commissioner of
Insolvency, has been at death’s door recently
through an attack of pneumonia. Russell is con-
sidered a certainty for the next vacancy on S.A.
judicial bench. He has the rare distinction of
never having had a decision upset on appeal.
She Carries It With Her.
When Mrs. Mary Wren is about to start on a
trip to Sydney she always packs in her bag a
bottle of Mother Seigel’s Syrup. Of course, she
is careful to put it where it is not likely to get
broken, as she does not, want to waste the medi-
cine, neither does she want to spoil her things.
So far, I am glad to say, she has carried her
precious bottle without an accident.
And when she gets home to Acacia Cottage,
Bridge-Street, Muswellbrook, New South Wales,
she has always reason to be thankful that she
took the Syrup with her.
For, you must know, that on the 20th day of
Sept., 1899, Mr 3. Wren was 75 years old, and fifty
of them she has spent in the town of Muswell-
brook. She has had fifteen children, ten of whom
are still living; certainly a record of which she
has a right to be proud.
Now, to make this little tale run straight; and
to keep the kinks out of it, we shall have to hark
back to the place where it properly starts. Better
still, perhaps, to let the good old lady tell it her-
self, as she does in a letter dated 21st of Septem-
ber (next day after her birthday), 1899.
“ Most of mv life,” so she goes on, “ I had suf-
fered from indigestion and wind on the stomach.
I have often been up half the night trying, to
relieve the terrible pains caused by the wind.
“ I spent a lot of money on the essence of ginger
and other things, but they all failed miserably.
The essence of ginger would wai'm me for a few
minutes, and then the pains would be on again ;
just as a barking dog begins again after you have
hit him with something. That’s the way it was
with me.
“About five years ago I had a very bad time
with influenza; and when I was slowly getting
over that the indigestion came on worse than
ever. We couldn’t do anything for it, or with it;
no more could the doctors.
“ Then an old friend happened in, and she said,
‘Why don’t you take Mother Seigel’s Syrup?’
I told her I didn’t believe in any of the advertised
medicines. She went on imploring and entreating,
and I said she might as well save her breath, for
she couldn’t move me an inch out of my own
opinions.
“What does that woman do but go and buy a
bottle without my knowledge, and fetch it to me.
Then I gave in and began trying it. That very
bottle helped, and, after taking a few bottles
more, I was as well as anybody wants to be.
“Since using Mother Seigel’s Syrup I have got
rid of all my pains and aches, and to make sure of
keeping them away I carry a bottle with me
wherever I go.”—Mary Wren.
Mr. William John Davison, Saddle and Harness
Maker, of Muswellbrook, writes that he has
known Mrs. Wren for 30 years, and the public
may put full faith in every word she says.*

Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to tlieir advantage

that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

of many historic incidents in early Australia, for
years a sauce factory, for years a boarding-house,
now goes into business as a grammar-school.

Brisbane Municipal Council is lavishing
£4OOO on arches, etc., for the Yorks, what time
the Plague is knocking loudly at the city’s door,
and the Health Commissioner is calling on the
Council to keep the place fairly free from filth.
Some incensed ratepayers are going to see if the
law allows such expenditure of the rates.
“ H.” : The Jook is to take a stare at the
postponed Mclb. Eight Hours’ procession most
likely. It is supposed that the men and banners
of the Trades will interest their Royal Nibses,
but, bless you! they won’t care tuppence about
the honest show. It isn’t in their line.
Q. Minister Chataway, who died the
other day, when he entered politics in N.Q. years
ago, found the hardest part of his task was to
meet puns and jibes on his peculiar name. Mackay
doesn’t grow much original wit, and facetious
people there took Chataway as a gift from Provi-
dence. On several occasions he cut speeches short
owing to the incessant ringing of changes on
chat—chat —chatteraway. He became an indus-
trious local member, and won the respect of most
in Parliament. Premier Byrnes had a high
opinion of his energy. Chataway ran a statistical
sugar-paper, The Sugar Journal, from the
Kanaka point of view. He remembered the time
when the traffic was undisguised slavery, and re-
garded the condition of things to-day as almost
idyllic. He died in a Brisbane hospital.
The psychological moment of the Coning-
ham-O’Haran verdict has been seized by a Mr.
Marcus Beresford for an appeal on behalf of
Melbourne’s excellently managed R.C. Hospital—
St. Vincent’s. He offers £lOOO towards the build-
ing of a new wing, on condition that another
£9OOO be raised by the public, and the offer is not
likely to fall through. Mr. Beresford doesn’t
belong to the Catholic Church, but his name sug-
gests that he is an Irishman, and probably a
connection of the Waterford family.
Edwin Smith, the prize patron of Ade-
laide football and cricket, was a crack rifle-shot 50
years ago. Recently called upon to open a new
butt in his district, Smith took up a rifle and
scored a bull’s-eye with his first and only shot.
He was 70 years old in March.

The SIXTEENTH REPORT of the DIRECTORS Of the COLONIAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
To be Presented to the Shareholders at the Sixteenth Ordinary General Meeting, to be held at the Bank,
IS6 Elizabeth-street, at Noon on Tuesday, 30th April, 1901.
REPORT.
rnHE DIRECTORS beg to submit to the Shareholders their Sixteenth Report, with a Balance-sheet and Statement
of Profit and Loss for the Half-year ended the 31st March, 1901,'duly audited.
After providing for expenses of management, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current, tax on note-
circulation, and making provision for bad and doubtful debts, the net profit, including the balance brought forward
from last half-year, amounted to £16,663 19s. 6d.. which the Directors propose to apportion as follows, viz. :
Dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum on preference shares £7,601 2 0
To reserve-fund .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. 5,000 0 0
Balance carried forward 4,062 17 6
£16,663 19 6
The business of the Bank shows a material improvement for the half-j'ear, and, in view of the increased profits,
the Directors recommend an increase in the dividend on the preference shares from 4 per cent, to 5 per cent, per
annum ; and also that the sum of £5OOO be transferred to the reserve-fund, which will then amount to £20,000.
The Directors paid off the sixth instalment of the deferred deposit receipts, payable from 1904-1905, during the
half-year, leaving a balance of £123,990 17s. Id. outstanding.
During the branches have been opened at Loch and Oobden.
The dividend will be payable at the head office on and after the Ist prox., and at the branches on receipt of advice.
The Sixteenth Ordinary General Meeting of Shareholders will be held at the head office of the Company, 126 Eliza-
beth-street, Melbourne, on Tuesday, the 30th day of April, 1901, at noon.
By Order of the Board, SELBY PAXTON, General Manager.
Melbourne, 18th April, 1901.

SIXTY-SEVENTH ANNUAL REPORT of the DIRECTORS of THE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA, with the
ACCOUNTS for the HALF-YEAR to 15th OCTOBER, 1900, presented to the Proprietors at the Annual General
Meeting, held on Thursday, 28th March, 1901, at one o’clock.
REPORT.
rpHE DIRECTORS submit to the Proprietors the Balance-sheet as at 15th October last, with the Profit Account for
the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and colonial rates and taxes,
and for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year, including some recoveries from old accounts,
amounted to £151,465 17s. 5d.
From this sum, increased by £12,575 2s. 5d. brought forward from the previous half-year, the Directors have
appropriated £50,000 to the Reserve Fund, and £20,000 in reduction of the cost of bank premises, leaving a net dis-
posable balance of £94,040 19s. 10d., of which the dividend declared will absorb £80,000, and there will then remain
£14,040 19s. lOd. to be carried forward to next account.
The season has been fairly satisfactory, though in some districts, and particularly in most parts of Queensland,
the disastrous drought still continues. The price of wool, which has again fallen to a very low level, materially re-
tards the improvement in the pastoral industry, upon which the prosperity of the colonies so much depends.
The Dividend declared is at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum, or £2 per share for the half-year, and will be pay-
able, free of income tax, in London and in the colonies on the 29th March.
4 Threadneedle-street, London, 18th March, 1901. LICHFIELD, Chairman.

A Masterton (M.L.) barber’s dog strutted
into the shop lately with a roll of seven £l-notes,
which he had found in the street. A poodle that
can differentiate between flimsies and ordinary
dirty paper is a 24-carat dog.
Stated that the Queen of Holland has put
aside a bottle of water from the Jordan, where-
with to christen her first-born. What a glorious
thing to be a Christian child, and not a little
Hindoo, brought up to believe the Ganges a sacred
river! By the way, it has been enacted by the
Dutch Parliament that if the recently-wedded
Queen has no children within five years of her
marriage the marriage is to be dissolved, ihe
Queen’s Consort is therefore a mere probationer.
Victoria hated Queen Elizabeth and sym-
pathised with Mary, Queen of Scots. The word
“Pretender” might never be used in her presence
—he had to be always called Charles Edward. She
writes that, when fourteen years old, she darted a
furious look at a woman who said “ May she prove
another Elizabeth ! ”
Counsel were once arguing a tedious case
before the late Judge Manning (N.S.W.) when a
portion of the wooden ceiling of the Equity Court
fell. His Honor asked what had happened, and
was told by the orderly that dry-rot had caused
the mishap. “Ah ! ” said the Judge—“ counsels’
arguments, of course ! ” An English Judge’s quip
in similar circumstances was better still —“Fiat
justitia ruat ccelum !”

THE GRIP OF LA GRIPPE.
The Expulsion of the Enemy.
The Case of
Mr. ALEXANDER HISLOP.
(For the “News.")
One o! the best known chefs In Mew
South Wales is Mr. Alexander Hislop,
residing at No. 34 Burton-street, Darling-
hurst. That gentleman has been engaged
as chief cook in some of the leading hotels
and restaurants in Sydney, and also in
several important country towns in the
mother colony. Hie popularity was account-
able for our representative hearing of a
visitation of illness whilst at business in
Albury, the details of which were supplied
in the course of the following interview :
“ A few years ago,” began Mr. Hislop,
“ I was at work on the Albury railway
station, and whilst there I had a very severe
attack of influenza. It came on like an
ordinary cold, and I treated it lightly for
some time; but it eventually got me down
altogether. *The first indications were a
tingling, dry sense of fullness in the nose,
and I used to sneeze nearly the whole day.
The pains in my head I shall never forget—
particularly across my forehead. I could
not breathe through my nose at all, and my
eyes vere red and watery. Through a
harassing dry cough, which came on at
regular intervals, my throat got very sore.
My lungs were quite clogged up with the
cold, and I became so hoarse that I could
not make my voice heard. I was unfortunate
in having aching pains all over my body,
but intensely acute in my back and legs I
was never free from thirst, being always
feverish. My temperature increased every
• lay, and caused me no little alarm.
Whenever I was exposed to air a little
cooler than what I was accustomed to, I felt
lashes of heat and chills one after the other.
£ was always wanting to get near the fire.
My windpipe was inflamed and seemed
swollen, and provoked considerable pain.
During the first stages of my attack I forced
myself to eat a little food, although my sore
throat made the task a difficult one ; but in
& few days all appetite had forsaken me
entirely, and I could only drink large
quantities of water. It was a risky and
foolhardy thing for me to do, but many a
night I got out of bed and tramped the
station platform from end to end because I
was too restless and feverish to sleep.
Luckily for myself I have a constitution
like a horse, or else those walks in search
of relief would have caused my death. I
was too weak to stay out long, and on
eturning to bed I had to lie in a feverish,
irning state till morning. As the disease
dd a firmer hold upon me I had water
aiming from the nose and eyes, and my
ough became a little more moist. There
vas also a slight discharge from my throat.
My pulse had attained a rapidity which I
did not think possible, and I was so reduced
in flesh that the complicated symptoms
-'’casiond me distressing forebodings.”
v“ What did your doctor say ? ” asked the
reporter.
WHe said I had influenza in its most
serious form. I came down to Sydney for
treatment, and remained three weeks here.
During those three weeks I took every care
of myself, with a view to getting better as
soon as possible. My doctor’s directions
were closely attended to, and I did every-
thing that common sense could suggest to
assist in my recovery. I did not waste one
teaspoonful of his medicine all the time;
but at the end of the third week I thought I
might just as well suffer at Albury as in
Sydney. My doctor had not given me any i
relief with his physic, so I went back again,
feeling every bit as bad as when I went
away. I thought I would never be well
again, so depressed and low-spirited had I
fot. However, on returning to the country
engaged a furnished house, away from the
noise of the station, as 1 was too worn out
and irritable to stand it, and I made up my
mind to lie up till I was either killed or
cured.”
“And what sort of progress did you
make ? ”
“ I made very rapid progress indeed.
The taking of that furnished house was the
luckiest thing I have ever done in my life.
Through an oversight on the part of the
previous occupier, a bottle of Clements
Tonic was left on one of the mantelpieces,
and I read the label. ‘Everything else has
failed to cure me, so I will give this a trial,’
I said, and right glad lam that I did. A
few doses eased my chest of a thick, yellow
phlegm, and my breathing became much
easier. The soreness of my throat was
cured simultaneously with my cough, and I
soon had a longing for food, which 1 ate with
a relish. The splitting frontal headaches
went as quickly as they came, and the
pains all over my body left me as 1 daily
grew stronger. I could walk about without
feeling as though my legs would collapse
under me, and without feeling light-headed
or giddy. My eyes, which had looked so
bleary red and watery for weeks, now
look-ed clear and bright. As I continued to
get along so well, I knew I had hold of the
right remedy, so I sent to the local store for
a couple more bottles of Clements Tonic
By the time they were exhausted I had not
a sign of influenza about me. I could eat
well, sleep well, and work well. I had not
been able to smoke for weeks, but now I
could enjoy a pipe as much as anybody. I
do not know what would have become of me
at that time if it had not been for Clements
Tonic, as it cured me of the most severe
attack of influenza when mv doctor and his
medicine had failed.”
“Do you ever feel anv i ll o«ncts of your
illness now ? ”
“No ; none whatever. But I frequently
work long hours at a stretch, and get s
little run-down. I am never without i
bottle of Clements Tonic in the house, so 1
soon pick myself up again. ”
“Do you consent to vour evnerienc
being made known?” _ _
“ Most certainly. I tell all my friends
what I am indebted to for my present good
health, and you are quite welcome to publish
my case in any way you like.”

Kutnouj’s pouider.
Tan-y-Bwlch, Chester Road, Erdington,
Messrs. Kutnow & Co., Ltd., London. 4 / 2 / 1901,
Dear Sirs.—l have recently seen your advertise-
ment and offer in the London Morning Leader, and
shall be glad if you will send me a bottle of your
Carlsbad Powder for use in my family. I first heard
?c.r V ° 1 , 1 r l preparafcion from Gardener, R.A.M.C.
(St. John Ambulance Brigade, Birmingham (City)
Division), who has just returned in charge of invalids
from South Africa, He was for some time stationed
at irmcess Christian Hospital, Pine Town Bridge
Natal, and had also served at several other base
hospitals. His first duty on arriving in Durban, in
April last, was to assist in burying four victims from
Enteric Fever. As I was about sending out nine
more men (they sail on the Lismore Castle on the
7th), I asked him to address them. In the course of
his remarks he urged them to lay in a supply of
KutnoW’s Powder. He himself had attended to
HUNDREDS of CASES of ENTERIC, and the only
medicine he partook of was KUTNOW’S POWDER
twice or thrice a week as instructed on the bottle
He had never been “ SICK OR SORRY ” for an hour’
and he attributed this fact to the bi-weekly use of
KUTNOW. In following Sgt. Gardener I suggested
that the men should lay in a stock of KUTNOW and
I trusted they would benefit from the advice given
and would return to England, looking as strong and
hearty as he did. B
Yours faithfully,
J. R. STAMP,
Supt. Sec., St. John Ambulance Brigade
Birmingham (City) Division. ’

An Island Yarn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The story of Membis, chief of Enoch Island,
New Ireland, translated into English'.
* * * *
You jeer me because I warn you not to go after
the big boar that comes year after year to destroy
our plantations. You mock, and say lam afraid
to go. You ask whether I think the boar will eat
your rifle ? You say that lam only a baby in my
fear, and that any white boy would go out and
conquer; why then should a white-headed warrior
be afraid ? Listen ! white man who boasts of his
strength and his quickness with the rifle. Hear,
scorner, the words of warning from one whose
counsel is not always given, but whose counsel is
never bad. The men of your race are strong in
vigour arid bravery, and laugh at those things they
do not understand ; and you will probably laugh
at my tale, But what I tell you happened when
I was awake and fighting for my life; not when
asleep and dreaming, nor ’ when the demon from
the “square-face ’’ had overcome ifae.
Many, many moons ago, when my father was
chief, and my elder brother was the next upon
whom the chieftainship would descend, I was
known as the best hunter and most fearless war-
rior within many days’ voyage from here. The
possession of the best tusk-pigs in the island, my
marriage with the most beautiful woman eyes had
ever rested upon (the daughter of a great chief in
Aoba), and my fame as hunter and warrior, had
caused a bitter jealousy and hatred to spring up
in the dark heart of my brother. How dark that
heart was I did not know for many seasons.
One season, when the yams were almost; ready
to dig-up, a very large boar came from the bush
and destroyed them, and all the efforts of the
hunters were unavailing. Coming home one day
with a large haul of fish I had speared on yonder
reef, I was annoyed to find that my father and
brother had set out that morning in the hopes of
ridding the neighbourhood of a dangerous enemy.
Greatly hurt and mortified was I at being left out
of an expedition wherein I fancied my fame would
not have suffered; and little thought that my
turn was to come in a future from which the suns
of time would disperse and roll away the clouds of
darkness and uncertainty.
That night the hunters did not return; and,
with an ill-concealed anxiety, I organised a party
to search for them, and, if possible, kill the boar,
should he not be already dead. Picking-up the
boar’s tracks of the day before, we passed through
the bush until we came to an open glade olothed
with grass. But the sight awaiting me
there caused horror and sorrow to almost
overwhelm me. These were the tracks of our
enemy. There, buried a fifth of its length in the
ground, quivered my father’s hunting spear, as
the morning wind swept through the scene of
death. There, too, was the tomahawk cleaving
the soft sward ; and by the side of both, alas ! lay
the old chief, stiff and rigid in death, with an
arrow piercing his neck from side to side. His
blood clotted the arrow and lay in horrible
blackened gouts on the turf, and his white hair,
venerated by all the tribe, was now dyed in
patches of ghastly red.
'But, horrible though this sight was, and start-
ling in its suggestion of treachery, a no less
hideous sight awaited us when our eyes turned in
sorrow from the spot. There, on the edge of the
bush, lay the fearfully-mangled body of him who
had been my brother. No arrow or spear had
killed him; for such ripping, tearing, gashing,
could only have been effected by the tusks of a
boar of extraordinary size. All round the body,
kneading the earth and grass into a mud with the
blood of the victim, were the same tracks as we
had previously seen ; while here and there were
drying flecks of foam churned by the teeth of a
maddened boar.
Sadly and mournfully were the bodies borne to
the village, the whole island summoned to the
rite, and the last respects paid to the venerated
an d his eldest son. The next' day all the
chiefs swore their allegiance to me as their head,
and vowed to assist in punishing the murderer.
But all our inquiries failed to find the guilty
party. No strangers were on the island ; and
there was no one upon whom could be fastened
any word or deed of enmity towards the victim ;
so the matter had, perforce, to drop, remaining a
mystery.
For many moons I inquired and searched fruit-
lessly, and at last settled down in my father’s
footsteps to administer justice and govern with
mildness. Being occupied with the settlement of
the property of my father and brother, you can
well imagine that I had no time for pig-hunting
for a few moons ; and when all was settled the
big pig had ceased his depredations and had dis-
appeared.
Next season, when our yams were just about
ready, lie again appeared, and began to destroy
our crops. Having the deaths of my father and
brother to avenge, my reputation as a hunter at
stake, and the interests of my people to defend, I
joyfully embraced the opportunity offered me.
Bidding my people to form different hunting-
parties, and not to expect me back until the fol-
lowing night, I took some food and started alone
on my quest. Finding fairly fresh tracks early in
the day, I followed them up through the dense
bush —past gigantic banyan trees spreading their
limbs in all directions, from which drooped
their many roots—through clumps of areca
trees upon which the pigeons were boom-
ing in content. Here the graceful bam-
boo leaned its top towards the banyan-tree in
friendly greeting. There the tall spear-grass al-
most blocked my path. Still, on and on I went,
until the sun began to get close to the horizon.
Round one hill, over another, through this swamp,
over that creek ; and when the moon rose, its fitful
gleams shone on the awesome glade where had
been fought the fearful battle of one year ago.
Concluding that here was the home of my
enemy, I decided to wait for him. In this one
thing the islander is superior to the white man—-
he can wait. Famous hunter though you are,
could you patiently wait hour after hour after a
long day’s search, knowing that you had a great
struggle before you ? As the hours slowly crawled
away you would have tired, and either slept or
gone away. Not so Membis. Without moving a
muscle, in case my enemy might be close at hand,
I patiently waited until the moon passed from
there to there [he indicated about four hours].
At last my patience was rewarded, for presently
I heal’d the leaves rustling, and then saw the
biggest boar my eyes had ever rested upon. Slowly
and carelessly he came on, as though unaware
of any enemy, or else disdainful. Choosing my
best arrow, I carefully fitted it to the string, and
awaited in the shade for a good opportunity to
present itself. Apparently ignorant of my pre-
sence, he passed obliquely by me, and, seizing the
chance, I released my arrow when lie was
only a few paces away. Striking him fairly
behind the shoulder, I saw the arrow go
right through him, and I eagerly jumped
forward in the expectation that he would fall
dead. But, as quick as thought, he wheeled
round and charged me with fury. Setting my
spear at rest, I sprang aside and thrust home,
only to fall on my face, for there was nothing to
resist my thrust. Hearing the spear break, I
naturally thought I had not been quick enough ;
so, drawing my tomahawk, I waited for his next
charge. Very little time was given me for
thought, for he had turned as I handled my
weapon. On he came, with head down, ready
for the awful stroke which should disable me.
His wicked eyes gleamed in the cold light of the
moon, and I could just see the blood-stained foam
gathering round his jaws.
Waiting until he was almost upon me, I sprang
into the air and brought my tomahawk down on
his loins. Again, I was foiled; for, to my dis-
may, the weapon struck through the loins without
resistance; and again I fell. Yet again I faced
him witli the same result. With a growing horror
slowly gripping my brain and heart, I faced him
again, and made one desperate effort to cleave his
head. Fairly as was the blow aimed, it was use-
less, and only placed me at the mercy of my foe,
for, as I fell he turned and stood over me. Ex-
pecting to be savagely ripped and torn, I was
preparing a blow, when his awful eyes stopped
me. Looking him carefully over I could see no
sign of wound, although his jaws were dropping
bloodstained foam upon me. Suddenly, the
horror which had been slowly creeping over me
culminated in the dreadful knowledge (how I can-
not tell you) that I was fighting an intangible
substance, but that I should receive no harm.
Then a revelation came to me which made my
hair bristle with fear. I found myself standing in
a shady part of the glade, and saw my father
attacking the same boar. He shot his arrow with
the same result as I had experienced ; he used his
spear with the same result; and he was drawing
his tomahawk for a final effort when my brother,
who up to then had been invisible, stepped out
from the darkness, drew his bow up to his
ear and transfixed my father with an arrow
as we found him. With a horrible snarl of
rage the boar charged the murderer; and,
in spite of arrow, spear, and tomahawk soon
left him the horrible spectacle which
sickened all who saw him in the bright
morning light of a year before. Realising the ex-
tent of the crime to which my brothers hate,
jealousy and ambition had driven him, and satis-
fied that it was no earthly thing which had
punished him, I lost my senses.
When I recovered consciousness, eleven moons
after, they told me they had waited two nights
for my i-eturn, and then, fearing they knew not
what, they set out in search of me. Seeing my
body lying in the same place where my father had
been found, they fled in horror. With a just rage
and indignation my wife drove them back, and I
was carried to my village, and carefully and ten-
derly nursed through the raging fever which fol-
lowed. Ho you wonder at the earnestness with
which I beseech you not to try to kill that boar ?
* * * *
As the old man ceased, his followers looked at
one another horror-stricken; and then, with
almost one voice, they implored me not to attempt
the life of this big* unnatural monster, nor to go
pig-hunting in that direction again. But I did
go, and although I saw some enormous tracks in
the glade where the tragedy had taken place, all
the pigs I shot were very ordinary ones.

“ Peter K.” : “ Carpum rarpound harpe-
yarpere Tarpora, tharpat arpold blopoke barpit
marpee jarpust narpow arpand arpime gopoarping
tarpoo garpive harpim arpay barpump,” is wliat I
heard a youngster say in a crowd, the other night.
The translation is: “ Come round here, Tom.
That old bloke hit me just now, and I’m going to
give him a bump.” The principle of the lingo is
very easy to see when the words are written, but
a sentence like the above, spoken quickly, would
be extremely hard to follow. The language seemed
perfectly intelligible to the youngster’s com-
panions, one Of whom supplied me with the words
and their translation.
Sensations after being shot through the
brain, as described in S.A. Register by *a
S’African scout:—
Suddenly a dullness came over my sight, and all was
dark. My head felt like a nest of minah birds, all of them
on the twitter. After that I felt as though water was
drop, drop, dropping inside my head, and some seconds
afterwards I knew that I was swaying in the saddle as my
horse eased down his speed near the base of our objective.
Almost instantaneously he sank from under me while I
rolled clear, rifle and reins in hand, over his left shoulder.
There we both lay, and I .imagined that I had been half
stunned by his fall. I could hear an occasional 151 b. shell
whizzing through the air, as I lay there, quite Content, if
not happy.”
The scale of seamen’s food allowed by law
on British ships, and usually maintained on sail-
ing-vessels :
Sunday—lJlb. beef, Jib. flour, lib. biscuits ; Monday—
ljlb. pork, Jib. peas ; Tuesday and Thursday—same as
Sunday; Wednesday and Friday—same as Monday:
Saturday—lJlb. beef, lib. biscuits. Sugar, 2oz. per day ;
tea, Joz. per day ; coffee, -joz. per day ; water, 3 quarts
per day (this is for making tea and coffee, soup or pudding,
drinking and washing). If butter or potatoes are given
half the meat is deducted. Most vessels give dry boiled
rice on Saturday—euphoniously known as “strike-me-
blind ’’—and Jib. of meat is stopped. Beef that has been
in pickle seven or eight years is not very uncommon.
Any Australian youngster with yearnings for the
sea will understand the life passed in the lapse of
luxury.
“ Veteran ” to The Bulletin on soldiers’
pay
Me the delusion that to fill the ranks of the British
army it is only necessary to offer more pay. The British
army is already the best-paid—(in fact, it is practically
the only one that is paid at all) in Europe. Despite all
the outcry about the pay of the commissioned rank, an
English lieutenant gets more than a Russian major, or a
French or German captain, and could live very comfort-
ably if it were not for the demands of “ society.” As for
the private soldier, prudential considerations have
nothing to do with his enlistment Either he is in trouble,
or out of work, or he wants to see the world. And, in any
case, no amount of pay will make some men submit even
to necessary, rational discipline. Quite one-third of the
soldiers enlisted in the British Army prove either stupid,
»insubordinate, or in some obscure way mentally or physi-
cally defective. Hundreds can’t, or won’t, shoot well
enough to hit a 6ft. x 4ft. target at 300yds., while others
can’t learn their drill, and spend their whole service as
recruits. You can’t punish men for stupidity, although
in most such cases it is certain that the men are only
“acting the goat” in order to be discharged. The
remedy is—lst, increase the pay to Is. a day clear ; and,
2nd, give the soldier a prospect by opening the highest
ranks to him.

“Is it your old trouble ivith the Bach and Kidneys?
It used to be considered that only urinary and bladder
troubles were to be traced to the kidneys, but now modern science
proves that nearly all diseases have their beginning in the
disorder of these most important organs.
The kidneys filter and purify the blood, : —that is their work.
So when your kidneys are weak or out of order, you can
understand how quickly your entire body is affected, and how
every organ seems to fail to do its duty.
If you are sick or “ feel badly,” begin taking Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills, because as soon as your kidneys are well they
will help all the other organs to health. A trial will convince
anyone.
If the following had occurred somewhere else
than in Sydney, you might believe it, but you
could not prove it, and that makes all the differ-
ence. The reader must have seen many such
statements before, but he took very little notice of
them, because he knew neither the persons men-
tioned nor the towns they were supposed to live in.
But here is the statement of a Sydney man,
spoken for the good of Sydney people, and that
is why we say you can prove this. It has the right
sort of ring to it.
Mr. W. Hibberd, St. Paul’s street, Randwick,
this city, says:—“ For years I have been a sufferer
from backache. In my work I have to stoop a
good deal, and prolonged exertion of this kind
would double me up with pain. After stooping I
would be scarcely able to stand up straight again,
but would have to unbend gradually, and get the
kinks out of my back. I used many sorts of
medicines for this ache, but with poor success.
The doctors I saw were also puzzled, I think.
I procured a box of Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills at Newman’s Pharmacy, in King-street, and
I am very pleased to tell you that these pills gave
me almost immediate relief. They went right to
the root of my trouble. The pains diminished
after further treatment, and at length totally dis-
appeared. I shall always recommend these pills
for backache trouble.”
Twelve months later Mrs. Hibberd states: —
“It is more than a year ago since my husband
used Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills for backache.
I am now enabled to say that the pills wrought a
permanent cure in his case. The pains never
returned since. I can speak with conviction of
the marvellous way they acted in his case. He is
very enthusiastic about the pills, and is always
recommending them for such ailments as the pills
are meant for. He had been bad over ten years,
so you can see it was no common remedy that
could cure him in the way Doan’s Backache Kid-
ney Pills did.”
This is only one case, but there are hundreds
more like it, both here and in Victoria, in Queens-
land, South Australia, and West Australia, in
fact, in every town in Australia. The papers in
every town have a fellow-townsman’s statement
every week, very much like Mr. Hibberd’s given
above, telling what this - medicine has done for
them.
Now, there must be some good reason for the
unparalled success of Doan’s Bachache Kidney
Pills in Sydney and N.S.W. generally. What is
that reason ?
In the first place, they are a medicine of great
merit, for Sydney people are praising them every
week in these columns.
In the second place, their whole action is on the
most important organs of the whole body—the
kidneys. In other words, they do one thing, and
do it well.
But why are the kidneys the most important
organs of the body ? Because they filter the blood.
Every drop of the blood goes through the kidneys
many times a day, and it is their duty to filter
out the surplus water and the impurities the blood
has gathered up during its circuit of the body.
These impurities are the cause of your headache,
restlessness, sleeplessness, backache, general de-
bility, rheumatism, gout, urinary disturbances,
pains in the joints and limbs, irregular heart,
stomach troubles, and a thousand and one symp-
toms. Help the kidneys to rid the body of these
impurities, and you can be well. You can be well
in no other way.

The Spirit of the Violin.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A nightingale at dawning
Sat list’ning to a lark
His song of triumph singing
Above the meadows dark.
She caught from that high music
Its highest, keenest note ;
And in the haunted twilight
It filled her throbbing throat.
But, ah, ’twixt pride and passe >
Her heart in twain was torn ;
And, in the haunted twilight,
A strange sweet sprite was born. .
It fluttered in the twilight,
Far into the forest deep;
But all the trees were silent,
And all the birds asleep.
And then a Poet’s window
It brushed with pinions frail;
But he was of red lips dreaming,
Though his own lips were pale.
And in the morning early,
When it was cold and numb,
A man came by wha caught it,
And sealed it in a drum.
In many a procession,
At many a parade,
That drum was proudly leading
The Fife-and-Drum Brigade.
And, when the wild, sad spirit
Cried out within the drum,
They beat on the walls of its prison,
And beat till it was dumb.
Then on a day in summer,
With banners brave displayed,
Away to war went marching
The Fife-and-Drum Brigade.
A brave young drummer bore it—
The drum that held the sprite—
And high his heart was beating
As he went forth to fight.
The bold Brigade charged foremost,
And won the victory ;
The drum and the young drummer
Were pierced by bullets three.
Then from the field of battle
There rose two spirits free,
And they became one spirit
All made of melody.
The ancient violin-maker
Awoke at dead of night,
He heard a spirit crying
All in the wan moonlight. . .
What violin of magic
The wondrous story told ?
Was it a sweet Amati —
Or rich Cremona old ?
Its tones by times were tender—
The voice of the nightingale
Spilling her soul of passion
Upon the moonlight pale.
And now, like piercing starlight
Thrilling the silent dark,
There rose, and rang in Heaven,
The voice of the soaring lark.
And then like death in Heaven
Again and yet again
Swept burning through the music
The voice of human pain.
That violin of magic,
And sorrow and delight,
I heard a lady playing
On an enchanted night.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

“ Kirk ” to The Bulletin :
j “My dear fellow,” said my doctor to me, “ you have no
idea what we have to put up with. If I call to see a
patient frequently, I am trying to run up a bill ; if I
don’t, it is shameful neglect. If I manage to get to
church and am called out, I hear afterwards —‘ Working
the Bob Sawyer dodge on Sunday, eh, doctor?’ If lam
so busy that I cannot go, I am sure to be asked ‘ How is
it that you doctors are all atheists ? ’ If my wife calls on
w people, ‘it is because she is trying to get patients for
me ’; but if she doesn’t, it is because she is ‘ too stuck-
up.’ If I cure a patient quickly—get credit, you say ? Oh,
dear, no; the patient ‘ wasn’t half as bad as the
doctor tried to make out; why, he was quite well
in a week ’; but, on the other hand, should the case de-
velop serious complications, ‘ Ah, the doctor never under-
stood the malady; in fact, he was worse when
he was taking the medicine a week than when
we called him in.’ If I suggest a consultation, ‘it is only
because I don’t know what’s the matter ; ’ if I pooh-pooh
the idea as unnecessary, I am ‘ afraid of showing my igno-
rance.’ lam expected, so to speak, to cast a horoscope
on a baby’s life and tell its mother what its ailments will
be. If I can’t do that, I ‘ cannot possibly know much.’
lam alsb expected to foresee all the ills that flesh is heir
to, six months before they come. I once lost a patient
whom I treated for influenza because I did not foretell an
attack of rheumatism which came on three months later.
In all cases, if they get worse, the fault lies in the medi-
cine ; if they get well, it is ‘ the goodness of Providence.'
If I send in my bill they say, ‘He is in a terrible hurry
for his money'; if I don’t, it is ‘so unbusinesslike.’
< But we get well paid,’ do you say ? My dear sir, if I re-
ceived payment for one-half I do, I should die from
Shook."
Our “Bushman.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He turned-up while Watson and I were hard at/
it pulling some sheep out of a Government dam)
they had got bogged in.
Our two dogs were having a lively time trying)
to keep the rest of the mob—a large one—to-'
gether; but he didn’t offer to lend a hand. In-;
stead, he rolled off his nag, sat on the fence, and
gave us gratuitous advice, which Watson resented.
The nag—a fair specimen of the North Queens-
land “brumby”—found a soft spot, rolled care-
fully over on the swag and saddle, and flattened
out the billy in a frantic endeavor to get right
over ; but even this, beyond inspiring a wish that
the quadruped might enjoy eternal perdition, did
not seem to evoke much interest or enthusiasm in
the owner.
“ Good game that! ” he volunteered, watching
Watson and myself struggling with a particularly
heavy crossbred ewe. “ Got a bit o’ weed, any-
body ? ”
I told him that he would find a plug in my coat
pocket. He found it. Watson told him to go and
work for his tobacoo.
“Work!” he said scornfully, “Work!
ibout the only game I’ve never tackled yet, work
is !”
Watson advised him to be careful how he went
about starting it: it might tell upon his constitu-
tion. He said he would.
“Where are you bound for now?” I inquired;
casually.
“Down t’ Hay,” he replied wearily, “I’m
goin’t’ join these ’ere ‘Bushmens.’ I’m chock-full
o’ whalin’ up an’ down the river, year in, year out.
A cove wants a bit o’ change, now an’ agen,
don’t ’e ? ”
I said I supposed he did, and inquired if he
didn’t expect to work in South Africa. The mere
idea of this appeared to tickle him immensely.
“Work!” he ejaculated. “Work be d—d !
Don’t call wingin’ Boers graft, do y’ ? No more
graft about that than sittin’ on that ole ' cow’s ’
back knockin’ possums.” He pointed to the re-
cumbent form of the “ole cow.” “B good
’orse that,” he continued, reflectively. “ I give 30
bob fer ’im out o’ the pound at J’ril’drie, three
year ago. S’pose, takin’ ’em all round, ther’ ain’t
many ’orses goin’t’ S’th Africker like ’im.”
Watson said he didn’t suppose there were, and
inquired caustically what harm the Boer Repub-
lics had done to Australia that two such fine
specimens of her products should go forth to quell
them. Whereupon we heard his opinions of the
Boers, in so far as they concerned him in par-
ticular.
“It’s like this,” he said ; “ere’s me, I’m no
good ’ere, am I? ” Nobody answering to
the contrary, he proceeded: “It’s gettin’ like
this. Nowadays they’re shuttin’ up tight on the
rations up along the river; an’ if me an’ that
there can knock out our crown a day, an’
scranno, I reckon we’d be chumps not t’ take it
on. That’s my lot—an’ I’m no yob.”
Watson remarked that anyone could see that at
a glance.
“Wot’s the odds wot they’re fightin’ about?
S’far’s I can see or ’ear, Boers is no good, anyhow.
Might’s well be buzznackin’ about there as ’ere.
Nothin’ t’ do ’ere but shearin’, an’ that ’ard toil.
Ever bin shearin’ ? ”
We averred that we had ; he said we were
chumps. Anybody that worked for squatters was
a chump, in his opinion. Anybody that worked
at all, for the matter of that. He wouldn’t ; he’d
see ’em all to—somewhere —first. He knew a bit,
he said.
After another hour or so of “ ’ard toil ” we got
the last of the sheep clear, and went into camp.
He reckoned he’d camp with us for the night, and
have a drop of tea (our tea). Watson told him
that if he did, he’d have to stand one of the night-
watches with the sheep. He agreed, and got a bit
of fire together while we bunched the mob for the
night.
For the greater portion of the night he smoked
(our tobacco), and regaled us with Murrumbidgee
yarns, of which he had a great selection. Then
we turned-in, it being arranged that our friend
should relieve me at three o’clock, which he did.
At daylight I awoke. The gallant patriot had
journeyed on towards Fame and Glory. Inciden-
tally, so had my good saddle; ditto Watson’s
bridle.
It took us nearly two hours to get the mot
together again ; and that was the last we ever saw
of him.
One day Watson picked up a paper in a pub at
Bourke, which stated that the derider of work
was now one of a “ mob” (so Wats.on called them)
upon their way to South Africa. He was a full-
blown “ Bushman a “ Soldier of the Queen.”
We’re rabid pro-Boers now, both of us; and
Watson has a picture of De Wet gummed on thf
flap of his saddle.
